The Necessity of Rational Inference (Naẓar)
Maturidi defines naẓar and states its necessity and functions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers rational inference (naẓar) a necessary source of knowledge and how it is used to evaluate sense perception and reports.

Core Idea
Rational inference is indispensable for discerning truth in the unseen and conflicting reports.

This passage introduces naẓar as the third source of knowledge. Maturidi argues that reason is necessary for evaluating the other sources: it helps us know distant sensible objects, resolve conflicting reports, and distinguish miracles from magic. He grounds this necessity in Qur'anic verses that command reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow, then read the English translation. Notice the key terms: النظر (naẓar) and الاستدلال (istidlāl).
2
Identify the three functions of reason
The passage lists three specific tasks for which reason is necessary. Can you find them? They involve knowing distant objects, evaluating conflicting reports, and distinguishing miracles from magic.
These are concrete examples of why naẓar is needed.
3
Notice the Qur'anic basis
Maturidi cites the Qur'an as evidence that God commands reasoning. This shows that naẓar is not just a philosophical tool but a religious obligation.
Look for the phrase 'دلّ الله في كثير من الآيات'.
4
Reflect on the necessity claim
The passage says we are 'compelled' to use naẓar. Why is it not optional? Think about how we could evaluate reports or miracles without reasoning.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عن النظر أو الاستدلال عند الماتريدي، فهو عامل مهم نضطر إليه خصوصا في تقييم مصدري المعرفة اللذين سبق ذكرهما. ويعنى بذلك أن العقل يمكننا استخدامه خصوصا في معرفة المحسوس الذي يبعد من الحواس أو يخفي عن الإدراك، وفيما يرد إلينا من الأخبار المتضاربة حقائقها، ثم معجزات الرسل وتمويهات السحرة والمشعوذين في التمييز بينها. وعلى هذا الأساس دلّ الله في كثير من الآيات القرآنية بالاستدلال والنظر، فأوضح بأن الأسلوب هذا هو الذي يوقف المرء على الحق ويبين له الطريق. ويُفهم من أسلوب الماتريدي الذي استخدمه هنا في الدفاع عن ضرورة الاستدلال والنظر بأنه قد ألمح فيه بالفرقة المحافظة وكذلك التيارات المعارضة للفلسفة؛ فيقول من هذه الزاوية بأن كل من ينكر النظر والاستدلال، فهو في الوقت نفسه يستخدم الأسلوب نفسه في إنكاره. وقد انتبه إلى ضرورة استخدام العقل سواء في العلم بالعالم المحسوس أو تدبير الحياة البشرية والتخلص من الأحداث المتضاربة فيها، وأن العقل في ذلك شبيه بحاستي البصر والسمع في اشتباه الألوان والأصوات إليهما. وقد صرح بأن الإنسان بجانب الميول المتعددة والأحاسيس المختلفة قد خُص بالعقل، وأن طبيعة البشر والعقل الذي يملكه لا يتطابقان في بعض الأحيان، وأنه في تلك الحالات يعطى للعقل سلطة الحكم على ملكة الطبع . وكذلك يدعى الماتريدي بأن العلوم المدركة بالحواس التي تعتبر أرقى مصادر المعرفة، فهي تتحقق عن طريق وظائف ذهنية، ويفيد بأن إدراكًا جديدا لن يتحقق إلا بعد الخواطر الحاصلة في الذهن قبل هذا الإدراك ؛ إذ ينبغي أن يكون العقل فعّالاً في النفوذ إلى تلك الخواطر وتعتمد سلبيته على كيفية فعاليته فيها. ويذهب الماتريدي بأن هؤلاء الذين سوّلوا لأنفسهم ترك استعمال العقل بالفكر بالأشياء فهم تحت ضغط شهواتهم النفسية وخواطر شيطانية
As for rational inference (naẓar) or reasoning (istidlāl) according to al-Māturīdī, it is an important factor to which we are compelled, especially in evaluating the two sources of knowledge previously mentioned. That means we can use the intellect especially in knowing the sensed object that is distant from the senses or hidden from perception, and in what reaches us from reports whose truths are conflicting, and then differentiating between the miracles of the messengers and the deceptions of magicians and sorcerers. On this basis, God has indicated in many Qur’anic verses the use of reasoning and inference, and clarified that this method is what makes one stop at the truth and shows him the way. It is understood from al-Māturīdī’s style used here in defending the necessity of reasoning and inference that he alluded thereby to the conservative faction as well as the currents opposed to philosophy; so from this angle he says that whoever denies reasoning and inference is at the same time using the same method in his denial. He has paid attention to the necessity of using the intellect both in knowledge of the sensible world and in managing human life and getting rid of the conflicting events in it, and that the intellect in this respect is similar to the two senses of sight and hearing in the confusion of colors and sounds to them. He has declared that man, alongside multiple inclinations and different sensations, has been distinguished by the intellect, and that human nature and the intellect he possesses do not correspond at times, and that in those cases the intellect is given the authority to judge over the faculty of nature. And likewise, al-Māturīdī claims that the sciences perceived by the senses, which are considered the highest sources of knowledge, are realized through mental functions, and he states that a new perception will not be achieved except after the thoughts (khawāṭir) occurring in the mind before this perception; for the intellect must be effective in penetrating those thoughts, and its passivity depends on the manner of its activity in them. Al-Māturīdī holds that those who allow themselves to abandon the use of the intellect in thinking about things are under the pressure of their soul’s desires and satanic thoughts.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage discusses the third source: naẓar (rational inference). Maturidi presents it as necessary for evaluating the other two sources: to assess sense perceptions that are distant or hidden, to resolve conflicting reports, and to distinguish true miracles from magic. He grounds this in Qur’anic injunctions to reason. He also responds to critics (conservatives and anti-philosophy currents) by noting that denying reasoning itself involves reasoning. He emphasizes the role of reason in managing life and resolving contradictions. He compares reason to sight and hearing, which may be confused. He highlights that reason can judge over natural disposition when they conflict. He asserts that even sensory knowledge involves mental processes, and that new perceptions require prior thoughts; the intellect must be active in engaging these thoughts. He concludes that those who abandon reason are driven by base desires and satanic whispers.

Key Terms
النظر
الاستدلال
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that rational inference is optional or only for philosophers.
Correction: Maturidi argues it is necessary for all, as it is used to evaluate sense perception and reports, and is commanded by God in the Qur'an.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a function of rational inference?
A) Knowing distant sensible objects
B) Resolving conflicting reports
C) Creating new laws from scratch
D) Distinguishing miracles from magic

Key Takeaway
Rational inference is a necessary, divinely-guided tool for evaluating the other sources of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter conflicting reports about a religious matter, do you rely solely on feelings or do you engage in rational inference as commanded by God? Have you ever avoided using your intellect because it felt hard?
?
The passage says naẓar is necessary for distinguishing miracles from magic. In your own life, how do you discern genuine divine signs from deceptive illusions that lead you away from truth?
?
God has indicated in many Qur’anic verses the use of reasoning. Are you actually using your reasoning when you read those verses, or do you just pass over them without reflecting?

Defending Reason Against Its Detractors
Maturidi refutes opponents and elaborates on reason's functions and dangers of abandoning it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of those who deny reasoning and his explanation of reason's role in relation to human nature and desires.

Core Idea
Denying reason is self-refuting; reason must govern over nature and desires.

Maturidi addresses critics who reject reasoning, arguing that their denial itself uses reasoning. He compares reason to the senses and asserts reason's authority over natural disposition. He also explains that sensory knowledge involves mental processes and that abandoning reason leads to being driven by base desires and satanic thoughts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the argument against deniers and the comparison to senses.
2
Find the self-refutation argument
Maturidi points out that those who deny reasoning are themselves using reasoning in their denial. Can you spot this point? How does it undermine their position?
This is a powerful rebuttal.
3
Understand the comparison with senses
Maturidi compares reason to sight and hearing, noting that these senses can be confused. What does this imply about reason?
It suggests reason is also needed to correct sensory errors.
4
Note the authority of reason over nature
The passage says reason can judge over human nature when they conflict. Why is this important for knowledge and action?
5
Consider the conclusion about desires
The passage ends by stating that those who abandon reason are under the pressure of desires and satanic thoughts. How does this reinforce the necessity of reason?
This connects reason to moral and spiritual well-being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عن النظر أو الاستدلال عند الماتريدي، فهو عامل مهم نضطر إليه خصوصا في تقييم مصدري المعرفة اللذين سبق ذكرهما. ويعنى بذلك أن العقل يمكننا استخدامه خصوصا في معرفة المحسوس الذي يبعد من الحواس أو يخفي عن الإدراك، وفيما يرد إلينا من الأخبار المتضاربة حقائقها، ثم معجزات الرسل وتمويهات السحرة والمشعوذين في التمييز بينها. وعلى هذا الأساس دلّ الله في كثير من الآيات القرآنية بالاستدلال والنظر، فأوضح بأن الأسلوب هذا هو الذي يوقف المرء على الحق ويبين له الطريق. ويُفهم من أسلوب الماتريدي الذي استخدمه هنا في الدفاع عن ضرورة الاستدلال والنظر بأنه قد ألمح فيه بالفرقة المحافظة وكذلك التيارات المعارضة للفلسفة؛ فيقول من هذه الزاوية بأن كل من ينكر النظر والاستدلال، فهو في الوقت نفسه يستخدم الأسلوب نفسه في إنكاره. وقد انتبه إلى ضرورة استخدام العقل سواء في العلم بالعالم المحسوس أو تدبير الحياة البشرية والتخلص من الأحداث المتضاربة فيها، وأن العقل في ذلك شبيه بحاستي البصر والسمع في اشتباه الألوان والأصوات إليهما. وقد صرح بأن الإنسان بجانب الميول المتعددة والأحاسيس المختلفة قد خُص بالعقل، وأن طبيعة البشر والعقل الذي يملكه لا يتطابقان في بعض الأحيان، وأنه في تلك الحالات يعطى للعقل سلطة الحكم على ملكة الطبع . وكذلك يدعى الماتريدي بأن العلوم المدركة بالحواس التي تعتبر أرقى مصادر المعرفة، فهي تتحقق عن طريق وظائف ذهنية، ويفيد بأن إدراكًا جديدا لن يتحقق إلا بعد الخواطر الحاصلة في الذهن قبل هذا الإدراك ؛ إذ ينبغي أن يكون العقل فعّالاً في النفوذ إلى تلك الخواطر وتعتمد سلبيته على كيفية فعاليته فيها. ويذهب الماتريدي بأن هؤلاء الذين سوّلوا لأنفسهم ترك استعمال العقل بالفكر بالأشياء فهم تحت ضغط شهواتهم النفسية وخواطر شيطانية
As for rational inference (naẓar) or reasoning (istidlāl) according to al-Māturīdī, it is an important factor to which we are compelled, especially in evaluating the two sources of knowledge previously mentioned. That means we can use the intellect especially in knowing the sensed object that is distant from the senses or hidden from perception, and in what reaches us from reports whose truths are conflicting, and then differentiating between the miracles of the messengers and the deceptions of magicians and sorcerers. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying reasoning is a valid stance that avoids using reason.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the denial itself uses reasoning, so it is self-contradictory.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about those who abandon the use of the intellect?
A) They become philosophers
B) They are under pressure of soul's desires and satanic thoughts
C) They achieve higher spiritual knowledge
D) They attain consensus

Key Takeaway
Reason is self-validating and essential for both knowledge and moral integrity; abandoning it leads to error and temptation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your intellect tells you something is wrong, but your desires pull you toward it, which do you usually follow? Can you think of a specific instance this week where you chose desire over reason? What would it cost you to start giving intellect authority over your nature in that area starting today?
?
You just read that those who abandon thinking are under the pressure of their soul's desires and satanic thoughts. Be honest: when you make decisions that you know are not rational, do you ever consider that you might be influenced by satanic whispers? Will you, from this moment, begin to examine the source of your impulses before acting on them?
?
Maturidi points out that if you deny reasoning, you are already using reason to deny it. Have you ever dismissed a logical argument for a belief you hold dear, assuming you were above needing proof? What would change if you admitted that your rejection itself is a form of reasoning? Are you willing to follow reason wherever it leads, even if it challenges your comfort?

The World is Created for Lasting: A Rational Proof
Maturidi gives a rational proof from wisdom to show the world is for lasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational proof that the world was created for lasting, not annihilation, based on the principle of wisdom.

Core Idea
Reason demands wisdom; a world created in vain contradicts reason itself.

Maturidi presents a concise argument from reason (al-‘aql) to establish that the world has a lasting purpose. He argues that since reason finds acting without wisdom repugnant, and the world itself contains reason, it cannot have been created in vain. This leads to the conclusion that creation is for lasting, not annihilation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow become clear.
Notice how Maturidi starts with 'As for reason' – he is contrasting with other proofs.
2
Identify the key premise
The first part states that the world being only for annihilation is not wisdom, and that reason finds departure from wisdom repugnant.
This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Trace the logic
Since the world contains reason, it cannot be founded without wisdom or be vain. Reason itself would contradict that.
This is a self-refutation argument.
4
Understand the conclusion
Therefore, the world must be for lasting, not annihilation. Wisdom demands purpose and continuity.
This sets the stage for further arguments about prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العقل فهو أن كون هذا العالم للفناء خاصة ليس بحكمة، وخروج كل ذي عقل بفعله عن طريق الحكمة قبيح عنه؛ فلا يحتمل أن يكون العالم الذي العقل منه جزء مؤسستا على غير الحكمة أو مجعولاً عبثاً. وإذا ثبت ذلك دلّ أن إنشاء العالم للبقاء لا للفناء.
As for reason, it is that the existence of this world specifically for annihilation is not wisdom, and the departure of every rational being from the path of wisdom is repugnant to it; so it is not conceivable that the world, of which reason is a part, be founded on other than wisdom or made in vanity. And if that is established, it indicates that the creation of the world is for lasting, not for annihilation.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī) based on the concept of wisdom (ḥikma). Maturidi begins by stating that reason judges that acting without wisdom is repugnant (qabīḥ). Since the world includes reason as a part, it cannot be created without wisdom—that would be a contradiction. A creation without purpose (ʿabath/vanity) is inconsistent with wisdom. Therefore, the world must have a wise purpose, and that purpose is lasting (baqāʾ), not annihilation (fanāʾ). This proof establishes a foundational premise for further arguments about prophethood.

Key Terms
العقل
الحكمة
الفناء
البقاء
عبث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is purely philosophical and not rooted in Islamic theology.
Correction: Maturidi uses innate reason (fitra) to affirm a theological truth about creation's purpose, showing that reason and revelation align. The argument is a form of kalām that grounds belief in rationality.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it not possible that the world was created for annihilation?
A) Because reason finds it repugnant to act without wisdom.
B) Because the world is too complex to be destroyed.
C) Because prophets have told us the world will last.
D) Because humans desire immortality.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's rational proof establishes that the world is created for lasting, because wisdom requires purpose, and the world contains reason itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If reason itself demands wisdom, how do you respond when you see your own life lacking purpose? Do you truly believe your existence is for lasting, or do you live as if this world is all there is?
?
The argument says the world cannot be founded in vanity. Where in your daily choices do you act as if your actions have no eternal consequence? What would change if you fully internalized that every moment is for lasting?
?
Maturidi uses reason to prove the world is for lasting. But do you ever let forgetfulness of that truth make you indifferent to the purpose of your own life? What is one step you can take today to realign yourself with the reality that you were created for baqā', not fanā'?

Rational Judgments

Sensory Perception as Necessary Knowledge
Defines sensory perception and refutes its denier through dialectic and coercion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sensory perception (al-ʿiyān) is considered necessary knowledge and how Maturidi refutes its denial through dialectic and, if needed, physical coercion.

Core Idea
Denial of sensory perception is not ignorance but obstinate stubbornness.

Maturidi here establishes a foundational principle: sensory perception is a source of necessary knowledge that even animals possess. Those who deny it are not sincerely ignorant but willfully obstinate. The passage then outlines a two-pronged response—first a clever dialectical question, and as a last resort, physical pain to break their stubbornness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text aloud if possible, then follow with the English translation. Get a feel for Maturidi's direct and forceful style.
Notice how he begins with a definition before moving to the refutation.
2
Identify the definition of al-ʿiyān
Locate the sentence that defines sensory perception. Maturidi says it is what falls under the senses and that it is the foundation for knowledge that has no opposite of ignorance.
This sets up why denial is so problematic.
3
Understand the argument from animal nature
Maturidi argues that even beasts know what benefits or harms them through sensory perception. The denier of such knowledge is thus acting against his own nature.
This shows the universal and necessary character of sensory knowledge.
4
Examine the dialectical trick
The passage presents a clever question: 'Do you know that you are denying?' If the denier says no, his denial is invalid; if yes, he affirms the very knowledge he denies.
This is a classic logical refutation designed to expose self-contradiction.
5
Consider the role of physical pain
Maturidi mentions that as a last resort, the denier may be subjected to physical pain because his denial is obstinate, not based on genuine doubt. This is meant to break his stubbornness.
This is an extreme measure and not the primary response—it comes after dialectic fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالعيان ما يقع عليه الحواس، وهو الأصل الذي لديه العلم الذي لا ضدّ له من الجهل. فمن قال بضده من الجهل فهو الذي يُسمَّي مُنكِرَه كل سامع مكابِرًا؛ تأبى طبيعة البهائم أن يكون ذلك رتبتها؛ إذ كل منها يعلم ما به بقاؤها وفناؤها وما يتلذذ به ويتألّم، وصاحب هذا يُنكر ذلك. وأجْمِعَ أن لا يُناظر مع مَنْ كان ذلك قوله، إذ لا يُثبت إنكاره ولا حضوره بنفسه؛ والمناظرة في مائية الشيء أو هستيته، وهو لهما وللدفع جميعا دافع. لكنه يُمازح، فيقال له: «تعلم بأنك تنفي؟». فإن قال: «لا»، بطل نفيه، وإن قال: «نعم»، أثبت نفيه، فيصير بما يدفع دافعا لدفعه. ويُؤلَم بالألم الشديد من قطع الجوارح ليدع تعنّته، إذ نحن نعلم أنه يعلم العيان إذ هو علم الضرورة، ولكنه يقوله متعنتا، وحق مثله ما ذكرت ليجزع ويضجر، فيُقابل بتعنّت مثله فينهتك لديه ستره. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Sensory perception (al-ʿiyān) is what falls under the senses, and it is the foundation that possesses knowledge which has no opposite of ignorance. Whoever says its opposite of ignorance is one whom every hearer calls an obstinate denier (mukābiran); the nature of beasts refuses that this be their rank; for each of them knows what pertains to its survival and destruction and what it delights in and is pained by, yet the possessor of this [view] denies that. It is agreed that one should not debate with one whose position is that, for he neither affirms his denial nor his own presence by himself; and debate is about the quiddity (māhiyya) of a thing or its hecceity (hastiyya), and he repels both and even repels repulsion. But he is jested with, and it is said to him: 'Do you know that you are denying?' If he says 'No,' his denial is invalidated; if he says 'Yes,' he affirms his denial, and thus by repelling he becomes a repeller of his repulsion. And he is pained with severe pain by cutting limbs to abandon his obstinacy, for we know that he knows sensory perception (al-ʿiyān) because it is necessary knowledge (ʿilm al-ḍarūra), but he says it obstinately. The proper treatment of such a one is what I mentioned so that he may be distressed and annoyed, and he is met with similar obstinacy so that his veil is torn before him. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage defines sensory perception (al-ʿiyān) as the foundation of knowledge that is immune to ignorance, and it provides a proof of its necessity. The author argues that even animals possess this basic knowledge, so denying it is an act of obstinacy (mukābara). The proper response to such a denier is not serious debate, but a dialectical trick: ask if he knows he is denying. If he says no, his denial collapses; if yes, he affirms the very knowledge he denies. As a last resort, physical pain can be used because his denial is wilful, not based on genuine doubt. The ultimate purpose is to expose his stubbornness.

Key Terms
العيان (al-ʿiyān)
المكابرة (al-mukābara)
التعنت (al-taʿannut)
العلم الضروري (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Maturidi advocates physical coercion as the primary method to refute deniers.
Correction: The passage shows that physical pain is a last resort, applied only after dialectical questioning fails. The primary response is logical argument: the question 'Do you know that you are denying?' forces the denier into self-contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the first step in refuting a denier of sensory perception?
A) Physical punishment
B) Dialectical questioning: 'Do you know that you are denying?'
C) Ignoring him
D) Citing animal behaviour

Key Takeaway
Sensory perception is undeniable necessary knowledge; its denial is obstinate and countered first by dialectical reasoning, and only as a last resort by physical coercion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage says even beasts know what preserves and destroys them. What 'necessary knowledge' about your spiritual well-being are you ignoring because obedience would be uncomfortable?
?
If you were asked, 'Do you know that you are denying?' about a truth in your life, would you answer yes or no? What would your answer reveal about your sincerity before Allah?

The Dialectical Refutation of Deniers of Reports and Perception
Maturidi refutes deniers of reports and perception through self-refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument that denial of reports or perception is self-defeating, and how a simple question reveals the denier's inconsistency.

Core Idea
A denier's reply itself relies on the very thing he denies.

In this passage, Maturidi presents a clever dialectical strategy to refute anyone who denies the validity of reports (khabar) or direct perception ('iyan). Instead of a complex proof, he uses a simple question that forces the denier to rely on the very source he denies, thus exposing his self-contradiction. This playful yet rigorous argument underscores the foundational role of these sources in all knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of speech and listening
Read the opening statement: the two senses (hearing and sight) cannot perceive good and evil without speech and listening. This sets up the necessity of report.
This is the premise for the argument.
2
Identify the refutation of the denier of report
Find where Maturidi says 'What do you say?' and trace the two outcomes: if he replies, he accepts your report; if he doesn't, he abandons discourse.
The denier cannot consistently deny report while using it.
3
Identify the refutation of the denier of direct perception
Notice the parallel argument for perception. Asking 'What do you say?' also forces reliance on hearing and sight.
The same logic applies: his response uses perception.
4
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider why this jesting dialectic is effective: it forces the denier into a performative contradiction. Even silence is a failure to engage.
Maturidi shows that denial is not rationally sustainable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
{قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : } ثم لا يُوصل بهما إلى إدراك المحاسن والمساوي التي يعمل العقول على الإحاطة بها إلا باستعمال الألسن بالتكلم بها وإدناء السمع إليها. وحق مناظرة منكر هذا - وإن كانت مناظرته سفها - أن يُمازح أيضاً ، فتقول له عند إنكاره الخبر : ما تقول؟ فإن عاد إليه فاعلم أنه قَبِلَ خبرك حيث عاد إلى قوله وهو استعادتك الخبر، وإن لم يعد إليه كُفيت شره وحمدت الله وضحكت منه . ومثله لمن ينكر العيان، تقول له: «ما تقول؟» فإن عاد إليه ظهر لك أنه يعلمه، ولكنه يتعنّت، وإن لم يعد إليه كفيت شره وشكرت الله تعالى على ما ألهمك؛ أو تَضْرِبُه وتُؤلمه ، فإنه لا يقدر أن يَضجَرَ أو يقابلك بالعتاب، لما لا يحتمل ذلك إلا بتسمية فعلك ، وذلك يُعرف بالخبر، وقد أنكره. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then they (the two senses) do not allow attaining knowledge of good and evil which minds encompass except by using tongues to speak them and bringing hearing close to them. The proper way to debate a denier of this — even if his debate is foolish — is to jest with him as well, so you say to him when he denies report (al-khabar): 'What do you say?' If he returns to it, know that he has accepted your report because he returned to his saying, which is your repetition of the report. If he does not return to it, you are spared his evil, you praise God, and you laugh at him. Similarly, for one who denies direct perception (al-`iyān), you say to him: 'What do you say?' If he returns to it, it becomes clear to you that he knows it but is being obstinate. If he does not return to it, you are spared his evil and you thank God Almighty for what He inspired you; or you strike him and cause him pain, for he cannot complain or confront you with blame, because that cannot happen except by naming your action, and that is known by report (khabar), which he has denied. There is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that the two senses (hearing and sight) cannot attain knowledge of good and evil without the use of speech and listening to reports. Then it provides a dialectical refutation of someone who denies the validity of report (khabar) or direct perception (al-`iyān). The refutation is playful but logical: ask the denier 'What do you say?' If he responds, he has implicitly accepted the validity of your report (since he relies on hearing your words) or his own perception. Thus his denial is self-refuting. If he refuses to respond, he abandons discourse and you are relieved of his evil. The same method works for denying direct perception: you ask 'What do you say?' and his reply relies on perception. The passage ends with a practical option of physically harming him (in a hypothetical, not real, sense) to force him to rely on report, exposing his inconsistency. The key is that denial of these foundational sources is impossible to maintain consistently.

Key Terms
خبر (khabar)
عيان (al-`iyān)
ضرورة العقل (necessity of reason)
ممازحة (jesting dialectic)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation is merely humorous and not a serious argument.
Correction: The jesting dialectic is a logical demonstration of self-refutation, showing that the denial cannot be consistently maintained.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the proper response to someone who denies the validity of report (khabar)?
A) Ignore him
B) Engage in serious debate
C) Playfully ask him 'what do you say?' to reveal self-contradiction
D) Accuse him of heresy

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's playful question reveals that denying foundational sources like report and perception is inherently self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you found yourself denying something that you actually depend on in your own reasoning? How does Maturidi's question 'What do you say?' expose that inconsistency in your own life? Will you examine your beliefs for such hidden contradictions today?

?
This passage shows that even the act of complaining about a painful strike relies on report. Have you ever complained about something while denying the very means of knowledge that complaint requires? What would it mean for you to humbly accept the tools of knowledge that God has given you?

Necessity of Reasoning in Sensory and Transmitted Knowledge
Maturidi gives the first reason for obligatory reasoning: necessity in sensory and transmitted knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first argument for why reasoning is obligatory, rooted in our need for certainty in sensory and transmitted knowledge and in distinguishing divine miracles from sorcery.

Core Idea
Reasoning is not optional—it is forced upon us by the very nature of knowledge.

Maturidi begins his proof for the necessity of reasoning by showing that even our most basic knowledge—what we see and hear—requires rational scrutiny. He argues that without reasoning, we cannot be certain of distant or subtle perceptions, nor can we trust transmitted reports that may contain error. Most strikingly, he points out that we need reasoning to distinguish the miracles of prophets from the tricks of sorcerers. This passage lays the foundation for why kalam reasoning is not a luxury but a religious duty.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: Maturidi first states the principle, then gives the first aspect, then lists three areas where reasoning is needed.
Pay attention to the word 'اضطرار' (compulsion/necessity) — it sets the tone.
2
Identify the three domains of reasoning
Find in the passage the three specific domains where reasoning is necessary: sensory perception of distant/subtle things, transmitted reports that may contain error, and distinguishing miracles from sorcery.
List them in your own words.
3
Reflect on why sensory and transmitted knowledge need reasoning
Consider: Why isn't direct sense perception enough? Why can't we just rely on reports? Maturidi shows that both are fallible without rational scrutiny.
4
Examine the miracle vs. sorcery distinction
Think about how reasoning helps us tell genuine miracles from deceptive sorcery. Maturidi says we must contemplate the miracles themselves and the state of the one performing them.
This connects to the broader proof of prophethood.
5
Connect to the necessity of reasoning
Maturidi's first proof shows that reasoning is not an abstract exercise but a practical necessity for reliable knowledge in everyday life and religious discernment.
This establishes the obligation of nazar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل في لزوم القول بعلم النظر وجوه. أحدها الاضطرار إليه في علم الحس والخبر، وذلك فيما يبعد من الحواس أو يلطف، وفيما يرد من الخبر أنه في نوع ما يحتمل الغلط [عظ] أو لا، ثم آيات الرسل وتمويهات السحرة وغيرهم في التمييز بينها، وفي تعرف الآيات بما يتأمل فيها [من] قوى البشر وأحوال الآتي بها، ليظهر الحق بنوره والباطل بظلمته.
Then the principle concerning the necessity of holding knowledge of reasoning has several aspects. One of them is the necessity of it in sensory knowledge and transmitted reports, and that is regarding what is distant from the senses or subtle, and regarding what comes from transmitted reports that it is in some type that admits error or not, then the miracles of messengers and the sorceries of sorcerers and others in distinguishing between them, and in recognizing the miracles by what is contemplated in them of human powers and the states of the one who brings them, so that the truth appears by its light and falsehood by its darkness.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (wajh) for the necessity of reasoning (nazar). Maturidi begins by stating that the foundation for holding knowledge of reasoning has multiple aspects. The first is the compulsion (idtirar) to use reasoning in two domains: sensory knowledge (ilm al-hiss) and transmitted reports (khabar). In sensory knowledge, reason is needed when objects are distant or subtle; in transmitted reports, reason is needed because reports may contain error—reasoning helps weigh probabilities. Moreover, reasoning is essential for distinguishing the miracles (ayat) of messengers from the sorcery (tamwihaat) of sorcerers, by examining the miracles themselves (their nature and the state of the one performing them) so that truth shines forth clearly and falsehood appears as darkness. The author argues that without reasoning, one cannot reliably differentiate genuine divine signs from deceptive imitations.

Key Terms
نظر
اضطرار
علم الحس
خبر
آيات الرسل
تمويهات السحرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think reasoning is only for abstract or theological matters, not for everyday knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi shows that reasoning is needed even for basic sensory and historical knowledge, and for distinguishing divine signs from trickery.

Checkpoint

A) To prove the existence of God.
B) The compulsion to use it in sensory and transmitted knowledge and to distinguish miracles from sorcery.
C) To understand the Quran.
D) To refute heretics.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's first proof for the obligation of reasoning shows that we need rational scrutiny for reliable knowledge in sensory perception, transmitted reports, and distinguishing divine miracles from deception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a transmitted claim you accept without question—whether from a scholar, a news source, or a friend. Are you willing to apply Maturidi's principle and rationally scrutinize it this week? What would that examination look like?

The passage says truth appears by its light and falsehood by its darkness. What area of your life are you most afraid to expose to that light? Will you commit to one act of honest reasoning about it today?
?
You rely on sensory knowledge every moment—but how often do you pause to consider that even your senses can deceive you? What subtle or distant truth have you been ignoring because it didn't strike your senses directly? Will you actively seek it out through reasoning?

Second Proof: Divine Commands to Observe Creation
Maturidi cites Quranic verses commanding observation and reflection as proof for obligatory reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Allah's commands in the Quran to observe creation serve as a proof for the obligation of reasoning (nazar).

Core Idea
Allah himself commands us to reason; it is not a human invention.

Maturidi now turns from the first proof (human endowments) to a second, scripturally grounded proof: that Allah has commanded humanity to reflect upon creation. The Quran itself, as a miraculous proof, along with specific verses calling for observation, establishes that reasoning is not merely permitted but obligatory. This lesson examines those verses and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of 'دل الله' (Allah indicated) as the starting point.
2
Identify the two layers of proof
The passage first mentions 'الأدلة المعجزة' (miraculous proofs) like the Quran's inimitability, then lists specific Quranic verses that command reflection.
These two layers work together: the Quran is itself a sign, and it also commands us to look at other signs.
3
List the Quranic verses cited
Find each verse reference in the passage and note what it commands: observation of horizons, camels, creation, and oneself.
These verses are from Surah Fussilat, al-Ghashiya, al-Baqara, and al-Dhariyat.
4
Connect to the obligation of nazar
Ask yourself: why do these commands make reasoning obligatory? The commentary explains that they command 'tafakkur' (thinking) and 'tadabbur' (deliberation) and promise guidance to truth.
Contrast this with the idea that reasoning is merely optional or cultural.
5
Reflect on the closing phrase
The passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' (And there is no power except through Allah). Why might Maturidi include this?
It reminds us that even the ability to reason is a gift from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى ذلك دل الله بالذي ثبت بالأدلة المعجزة أنه منه، من نحو القرآن الذي عجز الإنس والجن أن يأتوا بمثله. مع الأمر به بقوله: سنريهم آياتنا في الآفاق إلى آخر السورة، وقوله: أفلا ينظرون إلى الإبل - الآية، وقوله: إن في خلق السموات والأرض - الآية، وقوله: وفي أنفسكم أفلا تبصرون، وغير ذلك مما رغب في النظر وألزم الاعتبار وأمر بالتفكر والتدبر، وأخبر أن ذلك يوقفهم على الحق ويبين لهم الطريق. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And upon that, Allah indicated by what has been established through miraculous proofs that it is from Him, such as the Quran which humans and jinn are incapable of producing its like, along with the command by His saying: 'We will show them Our signs in the horizons' to the end of the surah, and His saying: 'Do they not look at the camels' – the verse, and His saying: 'Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth' – the verse, and His saying: 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' and other verses that encourage reflection (nazar) and necessitate consideration and command contemplation and deliberation, and inform that that will make them stop at the truth and show them the way. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the necessity of reasoning. Maturidi argues that Allah Himself has indicated this necessity through miraculous proofs (adilla mu'jiza) that are clearly from Him, like the Quran—which humans and jinn cannot imitate. Moreover, Allah explicitly commands reflection in several verses: 'We will show them Our signs in the horizons' (Surah Fussilat, to the end), 'Do they not look at the camels?' (Surah al-Ghashiya), 'Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth' (Surah al-Baqara), and 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' (Surah al-Dhariyat). These verses, Maturidi says, encourage nazar (reflection), necessitate i'tibar (consideration), command tafakkur (thinking) and tadabbur (deliberation), and affirm that this reflection leads to the truth and clarifies the path. Thus, the divine command itself makes reasoning obligatory.

أفلا ينظرون
تفكر
تدبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reasoning is solely a human intellectual exercise without divine backing.
Correction: Maturidi grounds reasoning in direct divine commands from the Quran, making it a religious obligation and a response to Allah's guidance.

Checkpoint

C) Allah's commands in the Quran to observe and reflect.

Key Takeaway
Allah's commands to observe creation in the Quran establish that reasoning (nazar) is a divinely mandated duty, not merely a human initiative.

Third Proof: Self-Refutation of the Denier of Reasoning
Maturidi argues that the denial of reasoning self-destructs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying reasoning forces one to use reasoning, thus proving its necessity.

Core Idea
To deny reasoning, you must reason—this self-refutation proves its necessity.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument: anyone who tries to deny reasoning must rely on reasoning itself to make that denial. This creates an inescapable contradiction that affirms the very thing being denied. Let's examine how this proof works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This is a very compact argument—every word counts.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The passage has a premise (the denier has no proof except reasoning) and a conclusion (therefore reasoning is necessary). Find these two parts.
3
Understand the self-refutation
Notice that the denier's act of denying is itself an act of reasoning. Therefore his denial undermines itself. Ask yourself: why is this a strong proof?
This is a classic performative contradiction.
4
Reflect on the implication
If reasoning is necessary even to deny it, then it must be an undeniable tool. Consider how this argument applies to other forms of skepticism about reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما ليس لمن ينكر النظر على دفعه دليل سوى النظر، فدل ذلك على لزوم النظر بما به دفعه.
Along with what: there is for the one who denies reasoning no proof for its refutation except reasoning, so that indicates the necessity of reasoning by that which refutes it.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the necessity of reasoning (nazar). Maturidi states that anyone who denies nazar and attempts to refute it has no proof for that refutation except nazar itself. In other words, the very act of arguing against reasoning is itself an act of reasoning. Therefore, this self-refutation demonstrates that nazar is necessary: one must use nazar even to deny it. The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum—the denier is caught in a performative contradiction.

Key Terms
من ينكر النظر
دليل
لزوم النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think reasoning can be rejected without consequence.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the denial is self-defeating because the very act of denying requires reasoning.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi prove the necessity of reasoning through the denier's position?
A) The denier cannot understand anything.
B) The denier must use reasoning to deny reasoning.
C) The denier is ignorant.
D) The denier follows tradition.

Key Takeaway
Denying reasoning is self-refuting because the denial itself is an act of reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a belief without thinking it through, only to realize later that your dismissal itself was an act of reasoning? What did that teach you about your approach to truth?
?
This argument shows that reasoning is inescapable—even in denial. Are you willing to follow your reasoning wherever it leads, even if it challenges your comfortable assumptions or cherished opinions? What would that cost you?
?
If the very act of rejecting reasoning proves its necessity, then what excuse do you have for not using your intellect to seek the truth about your Creator? How can you begin to engage your mind fully in matters of faith starting today?

The Necessity of Reasoning for Wisdom and Stewardship
Maturidi presents the fourth and fifth proofs for the necessity of reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that both recognizing wisdom in creation and fulfilling human responsibility require deliberate reasoning (nazar).

Core Idea
Creation is not in vain; reasoning unlocks its wisdom and our duty.

Maturidi presents two closely linked proofs for why reasoning is not optional. First, the very order of creation points to a purpose and a Maker that cannot be known without reflection. Second, humans are uniquely endowed with stewardship—but fulfilling that role requires the same reasoning. This passage establishes that nazar is the only path to both knowledge and responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of both proofs.
Notice the phrase 'لا بد من معرفة' underscores necessity.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage contains two distinct arguments: first about wisdom in creation (fourth proof), second about human stewardship (fifth proof). Find where each begins.
The first ends at 'إلا بالنظر', the second builds from 'على أن البشر'.
3
Analyze the fourth proof
Focus on why creation cannot be in vain and how it indicates the Originator. Ask: why does this require reasoning?
Key words: الحكمة, عبئًا, الدلالة.
4
Analyze the fifth proof
Examine the special endowment of humans: management, trial, seeking benefit, choosing good. Why is reasoning essential here?
Key terms: ملك تدبير, المحنة, العقول.
5
Synthesize the common thread
Both proofs conclude that nazar is the only way to attain knowledge. Reflect on the connection between purpose and responsibility.
This ties to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما لا بد من معرفة ما في الخلق من الحكمة، إذ لا يجوز فعل مثله عبئًا، وما فيه من الدلالة على من أنشأه أو على كونه بنفسه أو حدث أو قدم، وكل ذلك مما لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا بالنظر. على أن البشر خص بملك تدبير الخلائق والمحنة فيها وطلب الأصلح لهم في العقول واختيار المحاسن في ذلك واتقاء مضادة ذلك؛ ولا سبيل إلى معرفة ذلك إلا باستعمال العقول بالنظر في الأشياء
Along with what: it is necessary to know the wisdom in creation, for it is not permissible that an action like it be done in vain, and what is in it of indication of the one who originated it or of its being by itself or its being originated or eternal, and all of that there is no way to know except through reasoning. And upon that, humans are specially endowed with the faculty of managing the creatures and trial therein and seeking what is most beneficial for them in intellects and choosing the good in that and avoiding its opposite; and there is no way to know that except by using intellects through reasoning about things.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and fifth proofs for the necessity of reasoning. The fourth proof: one must know the wisdom (hikma) in creation, because it is not possible that such an ordered act be done in vain (abathan). The creation contains indications that lead to knowledge of its Originator, whether it exists by itself or is originated or eternal—all of which can only be known through nazar. The fifth proof: humans are uniquely endowed with the capacity to manage creatures and undergo trial, seeking what is most beneficial for them using intellects, choosing good and avoiding harm. This too can only be known by employing intellects through nazar about things. Thus, reasoning is indispensable for both recognizing divine wisdom and fulfilling human stewardship.

Key Terms

عبئًا
الدلالة
حدث
قدم
ملك تدبير
المحنة
العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think wisdom in creation is self-evident.
Correction: Maturidi says it requires reasoning to uncover; without nazar, one cannot properly understand the purpose or the Originator.

Checkpoint

A) To appreciate beauty.
B) Because creation cannot be in vain and indicates its origin, which requires reasoning to know.
C) To enjoy nature.
D) To prove one's intelligence.

Key Takeaway
Reasoning is essential for understanding the purpose in creation and for fulfilling the human role of stewardship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you actively use reasoning to uncover its wisdom, or do you take it for granted? What is one created thing you can commit to reflecting on deeply this week using nazar?
?
Maturidi says humans are endowed with the faculty to manage creatures and choose good. In your daily decisions, do you pause to use your intellect to choose what is most beneficial, or do you follow habit? Which decision today could you re-evaluate through reasoning?
?
If creation is not in vain, then your own existence has a purpose that reasoning can uncover. Have you ever used reasoned reflection to seek your personal purpose, or have you avoided it? What step can you take this week to intentionally use your intellect in seeking divine wisdom?

Reasoning as the Universal Recourse in Doubt
Maturidi concludes reasoning is the ultimate recourse, like senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that reasoning is the natural and necessary recourse for resolving doubts, analogous to the use of senses for perception.

Core Idea
Reasoning is to truth what sight is to color — the only route when things are unclear.

In this concluding argument, Maturidi sums up his case for the necessity of reasoning (nazar). He points out that when people face doubts or difficulties, they instinctively turn to reasoning and contemplation. This universal behavior indicates that reasoning is the tool for accessing realities. He draws a powerful analogy with the senses: just as we rely on sight when colors are confusing, we must rely on reasoning when truth is confusing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the flow and the closing phrase.
The phrase 'There is no power except through Allah' is a reminder of divine aid.
2
Identify the analogy
Find the analogy: reasoning is to truth as senses are to their objects. Note how Maturidi compares the recourse in doubt to using sight for color confusion.
3
Reflect on the proof
Ask yourself: Why does Maturidi think this analogy proves reasoning is necessary? What does it reveal about human nature?
The universality of the recourse shows it is inherent, not optional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن مفزع الكل عند النوائب واعتراض الشبه إلى النظر في ذلك والتأمل. فدل أنه يدل على الحقائق ويوصل به إليها، على نحو الفزع عند اشتباه اللون إلى البصر، والصوت إلى السمع، وكذا كل شيء إلى الحاسة التي بها دركها، فمثله النظر. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Upon that, the recourse of everyone in times of calamities and the occurrence of doubts is to reasoning about that and contemplation. So it indicates that it points to realities and leads to them, like the recourse when color is confused to sight, and sound to hearing, and likewise everything to the sense by which it is perceived, so similar to that is reasoning. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage provides the sixth proof and the overall conclusion of the arguments for the necessity of reasoning. Maturidi states that when people face calamities (nawa'ib) and doubts (shubah) arise, their ultimate recourse is to reasoning and contemplation. This shows that reasoning leads to realities (haqa'iq) and connects one to them. He draws an analogy: just as when a color is unclear one turns to sight, or when a sound is unclear one turns to hearing—each matter referred to its appropriate sense—so too, when truth is unclear, one turns to reasoning. Thus, reasoning is the faculty for grasping truth in matters that are not directly perceptible. The final phrase 'there is no power except through Allah' is a pious conclusion acknowledging divine assistance.

Key Terms
مفزع
النوائب
الشبه
الحقائق
البصر
السمع
الحاسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reasoning is optional or only for scholars.
Correction: Maturidi shows reasoning is the natural recourse for everyone in doubt, not merely an optional exercise for scholars.

Checkpoint

A) Reasoning is like a tool for building.
B) Reasoning is like sight for colors and hearing for sounds.
C) Reasoning is like a guide in the wilderness.
D) Reasoning is like a teacher for students.

Key Takeaway
Reasoning is the universal human recourse for resolving doubts, just as senses are for perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a doubt about your faith strikes you, what is your first instinct? Do you run to reasoning and contemplation, or do you suppress the doubt with emotion or tradition? If it is not reasoning, why not? Commit today to turn to thoughtful study when uncertainty arises.
?
The passage says reasoning points to realities — but are you willing to accept conclusions that contradict your desires or habits? Name one area where you have resisted rational evidence because it was uncomfortable. Will you let reasoning guide you even there?
?
If reasoning is as natural to the mind as sight is to the eye, why do you often neglect it in daily decisions about right and wrong? What would change if you treated every moral confusion as needing the same careful attention as a blurry color?

Why Nazar Is Needed to Resolve Conflicts Between Nature and Intellect
Maturidi argues that nazar is needed to discern good and evil and resolve conflicts between nature and intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi insists that nazar (reflection) is indispensable for moral discernment when human nature and intellect pull in opposite directions.

Core Idea
Nature and intellect often disagree; nazar is the tool to decide which one is right.

Maturidi here addresses a fundamental tension in human moral psychology: we are created with both a natural inclination (tabīʿa) and an intellect (ʿaql), and these two faculties frequently give conflicting signals about what is good or evil. He argues that without deliberate reflection (nazar), we cannot reliably determine which actions are truly praiseworthy or blameworthy. This passage establishes the necessity of intellectual effort in ethical judgment, grounding it in the very structure of human beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the rhythm of Maturidi's argument: he sets up the need for intellect, then introduces the conflict between nature and intellect.
The passage uses technical terms like محاسن, مساوي, عقل, طبيعة—pay attention to them.
2
Identify the two sources of moral knowledge
Maturidi mentions that even after sense experience and reports, the ultimate knowledge of good and evil is in the intellects. But he also says humans are constituted with both nature and intellect. Identify how these two sources interact.
3
Understand the conflict between nature and intellect
Find the sentence where Maturidi explicitly says that what intellect deems good may not be what nature desires, and what it deems ugly may not be what nature recoils from. Sometimes they agree, sometimes they oppose.
4
See the conclusion: nazar is necessary
Maturidi concludes that without nazar (reflection) on every matter, one cannot know which category an action belongs to. This is the core takeaway.
The final phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' reminds us that all success is from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن محاسن الأشياء ومساويها، وما قَبُحَ من الأفعال وما حَسُنَ منها فإنما نهاية العلم بعد وقوع الحواس عليها وورود الأخبار فيها - إذا أريد تقرير كل جهة من ذلك - في العقول ! والكشف عن وجوه ما لا سبيل إلى ذلك إلا بالتأمل والنظر فيها ؛ وعلى ذلك أمر المكاسب الضارة والنافعة . على أنّ البشر جبل على طبيعة وعقل، وما يُحسنه العقل غير الذي ترغب فيه الطبيعة، وما يُقبّحه غير الذي ينفر عنه الطَّبْعُ، أو يكون بينهما مخالفة مرة وموافقة ثانيا. فلا بد من النظر في كل أمر والتأمل لِيُعْلَم حقيقة أنه في أي فَنّ ونوع مما ذكرنا. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
That the goods and evils of things, and what actions are ugly and what are beautiful—the ultimate knowledge after the senses have encountered them and reports have come about them, if one wants to establish each aspect of that, is in the intellects! And the uncovering of aspects: there is no way to that except through deliberation and reflection (nazar) on them; and upon that is the matter of harmful and beneficial acquisitions. That humans are constituted with a nature and an intellect, and what the intellect considers good is not what nature desires, and what it considers ugly is not what nature recoils from, or there is between them sometimes opposition and sometimes agreement. So there is no avoiding reflection (nazar) on every matter and deliberation to know truly which category and type of what we mentioned it belongs to. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage explains why nazar is indispensable for practical and moral discernment. Maturidi states that knowledge of what is good and bad in actions, after sensory data and reports, is ultimately determined by the intellect—but this requires deliberation and nazar to uncover the various aspects. He emphasizes that humans are composed of both a lower nature (tabīʿa) and an intellect (ʿaql), and these often conflict: the intellect may deem good what nature dislikes, and vice versa. Moreover, sometimes they agree. Thus, to know the true nature of any action or acquisition, one must use nazar and careful consideration. The final clause again affirms that there is no power except from God, anchoring the argument in divine reliance.

Key Terms
محاسن (goods)
مساوي (evils)
عقل (intellect)
طبيعة (nature)
تأمل (deliberation)
نظر (nazar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that nature or instinct alone suffices to determine good and evil.
Correction: Maturidi shows that intellect and nazar are needed because nature and intellect often conflict.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say nazar is needed in discerning good and bad actions?
A) Because nature and intellect always agree
B) Because nature and intellect sometimes conflict
C) Because the senses are unreliable
D) Because revelation is unclear

Key Takeaway
Because human nature and intellect often disagree, deliberate reflection (nazar) is essential to correctly judge good and evil actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you recall a moment when your natural impulses urged you to do something that your intellect told you was wrong? Did you pause to deliberately reflect (nazar) before acting? Where might you have avoided that reflection, and what price did you pay?
?
In which area of your life—your habits, work, relationships—do you most often follow your nature without consulting your intellect? What would it cost you to bring nazar into that area starting today?
?
The passage ends with 'there is no power except with God.' Do you genuinely seek divine help when you try to resolve conflicts between your desires and your reason? Or do you rely solely on your own intellect? What would change if you humbly asked for guidance before every difficult decision?

The Necessity of Intellectual Reflection Beyond Senses and Nature
Maturidi explains the role of reason in discerning moral qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that intellectual reflection is necessary to discern true good and evil beyond sensory perception and natural inclination.

Core Idea
Intellect and nature can conflict; reflection is needed to know the reality of things.

Maturidi begins by stating that while senses and reports provide initial data, the ultimate knowledge of good and evil requires intellectual reflection. He then explains that humans are created with both an intellect and a nature that may pull in opposite directions. This passage establishes the epistemological foundation for using reason to evaluate moral and practical matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from senses/reports to intellect and then to the conflict between nature and intellect.
2
Identify the three sources of knowledge
Maturidi mentions senses (حواس), reports (أخبار), and intellect (عقل). He says the ultimate knowledge is in the intellects after senses and reports. Understand that all three are necessary but reflection elevates knowledge.
Reflection (تأمل ونظر) is the tool of the intellect.
3
Analyze the dual constitution of humans
He states humans are created upon nature (طبيعة) and intellect (عقل). What the intellect deems good or ugly may differ from what nature desires or recoils from. This conflict necessitates reflection to know the true category of an action.
Reflection resolves the conflict and leads to correct judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن محاسن الأشياء ومساويها، وما قبح من الأفعال وما حسن منها فإنما نهاية العلم بعد وقوع الحواس عليها وورود الأخبار فيها - إذا أريد تقرير كل جهة من ذلك - في العقول والكشف عن وجوه ما لا سبيل إلى ذلك إلا بالتأمل والنظر فيها؛ وعلى ذلك أمر المكاسب الضارة والنافعة. على أن البشر جبل على طبيعة وعقل، وما يحسنه العقل غير الذي ترغب فيه الطبيعة، وما يقبحه غير الذي ينفر عنه الطبع، أو يكون بينهما مخالفة مرة وموافقة ثانيا. فلا بد من النظر في كل أمر والتأمل ليعلم حقيقة أنه في أي فن ونوع مما ذكرنا. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
On that the good qualities of things and their bad qualities, and what is ugly from actions and what is beautiful from them—indeed, the ultimate knowledge after the senses fall upon them and reports come about them—if one intends to establish each aspect of that—is in the intellects and the uncovering of aspects; no way to that except through reflection and examination. And upon that is the matter of harmful and beneficial gains. Moreover, humans are created upon a nature and an intellect, and what the intellect considers good is other than what nature desires, and what it considers ugly is other than what nature recoils from, or there is between them sometimes conflict and sometimes agreement. So there is no avoiding reflection upon every matter and pondering to know its reality concerning which category and type of what we mentioned. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument for the necessity of intellectual reflection in discerning moral qualities and practical benefits. Maturidi begins by stating that the ultimate knowledge of the goodness and badness of things, both in actions and in worldly gains, is achieved through the intellect after the senses and reports provide initial data. However, to fully grasp each aspect, one must engage in reflection (التأمل) and examination (النظر). He then explains the dual constitution of humans: they are created with both a physical nature (طبيعة) and an intellect (عقل). What the intellect deems good often differs from what nature desires, and what it deems ugly differs from what nature recoils from. These two may sometimes conflict and sometimes agree. Therefore, one must reflect on every matter to determine its true category—whether it is of the type that senses perceive, reports convey, or intellect judges. The passage ends with the humble acknowledgment that all power is from Allah.

Key Terms
العقل
الطبيعة
النظر
التأمل
الحواس
الأخبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People may think they can rely solely on senses or reports without engaging reason to resolve conflicts or uncover hidden aspects.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that while senses and reports are necessary, the ultimate knowledge requires intellectual reflection, especially when intellect and nature conflict.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is reflection (التأمل) necessary in discerning good and evil?
A) Because the senses and reports are always unreliable.
B) Because humans have both an intellect and a nature that may conflict, requiring reflection to know the true category.
C) Because only revelation can provide knowledge of good and evil.
D) Because the intellect alone is sufficient without any input from senses or reports.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual reflection is essential because the intellect may judge differently than nature, and only through reflection can one determine the true moral status of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When has your natural inclination led you to something your intellect later judged as wrong? How did you resolve that conflict? Are you currently avoiding reflection on any such conflict because it would require changing your behavior?
?
The lesson states that we must reflect on every matter to know its true category. What matter in your life have you been hesitant to reflect upon because it might force you to change a deeply held assumption or habit? Will you set aside time today to examine it?
?
If you believe that your own natural desires are always in harmony with what is good, you are contradicting Maturidi's teaching. What desire do you currently feel that your intellect tells you is wrong, and what specific step will you take this week to act on the intellect instead of the desire?

Refuting Deniers of Sense Perception Through Self-Preservation
Maturidi refutes deniers of sense perception using self-preservation as evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the denial of sense perception by pointing to the denier's own actions of self-preservation, revealing that the denial is not genuine but driven by desires.

Core Idea
Your actions prove you trust sense perception; denial is just desire in disguise.

Maturidi addresses a common doubt: sense perception sometimes errs, so why trust it? He argues that the denier's own behavior—protecting himself from harm—shows he actually relies on sense perception. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how the denier's objection is self-refuting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the doubt
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the examples Maturidi gives where sense perception can be mistaken: impaired sight, dreams, distant pleasures, subtle things.
These are the grounds the denier uses to reject sense perception.
2
Observe the self-preservation argument
Maturidi points out that despite these theoretical doubts, the denier still acts to preserve his life—he avoids fire, water, harmful substances. This shows he actually trusts sense perception.
If he truly doubted, he would not avoid these dangers.
3
Understand the conclusion
The denial is motivated by love of pleasures and desires, not genuine ignorance. Furthermore, the fact that sense perception can err under certain conditions actually confirms its reliability when those conditions are removed.
True sense perception (حق العيان) is known and cannot be denied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر طرفًا من الشبه التي اعترضت من استحوذ عليه رفاه بها عما ظهر من البيان ليعلم أن الذي بعثه على ما اختار خدعة النفس والشيطان وصرة بتسويل عدوه، وذلك لا بتقصير من الله في نصب البرهان. ولا قوة إلا بالله. فنقول: سول الشيطان لمنكر العيان بما قد يخرج على غير الذي حسبه المتأمل فيه، ليصده عن عبادة الرحمن، نحو المؤوف بصره، أو الذي تنازعه نفسه في المنام، أو الملاذ الذي يبعد عنه، أو يدق عن الإحاطة. ثم لم يعمل عليه كيد الشيطان في الصرف عن وكف النفس عن الشهوات، وتوقيه من الجواهر المؤذية، وصون النفس عن اقتحام النيران والبحار ولو كان عن حقيقة جهل ينطق لكان لا بقاء له، لما يقتحم المهالك، ويمتنع عن تناول الأغذية. فثبت أن الذي دعاه إلى ما يقوله حب اللذات والميل إلى الشهوات. مع ما في الذي ذكر من اختلاف الأحوال وتبين الخلاف دليل كاف على أنه قد علم العيان حيث أخبر الخلاف لما ذكر من الحسبان. وعندنا أن ذلك كله بمعنى العيان: إن المؤوف، وفي حال النوم، والبعد والدقة لا يوصل إلى حقائق الأشياء، وعند الارتفاع يوصل. فذلك الذي أوجب من الاختلاف هو حق العيان، لم يجز أن ينكره.
Then we mention a portion of the doubts that afflicted one whom luxury took hold of concerning what appeared from the demonstration, so that it may be known that what urged him to what he chose is the deception of the soul and Satan and an enticement by the suggestion of his enemy, and that is not due to a shortcoming from Allah in erecting the proof. There is no power except with Allah. So we say: Satan embellished to the denier of sense perception what may come out contrary to what the contemplator supposed, in order to turn him away from the worship of the Merciful, such as the one afflicted in his sight, or the one whose soul disputes with him in sleep, or the pleasures that are far from him, or that which is subtle beyond comprehension. Then he did not act upon it by the plot of Satan in diverting from restraining the soul from desires, protecting it from harmful substances, and preserving the soul from throwing itself into fires and seas—and if he spoke out of true ignorance, he would have no survival, for he would throw himself into perils and refrain from taking nourishment. So it is established that what called him to what he says is love of pleasures and inclination to desires. Moreover, what has been mentioned of variation of states and the clear opposition is sufficient evidence that he has known sense perception, since he reported opposition according to what he supposed. And in our view, all of that is the meaning of sense perception: the afflicted one, in the state of sleep, distance and subtlety do not lead to the realities of things, and when the impediment is removed, they do lead. So that which caused the variation is the true sense perception; it is not permissible to deny it.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny the validity of sense perception (العيان). Maturidi introduces the topic by explaining that the doubts raised by such deniers are due to luxury and Satanic deception, not any deficiency in Allah's proofs. He then states the objection: Satan suggests to the denier that sense perception sometimes yields false results, such as the one with impaired sight (المؤوف بصره), dreams, distant pleasures, or things too subtle to perceive. These examples are meant to discredit sense perception. However, Maturidi points out that despite these occasional errors, the denier himself does not act as if he were truly ignorant; rather, he restrains his soul from desires, protects himself from harmful things, and avoids throwing himself into fire and water. If he genuinely doubted sense perception, he would not do these things. Therefore, his real motivation is not genuine doubt but love of pleasures and desires. The variation in sensory experiences (such as errors due to infirmity or sleep) actually proves that true sense perception is known; once obstacles are removed, it leads to reality. Hence, the true sense perception (حق العيان) cannot be denied.

Key Terms
العيان
الشيطان
النفس
الشبه
الاختلاف
اللذات
الشهوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that occasional errors in sense perception make it completely unreliable.
Correction: Maturidi argues that these errors are due to specific obstacles, not a flaw in sense perception itself. The denier's own behavior proves he trusts it.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim is the real reason deniers of sense perception reject it?
A) A genuine intellectual conclusion that sense perception is always false.
B) Love of pleasures and inclination to desires, not genuine doubt.
C) Lack of proper evidence from Allah.
D) The influence of other philosophers' arguments.

Key Takeaway
Sense perception is reliable; its occasional errors are due to specific obstacles, and our actions of self-preservation prove we trust it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the passage again: 'what called him to what he says is love of pleasures and inclination to desires.' Where in your own life do you find yourself rejecting an uncomfortable truth because it conflicts with a desire you cherish? Be honest—not about others, but about yourself. What will you do this week to confront that desire and submit to the truth instead?
?
Maturidi points out that the denier's actions of self-preservation betray his verbal denial. Are there beliefs you claim to hold but your actions contradict? For example, do you act as if you trust Allah's provision while worrying excessively, or as if the Hereafter is real while prioritizing this world? Identify one inconsistency and write a concrete step to align your action with your belief today.
?
The passage says the denier does not act on his doubts—he still avoids fire and water. This means he knows the truth deep down but suppresses it. Is there a truth about yourself, your faith, or your relationship with Allah that you have been avoiding? What is the fear or pleasure that holds you back? Commit to facing it with sincerity before the night ends.
