The Practical Necessity of Reports
Maturidi argues for the practical necessity of reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the practical necessity of reports to refute those who deny them by analogy with sense perception.

Core Idea
Reports are indispensable for daily life; denying them is self-defeating.

In this passage, Maturidi continues his refutation by drawing a parallel between deniers of sense perception and deniers of reports. He begins by noting that falsehood can appear in reports, but then demonstrates through numerous examples that people inevitably rely on reports for their worldly benefits. This practical necessity undermines the denier’s position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the examples Maturidi gives.
The passage begins with an analogy to sense perception.
2
Identify the examples of report reliance
List the specific things Maturidi says depend on reports: pleasant beings, avoiding harms, earnings, strategies, caution, etc.
These are all practical matters.
3
Understand the analogy
Maturidi argues that just as sense perception is accepted despite occasional error, reports should be accepted despite occasional falsehood because of their practical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى مثله قول مثبتي العيان ومنكري الخبر بما قد يظهر فيه الكذب بعد أن ينتشر به القول. ثم قد قيل: الإخبار في الأعيان اللذيذة والجواهر الشهية في الانتفاع مما لولا الإخبار لذة ما احتمل عاقل الخطر بنفسه في الامتحان، وكذلك اتقاء المضار من غير الامتحان، فما نالوا إلى ذلك كله إلا بالإخبار. وعلى ذلك المكاسب والحيل والحذر ونحو ذلك مما يرجع منافع ذلك إلى أبدانهم ودنياهم، وكذلك المضار. فثبت أن الذي بعث هذا إلى التكذيب ما في القول به من إثبات الحرمات، وكف النفس عن الشهوات، فيصير السبب الذي به خدع الشيطان هذا الصنف هو السبب الذي خدع الصنف الأول. ما مع يوجد ذلك في العيان من الوجه الذي بينا، ولم يمنع هؤلاء القول به، فمثله الأول؛ لأنه يظهر الكذب في الإخبار بما اعترض المخبرين من الآفات التي تحملهم عليه. وبعد، قد ظهر صدق كثير من الإخبار، فلم يكن أحد الوجهين به أولى من الآخر إلا بدليل يوضح. والله الموفق. وقد يعامل بالمعاملة الوحشة، والضرب مما يؤذيه ويؤلمه حتى يضطر إلى القول بما لا يحتمل معرفته إلا بالخبر. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And similarly is the statement of those who affirm sense perception and the deniers of reports, by what may appear of falsehood in it after the statement spreads. Then it has been said: Reports about pleasant beings and delightful substances in benefit—were it not for reports, no sane person would have borne the risk to himself in the test—similarly, avoiding harms without testing; they only attained all of that through reports. And upon that are earnings, strategems, caution, and the like, the benefits of which revert to their bodies and worldly life, and likewise harms. So it is established that what drove these to denial is what is in the statement of it: establishing inviolabilities and restraining the soul from desires. So the cause by which Satan deceived this group is the same cause by which he deceived the first group. What meaning exists in sense perception from the aspect we explained did not prevent these from affirming it, so the case is similar to the first; because falsehood appears in reports due to the afflictions that befall the reporters which compel them. Moreover, much truth has appeared in reports, so neither of the two aspects is more entitled to be disbelief than the other except by a clarifying proof. And Allah is the grantor of success. And he may be treated with harsh treatment and beating that harms and pains him until he is compelled to say what cannot be known except through reports. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage extends the previous refutation to those who deny reports (الخبر). Maturidi begins by drawing an analogy with those who affirm sense perception but deny reports, noting that falsehood can sometimes appear in reports after they spread. However, he argues that people inevitably rely on reports for beneficial things—such as enjoying pleasant things (الأعيان اللذيذة) or avoiding harm without testing—and for practical matters like earnings, strategies, and caution. If reports were always false, no one would take risks based on them. Therefore, the real reason for denying reports is the same as for denying sense perception: the desire to avoid religious obligations (إثبات الحرمات) and restrain desires. Satan uses the same trick on both groups. While falsehood can occur in reports due to the condition of the reporters (through afflictions that compel them), much truth is also clearly established, so neither sense perception nor reports can be rejected outright without a clarifying proof (دليل). The passage ends with a remark that sometimes people are coerced by pain and harsh treatment to accept reports they otherwise would reject, indicating that denial is often a matter of will rather than evidence.

Key Terms
الخبر
الإخبار
التكذيب
الكذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following is an example of something that depends on reports?
A) Sense perception of physical objects
B) Avoiding harms without direct testing
C) Innate knowledge of God
D) Logical deduction

Key Takeaway
Reports are essential for practical life; denying them contradicts the very means by which people gain worldly benefits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on reports daily—from weather forecasts to medical advice—yet you may still doubt certain reports. In what specific area of your life does your skepticism contradict your actual behavior, and what would it cost you to act consistently on that doubt?
?
Think of a time you avoided a harm (like spoilt food or a risky area) solely because someone told you. Did you ever consider that your action itself is a testimony to the truthfulness of reports—and what does that imply about your attitude toward the reports of revelation?
?
If you truly believed that all reports could be false, you would starve, avoid all relationships, and never learn. How does your daily reliance on reports expose the dishonesty of your selective skepticism, and what step will you take today to align your belief with your practice?

The Real Motive for Denying Reports
Maturidi reveals the true motive for denying reports and defends their reliability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the real motive for denying reports is the avoidance of religious duties, and that occasional falsehood does not invalidate reports without clear proof.

Core Idea
Denial of reports stems from a desire to escape religious obligations, not from evidence.

Maturidi now reveals the deeper cause behind the denial of reports: the wish to avoid the inviolabilities (hurmat) and restrain desires. He argues that Satan deceived both deniers of sense perception and deniers of reports with the same trick. Moreover, he addresses the objection that falsehood occurs in reports, showing that truth also appears abundantly, so neither can be rejected without proof. Finally, he notes that sometimes people are coerced into accepting reports through pain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and the translation, focusing on the causal link to religious duties.
2
Identify the real cause
Find where Maturidi states the motive for denial: 'إثبات الحرمات وكف النفس عن الشهوات' (establishing inviolabilities and restraining desires).
This is the same cause as for sense perception deniers.
3
Analyze the argument about falsehood and truth
Note that falsehood can appear due to reporters' afflictions, but much truth is clear; neither reports nor sense perception can be dismissed without proof.
4
Reflect on the coercive element
The passage ends with the observation that people can be forced through pain to accept reports, showing that denial is often willful.

The True Cause for Denying Testimony
Maturidi argues that deniers of testimony are motivated by love of pleasures, not ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi traces the denial of testimony to love of pleasures and desires, not ignorance, and that testimony is essential for human benefit.

Core Idea
Denial of testimony is driven by desire, not ignorance.

Imam Maturidi refutes the claim that testimony can be rejected due to its occasional falsehood. He argues that if denial were from ignorance, one could not survive. The real motive is love of pleasures and the desire to avoid prohibitions. This passage reveals how Satan exploits human desires to lead people to reject divine guidance conveyed through testimony.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the flow of Maturidi's argument.
Pay attention to the repeated phrases 'فثبت' and 'فثبت أن' marking conclusions.
2
Identify the core argument
Find where Maturidi states that the denier's motive is love of pleasures. Understand the syllogism: if truly ignorant, one would behave self-destructively; since deniers are alive and functional, their denial must be from desire.
3
Compare sense-perception and testimony
Note how Maturidi treats sense-perception (al-‘iyān) and testimony (al-khabar) as both subject to error, yet both necessary. He argues that denial of testimony is not based on a sound epistemological principle.
The key phrase: 'neither of the two aspects is more deserving than the other except by a clarifying proof'.
4
Reflect on the role of desires
Consider the psychological insight: humans deny truths that conflict with their desires. Reflect on how this applies to contemporary rejections of religious teachings.
This is a timeless lesson in self-awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الملاذ الذي يبعد عنه، أو يدق عن الإحاطة. ثم لم يعمل عليه كيد الشيطان في الصرف عن وكف النفس عن الشهوات، وتوقيه من الجواهر المؤذية، وصون النفس عن اقتحام النيران والبحار ولو كان عن حقيقة جهل ينطق لكان لا بقاء له، لما يقتحم المهالك، ويمتنع عن تناول الأغذية. فثبت أن الذي دعاه إلى ما يقوله حب اللذات والميل إلى الشهوات. مع ما في الذي ذكر من اختلاف الأحوال وتبين الخلاف دليل كاف على أنه قد علم العيان حيث أخبر الخلاف لما ذكر من الحسبان. وعندنا أن ذلك كله بمعنى العيان: إن المؤوف، وفي حال النوم، والبعد والدقة لا يوصل إلى حقائق الأشياء، وعند الارتفاع يوصل. فذلك الذي أوجب من الاختلاف هو حق العيان، لم يجز أن ينكره. وعلى مثله قول مثبتي العيان ومنكري الخبر بما قد يظهر فيه الكذب بعد أن ينتشر به القول. ثم قد قيل: الإخبار في الأعيان اللذيذة والجواهر الشهية في الانتفاع مما لولا الإخبار اللذة ما احتمل عاقل الخطر بنفسه في الامتحان، وكذلك اتقاء المضار من غير الامتحان، فما نالوا إلى ذلك كله إلا بالإخبار. وعلى ذلك المكاسب والحيل والحذر ونحو ذلك مما يرجع منافع ذلك إلى أبدانهم ودنياهم، وكذلك المضار. فثبت أن الذي بعث هذا إلى التكذيب ما في القول به من إثبات الحرمات، وكف النفس عن الشهوات، فيصير السبب الذي به خدع الشيطان هذا الصنف هو السبب الذي خدع الصنف الأول. ما مع يوجد ذلك في العيان من الوجه الذي بينا، ولم يمنع هؤلاء القول به، فمثله الأول؛ لأنه يظهر الكذب في الإخبار بما اعترض المخبرين من الآفات التي تحملهم عليه. وبعد، قد ظهر صدق كثير من الإخبار، فلم يكن أحد الوجهين به أولى من الآخر إلا بدليل يوضح. والله الموفق. وقد يعامل بالمعاملة الوحشة، والضرب مما يؤذيه ويؤلمه حتى يضطر إلى القول بما لا يحتمل معرفته إلا بالخبر. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The pleasures that are far from him, or that are too subtle to encompass. Then he did not act upon it like the deception of Satan in turning away and restraining the soul from desires, and guarding it from harmful substances, and protecting the soul from plunging into fires and seas. If he were speaking from true ignorance, he would have no survival, because he would plunge into destruction and refrain from taking nourishment. So it is established that what called him to what he says is love of pleasures and inclination towards desires. Along with what has been mentioned of difference of states and manifestation of disagreement, it is a sufficient proof that he has known sense-perception (al-‘iyān) since he reported the disagreement for what he mentioned of reckoning. And in our view, all of that is in the sense of sense-perception: that the one afflicted, and in the state of sleep, and distance and subtlety do not reach the realities of things, and when they are removed, they reach. So that which caused the disagreement is the truth of sense-perception; it is not permissible to deny it. And similarly is the statement of those who affirm sense-perception and deny testimony (al-khabar), because falsehood may appear in it after the statement has spread. Then it has been said: The information about pleasurable things and desirable substances in benefit, were it not for the information, no sane person would risk himself in testing, and similarly avoiding harms without testing; they only attained all of that through testimony. And upon that are earnings, tricks, caution, and the like, whose benefits return to their bodies and their worldly life, and similarly harms. So it is established that what drove this one to denial is that which affirming entails of establishing prohibitions and restraining the soul from desires. Thus the cause by which Satan deceived this group is the same cause by which he deceived the first group. What? That exists in sense-perception from the aspect we explained, and these did not prevent [the speaker] from asserting it, so it is like the first; because falsehood appears in testimony due to afflictions that befall the reporters that compel them [to lie]. Moreover, much testimony has appeared true, so neither of the two aspects is more deserving than the other except by a clarifying proof. And God is the granter of success. One may be treated with harsh treatment and beating that harms and pains him until he is forced to say what cannot be known except through testimony. And there is no power except in God.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny testimony (khabar) as a valid source of knowledge. Maturidi begins by arguing that if a person's denial were due to genuine ignorance, he would be unable to survive, as he would recklessly plunge into dangers and refrain from sustenance. Thus the real cause is love of pleasures and desire. He then discusses sense-perception (al-‘iyān), noting that it can be unreliable due to affliction, sleep, distance, or subtlety; nonetheless, sense-perception is true and cannot be denied. He then turns to those who accept sense-perception but deny testimony. He points out that many benefits in life, such as testing pleasures or avoiding harms, rely solely on testimony. Therefore, their denial is also driven by desire to avoid prohibitions and restrain passions. Both groups are deceived by Satan through the same cause: desire. The author further notes that falsehoods may appear in testimony, but so do errors in sense-perception; neither is inherently more reliable without a clarifying proof. Finally, he mentions that people may be forced through harsh treatment to admit what can only be known by testimony, underscoring the necessity of testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That testimony (khabar) is rejected solely because it can be false or because it provides no certain knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the real cause is love of pleasures and desires, and that testimony is indispensable for survival and benefit. Epistemologically, testimony and sense-perception both have errors, so neither can be dismissed without a clarifying proof.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the true cause behind the denial of testimony (khabar)?
A) Genuine ignorance
B) Love of pleasures and inclination towards desires
C) Lack of evidence
D) Following ancestors

Key Takeaway
Denial of testimony is rooted in love of desires, not rational epistemology, and testimony is indispensable for human life and guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reject a truth that demands you change your lifestyle, do you examine your true motives, or do you hide behind intellectual excuses? Will you ask Allah to purify your heart before you judge His message?
?
What pleasures in your life—comfort, reputation, habits—might be preventing you from fully accepting or acting on a known truth? Are you willing to sacrifice them for the sake of what is true?
?
You trust testimony daily in medicine, news, and earnings without demanding proof, yet you may require absolute certainty for religious truths. What does this double standard reveal about the state of your heart toward Allah's guidance?

The Inescapability of Inference
Maturidi exposes the self-refuting nature of denying inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why even those who claim to reject inference must rely on it for practical knowledge of benefits and outcomes.

Core Idea
Inference is unavoidable even for those who deny it.

Maturidi addresses a peculiar contradiction: some people accept sense-perception and testimony as valid sources of knowledge but dismiss inference (istidlāl). Yet, as he shows, everyday judgments about worldly benefits and future outcomes—things not directly sensed or reported—depend entirely on inference. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on how Maturidi describes the position of those who deny inference.
Notice the rhetorical structure: he quotes their position then exposes its inconsistency.
2
Identify the contradiction
Maturidi points out that these individuals rely on inference for worldly benefits and anticipated outcomes. Find where he states this.
The phrase 'على عقله لوجوه المنافع في الدنيا ولعواقب مأمولة' is key.
3
Reflect on everyday examples
Think of a decision you made today based on inference rather than direct sense or testimony. This illustrates the point.
The lesson is that inference is not optional; it is woven into daily life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على مثله قول المقرين بعلم العيان والخبر المنكرين لعلم الاستدلال؛ على عقله لوجوه المنافع في الدنيا ولعواقب مأمولة ليس عنده علم من جهة العيان والخبر، وإنما ذلك بالاستدلال، و [على] ما في ذلك من ظنون الإصابة؛ وكذا معرفة صدق الإخبار وكذبه. مع ما يقال له في كل شيء يعلم مما ليس فيه علم الحس بم علمت ذلك؟ فإن قال: بالخبر يسأل عن معرفة صدقه وكذبه، ويدخل في ذلك جميع الملاذ والمضار مما يتقى ويؤتى مع ما كانت الضرورة تلزم النظر بما عاين وسمع لتعلم منشأ العالم أو حدثه وقدمه. وبعد، فإنه ليس في شيء - يمنع الاستدلال له - خبر في المنع أو عيان؛ فكأنه بالاستدلال يمنع القول به ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبعد، فإن معرفة إنسان أو نار أو شيء بالذي شوهد مرة لا تخرج إلا على الاستدلال بالذي عرف، ولو لم يدلّه للزمه أن لا يعامل أحدا قط؛ ثم لا يقبل تعليم أحد، لأنه لا دليل عنده يعلم أنه من [هو]، ويجوز أن يوجد [دليل] بخبره أولا. ثبت أن كل ذلك استدلال، وهو لازم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Similarly is the statement of those who affirm knowledge of sense-perception and testimony but deny knowledge by inference; they rely [on inference] for the benefits in this world and hoped-for outcomes for which they have no knowledge from sense or testimony, and that is only through inference, and upon what is in that of conjectures of correctness; and likewise knowledge of the truthfulness and falsehood of testimony. Along with what is said to him regarding everything known that does not involve sense-knowledge: 'How did you know that?' If he says: 'By testimony,' he is asked about knowledge of its truthfulness and falsehood, and this includes all pleasures and harms that are avoided and sought, whereas necessity compels reflection upon what one has seen and heard to know the origin of the world and its temporality or eternity. Moreover, there is nothing - for which inference is prevented - that has a preventing testimony or sense-perception; so it is as if by inference he prevents the assertion of it. And there is no power except in God. Moreover, knowledge of a person, or fire, or a thing by means of what was seen once only emerges through inference from what was known. And if he does not infer, it would be necessary for him not to interact with anyone at all; then he would not accept anyone's teaching, because he has no evidence by which he knows who [the teacher] is, and it is possible that evidence exists from his testimony first. So it is established that all of that is inference, and it is necessary. And there is no power except in God.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage refutes those who accept sense-perception and testimony as sources of knowledge but reject inference (istidlāl). Maturidi argues that even the worldly benefits and anticipated outcomes that are not known directly through sense or testimony are known only through inference. Furthermore, determining the truthfulness or falsehood of testimony itself requires inference. He poses a question: for any knowledge not derived from the senses, how is it known? If one answers 'by testimony', then the truth of that testimony must be established, again requiring inference. Necessity compels us to reflect on what we see and hear to understand the origin and nature of the world. He then asserts that there is no proof from sense or testimony that prohibits inference; indeed, to deny inference is to prevent any assertion. He provides a concrete example: recognizing a person or a fire seen once depends on inference from previously known things. Without inference, one could not interact with anyone or accept teaching, as one would have no evidence to identify a teacher or to trust his testimony. Thus inference is necessary. This argument demonstrates that inference is not an optional tool but a fundamental requirement for knowledge and social life.

Key Terms
العيان
الخبر
الاستدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inference is an unnecessary or unreliable method of knowledge that can be discarded in favor of sense and testimony.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even those who deny inference use it for all practical decision-making outside direct sense and testimony, proving it is unavoidable.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do those who deny inference rely on for hoped-for outcomes?
A) Sense-perception
B) Testimony
C) Inference
D) Revelation

Key Takeaway
Inference is inescapable: every practical judgment about future benefits or unseen matters depends on it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision about your career, education, or investments, do you admit that you are relying on inference—or do you pretend you have certainty? What would change if you honestly acknowledged that your plans are based on conjecture?
?
Think of a time you rejected a religious truth claim because it seemed 'not proven' or 'speculative.' Were you secretly relying on inference in other areas of your life at that same moment? Does that inconsistency trouble you?
?
If inference were truly invalid, every hope you have for the future—every intention to act for an unseen outcome—would collapse. Are you willing to examine your own life and see where you depend on inference daily? What would it mean for your faith in the unseen if inference were not a valid path to knowledge?

The Necessity of Inference for Knowledge and Social Life
Maturidi demonstrates inference's role in verifying testimony and enabling social life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how inference is required for verifying testimony, understanding the world's origin, and basic recognition in daily interactions.

Core Idea
Inference is the foundation of testimony verification and social interaction.

Maturidi continues his argument by showing that inference is not only used for practical benefits but is essential for determining the truthfulness of testimony, for reasoning about the origin of the world, and even for recognizing a person or object seen once. Without inference, knowledge and social life become impossible. This lesson explores these deeper dimensions.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
From 'وكذا معرفة صدق الإخبار وكذبه' to the end. Notice the questions Maturidi poses.
The argument becomes more systematic, showing inference underlies all knowledge claims.
2
Trace the logic about testimony
Maturidi asks: if someone claims knowledge by testimony, how do they know the testimony is true? That requires inference.
This shows that testimony itself depends on inference for its validation.
3
Consider the practical example
The example of recognizing a person or fire seen once shows that even simple recognition requires inference from past knowledge.
Without inference, one could not interact with others or accept teaching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على مثله قول المقرين بعلم العيان والخبر المنكرين لعلم الاستدلال؛ على عقله لوجوه المنافع في الدنيا ولعواقب مأمولة ليس عنده علم من جهة العيان والخبر، وإنما ذلك بالاستدلال، و [على] ما في ذلك من ظنون الإصابة؛ وكذا معرفة صدق الإخبار وكذبه. مع ما يقال له في كل شيء يعلم مما ليس فيه علم الحس بم علمت ذلك؟ فإن قال: بالخبر يسأل عن معرفة صدقه وكذبه، ويدخل في ذلك جميع الملاذ والمضار مما يتقى ويؤتى مع ما كانت الضرورة تلزم النظر بما عاين وسمع لتعلم منشأ العالم أو حدثه وقدمه. وبعد، فإنه ليس في شيء - يمنع الاستدلال له - خبر في المنع أو عيان؛ فكأنه بالاستدلال يمنع القول به ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبعد، فإن معرفة إنسان أو نار أو شيء بالذي شوهد مرة لا تخرج إلا على الاستدلال بالذي عرف، ولو لم يدلّه للزمه أن لا يعامل أحدا قط؛ ثم لا يقبل تعليم أحد، لأنه لا دليل عنده يعلم أنه من [هو]، ويجوز أن يوجد [دليل] بخبره أولا. ثبت أن كل ذلك استدلال، وهو لازم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Similarly is the statement of those who affirm knowledge of sense-perception and testimony but deny knowledge by inference; they rely [on inference] for the benefits in this world and hoped-for outcomes for which they have no knowledge from sense or testimony, and that is only through inference, and upon what is in that of conjectures of correctness; and likewise knowledge of the truthfulness and falsehood of testimony. Along with what is said to him regarding everything known that does not involve sense-knowledge: 'How did you know that?' If he says: 'By testimony,' he is asked about knowledge of its truthfulness and falsehood, and this includes all pleasures and harms that are avoided and sought, whereas necessity compels reflection upon what one has seen and heard to know the origin of the world and its temporality or eternity. Moreover, there is nothing - for which inference is prevented - that has a preventing testimony or sense-perception; so it is as if by inference he prevents the assertion of it. And there is no power except in God. Moreover, knowledge of a person, or fire, or a thing by means of what was seen once only emerges through inference from what was known. And if he does not infer, it would be necessary for him not to interact with anyone at all; then he would not accept anyone's teaching, because he has no evidence by which he knows who [the teacher] is, and it is possible that evidence exists from his testimony first. So it is established that all of that is inference, and it is necessary. And there is no power except in God.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage refutes those who accept sense-perception and testimony as sources of knowledge but reject inference (istidlāl). Maturidi argues that even the worldly benefits and anticipated outcomes that are not known directly through sense or testimony are known only through inference. Furthermore, determining the truthfulness or falsehood of testimony itself requires inference. He poses a question: for any knowledge not derived from the senses, how is it known? If one answers 'by testimony', then the truth of that testimony must be established, again requiring inference. Necessity compels us to reflect on what we see and hear to understand the origin and nature of the world. He then asserts that there is no proof from sense or testimony that prohibits inference; indeed, to deny inference is to prevent any assertion. He provides a concrete example: recognizing a person or a fire seen once depends on inference from previously known things. Without inference, one could not interact with anyone or accept teaching, as one would have no evidence to identify a teacher or to trust his testimony. Thus inference is necessary. This argument demonstrates that inference is not an optional tool but a fundamental requirement for knowledge and social life.

Key Terms
الخبر
الاستدلال
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Testimony is a standalone source of knowledge that does not require inference.
Correction: Maturidi shows that testimony must be verified, and that verification depends on inference, making inference prior to reliable testimony.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen if a person refused to use inference?
A) He would still know through sense-perception
B) He could not interact with anyone or accept teaching
C) He would only rely on testimony
D) He would be saved from error

Key Takeaway
Inference is necessary not only for theoretical knowledge but for the simplest acts of recognition and social life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on others' testimony for much of your religious knowledge. When you accept the Prophet's report (khabar) about the unseen, do you recognise that you are using inference? What does this lesson demand from you in terms of actively reflecting (naẓar) on that testimony to strengthen your certainty? Will you commit to studying the proofs for the truth of his message?
?
If even recognising a friend's face after one meeting requires inference, how much more do you need inference to know your Creator through His creation? Yet do you engage in such inference daily? What one step will you take today to practice this inferential thinking when you observe the universe?
?
The lesson says that without inference you could not accept a teacher or engage in social life. Think of a time you trusted someone without proper verification. How does this passage urge you to be more diligent in verifying claims—especially in matters of faith? What will you do differently when you hear a new religious claim tomorrow?

Proof of the Temporality of Living Beings
Maturidi proves living beings are temporal from their dependency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that living beings are temporal because of their inherent dependency and subjugation.

Core Idea
Living beings are temporal because they are not self-sufficient; they depend on others.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise proof for the temporality (huduth) of living beings. He begins with a general observation about things described with life: they are not self-existing but are subjugated and used for the needs of others. From this, he derives a powerful argument that applies to all living beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the key terms: muhdatha, taskhir, tadhlil.
2
Understand the core argument
The passage argues that anything described with life is temporal because it is not self-sufficient but is subjugated and used for others' needs.
3
See the application to living beings
If this is true for substances, then living beings (who are even more dependent) are even more deserving of being temporal.
This is an a fortiori argument (from the stronger).
4
Reflect on the rational basis
Consider why dependency implies temporality: a self-sufficient thing would be eternal, but dependence shows need and thus originatedness.
This connects to the broader kalam proof from contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
توصف بالحياة ثبت أنها محدثة؛ إذ هي لا على ما هي بنفسها ولكن على تسخيرها وتذليلها واستعمالها في حوائج غيرها . وإذا ثبت ذلك في أصل الجواهر، فالأحياء" الذين هم فيها وبها تقر وتنتفع وهم مجبولون على الحاجات والمنافع أحق بذلك. والله الموفق.
Everything described with life is proved to be temporal (muhdatha); because it is not as it is by itself, but rather by its subjugation (taskhir), taming (tadhlil), and use in the needs of others. And if that is established for the essence of substances (asl al-jawahir), then living beings who are in them and by them are settled and benefit, and they are created with needs and benefits, are more deserving of that. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a brief proof for the temporality (huduth) of living beings. It argues that anything described as alive is temporal because it is not self-sufficient; rather, it is subjugated, tamed, and used for the needs of others. This principle is first established for the essence of substances (asl al-jawahir), and then applied to living beings (al-ahya') who are even more dependent, since they are innately needy and benefit from others. The conclusion is that living beings are even more deserving of being considered temporal. The argument is concise and relies on observed dependency.

Key Terms
محدثة (muhdatha)
تسخير (taskhir)
تذليل (tadhlil)
جواهر (jawahir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims life itself is an accident that is temporal.
Correction: The argument does not claim life itself is an accident; it argues from the observed dependency of living things, not from the nature of life as an attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are things described with life proven to be temporal?
A) Because they are self-sufficient
B) Because they are subjugated and used for others' needs
C) Because they are eternal
D) Because they have no needs

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's proof shows that living beings are temporal because they are inherently dependent and subjugated, not self-sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says living beings are 'subjugated, tamed, and used in the needs of others.' In your own life, when do you most feel that you are not independent but rather subjugated and used by Allah's design? How does that feeling drive you to recognize your createdness? What is one way you can actively submit to that reality today?
?
If it is true that you are not self-sufficient but dependent on Allah at every moment, what does that mean for how you handle your pride and sense of self-importance? Is there an area in your life where you act as if you are independent? What step can you take this week to humble yourself in that area and acknowledge your need for Allah?
?
The proof concludes that living beings are temporal—they have a beginning. You know you were born and will die. But do you live as if this life is your own, or as a temporary trust from the Eternal? If you truly believed you are temporal and owned by Allah, what would you change about how you spend your time, money, or energy today?

The World's Dependence on Accidents
Maturidi's main argument from accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the world cannot be eternal because it is never free from temporal accidents.

Core Idea
The world's continual change proves it cannot be eternal.

This passage presents a second proof for the temporality of the world. Maturidi argues that the world is always in a state of change, with accidents like motion and stillness succeeding each other. Since these accidents are temporal, the world itself must be temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the English translation.
Notice the list of opposites: composition/separation, motion/stillness, etc.
2
Identify the key premise
The key premise is that the world is never free from accidents. These accidents are known to be temporal because opposites cannot coexist, so they must succeed each other.
This establishes succession (ta‘aqub) and temporality (hadath).
3
Understand the conclusion
If accidents are temporal, and the world is never free from them and does not precede them, then the world must also be temporal.
4
Reflect on the implication
This argument does not necessarily prove a beginning in time, but it proves the world cannot be eternal as it is (i.e., unchanging). It must have a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و دلیل آخر، أن العالم لا يخلو من أن يكون (۱) قديما على ما عليه أحواله من اجتماع وتفرق، وحركة وسكون، وخبيث وطيب وحسن وقبيح، وزيادة ونقصان، وهن حوادث بالحس والعقل، إذ لا يجوز اجتماع الضدين؛ فثبت التعاقب، وفيه الحدث، وجميع الحوادث تحت الكون بعد أن لم تكن ، فكذلك ما لا يخلو عنها ولا يسبقها؛ ٢) أو كان إنشاء عن أصل لا بهذه الصفة، وانتقل إليها باعتراضها فيه . فإن كان ذلك ثبت أن هذا العالم حادث، وبطل قول من ينكر [الحدث ]". وإن كان غير هذا، فإن كان الأول هو المنشيء له فهو قولنا : هو البارئ، وسموه هَيولى . وإن كان على الانتقال إليها فذهب الأول وصار هذا غيره؛ فهذا محدث بما لم يكن هو الأول، والأول محدث بما هلك لما انتقل إلى الثاني. مع ما لا يكون شيء
٤
من شيء [خاليا] من أن يكون مستجنّاً فيه فيظهر، أو محدثًا فيه فيتولد ويخرج، أو يتلف الأول فيكون الثاني. فالأول كالولد / والشيء الموضوع في الوعاء، ومحال كون أضعاف ما فيه فيما [٦ظ]
هو فيه؛ لذلك يبطل القول بكون الإنسان في النطفة ، والشجر في الحب. وعلى ذلك من يقول بالبروز بالقوة " . مع ما كان في ذلك إيجابُ حَدَثٍ ذاته، لأن القوة علية غيره إذ هو وجد بالفعل لا بالقوة، أو تلف الأول نحو النطفة ثم النَّسَمَةِ ونحو ذلك، فيصير الأول هالكًا حتى لا يبقى له الأثر، والثاني حادثاً حتى لم يبق من الأول فيه أثر، وفي ذلك حدث الأول
والثاني.
Another proof: that the world is never free from being either eternal as it is in its states of composition and separation, motion and stillness, foul and pure, good and evil, increase and decrease — and these are accidents (hawadith) by sense and reason, for it is impossible for two opposites to coexist; thus succession (ta‘aqub) is established, and in it is temporality (hadath), and all accidents fall under existence after non-existence; so likewise that which is never free from them and does not precede them. Or it was created from an original not in this condition, and then transferred to them by occurring in it. If that is the case, then it is established that this world is temporal, and the saying of one who denies [temporality] is refuted. And if it is other than this, then if the first is the creator of it, that is our saying: He is the Creator, and they call it hyle (hayula). And if it is by transfer, then the first perishes and this becomes other than it; so this is temporal in that it was not the first, and the first is temporal in that it perishes when it transferred to the second. Moreover, nothing comes from something [void] of it, either being hidden within it and then appearing, or being temporally originated in it and then generated and exiting, or the first perishes and the second comes to be. The first is like the child and the object placed in a vessel, and it is impossible for multiples of what is in it to be in it; therefore the saying that man is in the sperm and the tree in the seed is refuted. And likewise the one who says about emergence from potentiality (al-quwwa). Moreover, in that is necessarily the temporality of its essence, because the potentiality is the cause of other than it, for it exists actually not potentially; or the first perishes like the sperm and then the living being, and so on. Thus the first becomes destroyed until no trace of it remains, and the second becomes temporal until no trace of the first remains in it, and in that is the temporality of the first and the second.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof for the temporality (huduth) of the world, structured in two parts. First, the main argument: the world is never free from opposing accidents such as composition/separation, motion/stillness, good/evil, etc. These accidents are known by sense and reason to be temporal because opposites cannot coexist, so they must succeed each other. That which is never free from such accidents and does not precede them must itself be temporal. Second, the passage refutes alternative theories: (a) if the world was created from an original entity (like hyle) and then changed, that original entity either created it directly (which is the Islamic view: Allah is the Creator) or it was transferred. In the transfer case, both the original and the new are temporal. The passage further argues against the idea of hidden potentialities (like the tree in the seed) by showing that if something comes from another, either it was latent (which is impossible as the contained cannot be greater than the container) or it is generated anew, which implies temporality. The argument concludes that both the original and the resultant are temporal.

Key Terms
اجتماع (ijtima‘)
تفرق (tafarruq)
حركة (haraka)
سكون (sukun)
ضدين (diddayn)
تعاقب (ta‘aqub)
حادث (hadith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves the world had a beginning in time.
Correction: The argument proves the world cannot be eternal (unchanging), but it does not necessarily prove a first moment of existence; it only shows dependence on a temporal cause.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's proof, why must the world be temporal?
A) Because it was created from nothing
B) Because it is never free from accidents that succeed each other
C) Because it has potentiality
D) Because it is composed of atoms

Key Takeaway
The world's constant change proves it cannot be eternal; it must be temporal and dependent on a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You witness constant change in your own life—your emotions, your body, your relationships. How does Maturidi’s argument that the world is never free from accidents challenge your tendency to cling to these changing things as if they are permanent? What one attachment will you consciously loosen today because of this truth?
?
The proof states that opposites cannot coexist. Consider a time you tried to hold two contradictory desires or beliefs in your heart (e.g., loving the world and loving Allah). Did your inability to sustain that contradiction reveal the temporality of your own state? How will you resolve that contradiction now?
?
If the world is temporal and dependent on accidents, then you too are dependent on a Creator every moment. Does your daily life reflect that dependence? What specific habit or mindset denies it, and what concrete change will you make this week to affirm it?

Refutation of Hyle and Potentiality
Maturidi refutes hyle and potentiality theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the ideas of a pre-existing matter (hyle) and latent potentialities as explanations for the world's origin.

Core Idea
Every alternative theory leads to temporality, affirming the need for a Creator.

After presenting his main argument, Maturidi addresses alternative theories that deny direct creation from nothing. He shows that even if one posits an original matter (hyle) or hidden potentialities, the world must still be temporal. This reinforces the necessity of a Creator who brings things into existence from non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
This passage is dense and refutes several alternatives. Read both Arabic and translation slowly.
Look for the structure: 'if it is other than this...'
2
Identify the first alternative: hyle
Maturidi considers the view that the world was created from an original matter (hyle) that was not in this condition. He argues that if that original created the world, it is the Creator (our view). If it transferred, both original and new are temporal.
3
Identify the second alternative: potentiality
The passage refutes the idea that things like trees are hidden in seeds. This is impossible because a larger thing cannot be contained in a smaller. Also, potentiality implies temporality because the thing exists actually, not potentially.
4
Understand the conclusion
Both alternatives fail to maintain the eternity of the world. The only consistent view is that the world is temporal and created directly by Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second, the passage refutes alternative theories: (a) if the world was created from an original entity (like hyle) and then changed, that original entity either created it directly (which is the Islamic view: Allah is the Creator) or it was transferred. In the transfer case, both the original and the new are temporal. The passage further argues against the idea of hidden potentialities (like the tree in the seed) by showing that if something comes from another, either it was latent (which is impossible as the contained cannot be greater than the container) or it is generated anew, which implies temporality. The argument concludes that both the original and the resultant are temporal.

Key Terms
هيولى (hayula)
قوة (quwwa)
بارئ (al-bari')
حدث (hadath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi argues for creation from nothing (ex nihilo).
Correction: Maturidi argues that the world is temporal, but he does not explicitly argue for creation from nothing in this passage; he refutes alternative theories and shows they imply temporality, leaving direct creation as the only coherent option.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the idea that a tree is latent in a seed?
A) Because seeds are too small
B) Because the tree is not actually in the seed
C) Because opposites cannot coexist
D) Because the seed is temporal

Key Takeaway
Alternative theories like hyle and potentiality cannot explain the world's existence without implying temporality; only direct creation by Allah is consistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own talents and potential, do you instinctively assume they are hidden within you like the tree in the seed? How does Maturidi's refutation of that idea force you to recognize that every good quality you have is directly created by Allah moment by moment? Will you thank Him for each one as a new gift today?
?
The hyle theory suggests that change can happen without a Creator. In your life, where do you tend to attribute change and growth to natural processes or your own efforts, forgetting that Allah is the only true agent? What specific step will you take today to see His hand in every small change?
?
If both the original and the transformed are temporal, what does that say about your attachments to things that seem to 'evolve' over time—like your career, relationships, or even your own identity? Are you clinging to something that is transient and perishing, or are you anchoring your trust in the Eternal Creator who alone remains?

The Distinction Between Perpetual Existence and Pre-Eternity
Maturidi presents the objection and the first rebuttal defining hadath and baqa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conceptual difference between temporal existence (hadath) and perpetual existence (baqa') and why the objection equating afterlife permanence with pre-eternity is contradictory.

Core Idea
Temporality means existence after non-existence; perpetuity means continued existence in time.

The objector tries to draw an analogy between the afterlife's perpetuity and the world's pre-eternity. Maturidi first responds by exposing a fundamental confusion: the meaning of 'temporal' is not the same as 'perpetual'. He defines both terms to show they are distinct categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first response
Read the Arabic passage that includes the objection and Maturidi's first reason. Notice how he defines hadath and baqa'.
The objection is phrased as a rhetorical question.
2
Identify the definitions
Locate the definitions of hadath (temporality) and baqa' (perpetual existence). Understand that hadath is existence after non-existence, while baqa' is existence in subsequent time.
3
See the contradiction
Maturidi argues that calling something 'qadim' (pre-eternal) when it has no prior non-existence contradicts the definition of hadath. Reflect on why the analogy fails.
The objection conflates two distinct logical categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل: إذا جاز عندكم بقاء الأعيان في الآخرة بما لا يبقى"، لم لا جاز قدمها بما
لا يتقدم " ؟
قيل لوجوه أحدها للتناقض، وهو أن معنى الحدث هو الكون بعد أن لم يكن، فمن لا يسبقه ففيه حقيقية ، فالقول فيه بالقدم ينقضه " ، ومعنى البقاء هو الكون في مستأنف الوقت، معه غير أو لا، لذلك اختلفا. والثاني أن القول بالذي ذكرت من البقاء إنما هو سمعي، فإما أن تُسلّم لى ذلك، فيجب حدث الأعيان لما به عرفناه، أو لا تُسلّم، فيبطل حِجَاجُك" بالسمعي على الإنكار به. والله
المستعان .
وأيضا إن الشيء إذا لم يكن إلا بِغَيْرِ يتقدمه، وذلك شرط كل الأغيار، فيبطل كون الجميع ، ولا كذلك أمر البقاء. ألا ترى أن من قال لآخر : لا تأكل شيئًا حتى تأكل غيره - وكذا كل غير فيه ذلك الشرط - فيبقى " أبداً غير آكل. ولو قال : كلما أكلت لقمة فكل أخرى فهو يبقى أبدا» في الأكل، فمثله الأول. وعلى ذلك أمر تضاعف الحساب، إنه إذا لم يُجعل له ابتداء منه يُبدأ لا يجوز وجود شيء منه بَنّة، وإذا حصل البداية يجوز أن يبقى فيه فيما يزيد، ثم [۷] يزيد دائما . / ولا قوة إلا بالله . مع ما يُذكر أوائل الحساب في كل عدد إذ به بلغ ذلك ولا يُذكر
٤
نهايتها، لذلك اختلفا.
وأيضا إنا لو توهمنا أن لا جسم وأنه يجوز وجود عَرَض قبل عَرَض لم يجز وجود شيء منه، إذ لم يُجعل له أولية وابتداء ؛ ويجوز الوجود أبداً بلا نهاية له، فمثله ما لا يخلو عن * الأعراض. والله الموفق.
وأيضا إن كل حركة أو اجتماع نشير إليه هو نهاية ما مضى من ذلك النوع، فمحال وجود نهاية الماضي بلا ابتداء له. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضا أن قد يوصف جسم بالبقاء مع بقاء واحد وإن كان ذلك البقاء لا يبقى
If one says: 'Since you allow the perpetual existence (baqa') of entities in the afterlife through what does not remain, why do you not allow their pre-eternity (qidam) through what does not precede?' It is said for several reasons: first, for the contradiction, which is that the meaning of temporality (hadath) is existence after non-existence; so that which is not preceded by it has the reality of it; therefore, the claim of pre-eternity for it contradicts it. And the meaning of perpetual existence (baqa') is existence in subsequent time, whether with something else or not; therefore they differ. Second, that the claim you mentioned about perpetual existence is only scriptural (sam‘i). So either you accept that, then the temporality of entities is necessarily required by what we know, or you do not accept, then your argument by the scriptural against the denial of it is invalid. And Allah is the Succorer. Also, if a thing only exists through another that precedes it, and that is the condition of all other things, then the existence of all is invalid; but the matter of perpetual existence is not like that. Do you not see that if one says to another: 'Do not eat anything until you eat something else' — and likewise every other thing has that condition — then he will never eat. But if he says: 'Whenever you eat a morsel, eat another,' then he will always be eating. The first is analogous. And likewise the matter of the doubling of numbers: if no beginning is assigned from which to start, then no part of it can exist at all; but if a beginning is obtained, it can continue to increase, then [thus] always increase. And there is no power except from Allah. Along with the fact that the beginnings of numbers are mentioned in every number, because by it that is reached, but its end is not mentioned; therefore they differ. Also, if we suppose that there is no body and that it is permissible for one accident to exist before another, then no accident could exist, because no first and beginning is assigned; but existence could always occur without end. So likewise that which is never free from accidents. And Allah is the Grantor of success. Also, every motion or composition we point to is the end of what has passed of that kind; so it is impossible for the end of the past to exist without a beginning. And there is no power except from Allah. Also, a body may be described with perpetual existence (baqa') with a single perpetual existence even if that perpetual existence does not remain.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is an objection-response unit. The objector argues: if perpetual existence (baqa') is possible in the afterlife (as you Muslims claim) through something that does not itself remain (i.e., continuous renewal), then why can't the world be pre-eternal (qadim) through something that does not precede it (i.e., an eternal duration without beginning)? The response provides multiple reasons. First, a linguistic/conceptual distinction: hadath (temporality) means existence after non-existence, so if a thing has no prior non-existence, calling it pre-eternal contradicts the definition. Baqa' means continued existence in subsequent time, regardless of whether it is with another or not, so the two concepts differ. Second, the claim about afterlife is known through revelation (sam‘i), not reason; thus if you accept revelation, it requires that worldly entities are temporal, but if you reject revelation, your argument from it fails. Third, if a thing's existence depends on a preceding different thing, and this condition applies to all others, then nothing could exist at all; but baqa' does not have this condition (it is a continuation, not a new beginning). The analogy of eating: if one says 'don't eat until you eat another', one never eats (requiring an infinite regress); but if one says 'every time you eat a morsel, eat another', one can always continue (like baqa' after a beginning). Fourth, the analogy of doubling numbers: if no starting number is given, no number can exist; but if a start exists, it can increase indefinitely. Fifth, if we suppose no body (no spatial substrate) and permit one accident after another, no accident could exist because there would be no first; but if a first exists, continuation is possible. Sixth, every motion or composition is the end of a past sequence, so there must be a beginning. Seventh, a body can be described with a single baqa' (perpetual existence) even if that baqa' itself does not persist (i.e., it is renewed). The overall point is that pre-eternity and perpetual continuation after a beginning are fundamentally different.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqa')
قدم (qidam)
تناقض (tanaqud)
حدث (hadath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection assumes that perpetuity in the afterlife is logically the same as pre-eternity of the world.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the two concepts have different definitions: hadath (temporality) is existence after non-existence, while baqa' (perpetuity) is existence in subsequent time. They are not interchangeable.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the meaning of hadath (temporality)?
A) Existence without beginning
B) Existence after non-existence
C) Existence forever
D) Existence in the afterlife

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it ignores the definitional difference between temporality (existence after non-existence) and perpetuity (continued existence in time).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that hadath means existence after non-existence. When you reflect on your own life—that you were not, then you were—does this reality humble you enough to submit to the One who always was? In what specific habit are you living as if you will never cease to be, forgetting your own temporality?
?
The objector failed to distinguish between what is truly eternal and what merely persists. Think of one thing in your life—a relationship, a possession, or a status—that you treat as if it were eternal. How does the lesson's distinction between hadath and baqa' expose this error? What is one concrete step you will take today to reorient that attachment?

The Role of Revelation in Arguments About the Afterlife
Maturidi presents the second reason: the scriptural nature of the premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the scriptural basis (sam‘iyyat) of afterlife belief to undermine the objector's argument.

Core Idea
The claim about perpetual existence in the afterlife is based on revelation, not reason, so the objector must accept revelation or lose his ground.

The objector attempts to use the Muslim claim about afterlife perpetuity as a premise in a rational argument. Maturidi points out that this premise is known through scriptural evidence, not pure reason. He then presents a dilemma: either accept revelation and its implications, or reject it and invalidate your own argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the second reason
Read Maturidi's second response. He states that the claim about perpetual existence is only scriptural (sam‘i).
Sam‘i refers to knowledge derived from revelation.
2
Understand the dilemma
Maturidi presents a conditional: if you accept the scriptural basis, then the world's temporality is also required; if you reject it, your argument based on that premise fails.
The objector cannot have it both ways.
3
Reflect on the strategy
Notice how Maturidi uses the objector's own reliance on a revealed premise to turn the argument back on him.
This is a common dialectical move in kalam.

?

?

The Necessity of a Beginning for Perpetual Continuation
Maturidi provides multiple analogies to illustrate the necessity of a beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that perpetual existence after a beginning is possible, but existence without a beginning leads to infinite regress and impossibility.

Core Idea
For anything to have perpetual continuation, it must have a starting point; otherwise, nothing can exist.

Maturidi now presents a series of analogies to demonstrate that for something to persist indefinitely, it must first have a beginning. Without a beginning, an infinite regress arises where nothing can ever start. This proves that the world's pre-eternity is impossible, while the afterlife's perpetuity after a creation is perfectly logical.

Guided Path
1
Read the third to seventh reasons
Read the remaining part of the passage from 'وأيضا إن الشيء' to the end. Notice the multiple analogies.
The arguments are presented as 'also' (wa-aydan) statements.
2
Analyze the eating analogy
Maturidi gives an example: if one says 'do not eat until you eat another', one never eats. But if one says 'whenever you eat, eat another', one always eats. The first case requires a preceding other, leading to infinite regress; the second has a start.
This illustrates the need for a first action.
3
Examine the doubling numbers analogy
If no beginning number is set, no number can be reached. With a start, numbers can increase indefinitely. This mirrors the need for a first moment for the world.
The 'beginning' is the first number.
4
Review the accidents and motion arguments
Without a body, accidents cannot begin; with a first, they can continue. Similarly, every motion is the end of a past sequence, so a beginning must exist.
These arguments reinforce the same point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وأيضا إنا لو توهمنا أن لا جسم وأنه يجوز وجود عَرَض قبل عَرَض لم يجز وجود شيء منه، إذ لم يُجعل له أولية وابتداء ؛ ويجوز الوجود أبداً بلا نهاية له، فمثله ما لا يخلو عن * الأعراض. والله الموفق.
وأيضا إن كل حركة أو اجتماع نشير إليه هو نهاية ما مضى من ذلك النوع، فمحال وجود نهاية الماضي بلا ابتداء له. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضا أن قد يوصف جسم بالبقاء مع بقاء واحد وإن كان ذلك البقاء لا يبقى
If one says: 'Since you allow the perpetual existence (baqa') of entities in the afterlife through what does not remain, why do you not allow their pre-eternity (qidam) through what does not precede?' It is said for several reasons: first, for the contradiction, which is that the meaning of temporality (hadath) is existence after non-existence; so that which is not preceded by it has the reality of it; therefore, the claim of pre-eternity for it contradicts it. And the meaning of perpetual existence (baqa') is existence in subsequent time, whether with something else or not; therefore they differ. Second, that the claim you mentioned about perpetual existence is only scriptural (sam‘i). So either you accept that, then the temporality of entities is necessarily required by what we know, or you do not accept, then your argument by the scriptural against the denial of it is invalid. And Allah is the Succorer. Also, if a thing only exists through another that precedes it, and that is the condition of all other things, then the existence of all is invalid; but the matter of perpetual existence is not like that. Do you not see that if one says to another: 'Do not eat anything until you eat something else' — and likewise every other thing has that condition — then he will never eat. But if he says: 'Whenever you eat a morsel, eat another,' then he will always be eating. The first is analogous. And likewise the matter of the doubling of numbers: if no beginning is assigned from which to start, then no part of it can exist at all; but if a beginning is obtained, it can continue to increase, then [thus] always increase. And there is no power except from Allah. Along with the fact that the beginnings of numbers are mentioned in every number, because by it that is reached, but its end is not mentioned; therefore they differ. Also, if we suppose that there is no body and that it is permissible for one accident to exist before another, then no accident could exist, because no first and beginning is assigned; but existence could always occur without end. So likewise that which is never free from accidents. And Allah is the Grantor of success. Also, every motion or composition we point to is the end of what has passed of that kind; so it is impossible for the end of the past to exist without a beginning. And there is no power except from Allah. Also, a body may be described with perpetual existence (baqa') with a single perpetual existence even if that perpetual existence does not remain.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is an objection-response unit. The objector argues: if perpetual existence (baqa') is possible in the afterlife (as you Muslims claim) through something that does not itself remain (i.e., continuous renewal), then why can't the world be pre-eternal (qadim) through something that does not precede it (i.e., an eternal duration without beginning)? The response provides multiple reasons. First, a linguistic/conceptual distinction: hadath (temporality) means existence after non-existence, so if a thing has no prior non-existence, calling it pre-eternal contradicts the definition. Baqa' means continued existence in subsequent time, regardless of whether it is with another or not, so the two concepts differ. Second, the claim about afterlife is known through revelation (sam‘i), not reason; thus if you accept revelation, it requires that worldly entities are temporal, but if you reject revelation, your argument from it fails. Third, if a thing's existence depends on a preceding different thing, and this condition applies to all others, then nothing could exist at all; but baqa' does not have this condition (it is a continuation, not a new beginning). The analogy of eating: if one says 'don't eat until you eat another', one never eats (requiring an infinite regress); but if one says 'every time you eat a morsel, eat another', one can always continue (like baqa' after a beginning). Fourth, the analogy of doubling numbers: if no starting number is given, no number can exist; but if a start exists, it can increase indefinitely. Fifth, if we suppose no body (no spatial substrate) and permit one accident after another, no accident could exist because there would be no first; but if a first exists, continuation is possible. Sixth, every motion or composition is the end of a past sequence, so there must be a beginning. Seventh, a body can be described with a single baqa' (perpetual existence) even if that baqa' itself does not persist (i.e., it is renewed). The overall point is that pre-eternity and perpetual continuation after a beginning are fundamentally different.

Key Terms
ابتدا (ibtida')
نهاية (nihaya)
عرض ('arad)
جسم (jism)
حركة (haraka)
اجتماع (ijtima')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal existence is logically impossible regardless of whether it has a beginning or not.
Correction: Maturidi shows that perpetual existence after a beginning is possible (like afterlife), but pre-eternal existence without beginning leads to paradox.

Checkpoint
What does the eating analogy illustrate?
A) The impossibility of perpetual eating
B) The necessity of a beginning for continuation
C) The role of revelation
D) The difference between hadath and baqa'

Key Takeaway
Without a beginning, no infinite sequence can start; with a beginning, indefinite continuation is logically possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The eating analogy shows that without a starting morsel, you never eat. What spiritual 'first morsel' have you been avoiding because you think you need something else first? Will you take that step today, trusting Allah to provide the continuation?
?
Numbers cannot increase without a beginning. Is there an area of your character or worship where you have been waiting for a 'perfect start' instead of simply beginning? What is holding you back from taking that first, possibly imperfect, step?
?
Every motion you make is the end of a past series. Think of a habit you have. Can you trace it back to its beginning? If that beginning was not for Allah, are you willing to repent and start a new series today?

Objection and Answer on Color and Accident
Maturidi refutes an objection about color and temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the objection that a thing's existence without color does not make it a color, and how he uses the definition of temporality to distinguish it from physical properties like color.

Core Idea
Temporality is about existence after non-existence, not about having a color.

Maturidi addresses a subtle objection that confuses two different kinds of properties: the metaphysical property of being temporally originated (hadath) and the physical property of color. The objector argues that because a thing cannot exist without color, it does not follow that the thing itself is a color. Maturidi shows that this analogy fails because temporality is defined differently.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims that a thing cannot exist without color, but that does not mean the thing is itself a color. Identify the key claim and its apparent logic.
The objection seems to draw an analogy between the relationship of substance to color and substance to temporality.
2
Follow Maturidi's rebuttal
Maturidi defines temporality (al-hadath) as 'existence after non-existence.' He argues that if an accident (like color) has the property of being temporally originated, then any substance that inseparably bears that accident also becomes temporally originated.
This is a crucial move: the judgment of temporality transfers from accident to substance due to inseparability.
3
Distinguish color from temporality
Maturidi explains that color is not the same kind of accident as temporality. Color inheres in a colored thing, but temporality is a description of existence after non-existence. The body never lacks colors as a whole, but it can precede individual colors one after another.
Thus, the objection fails because it confuses the two different meanings.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how Maturidi uses precise definitions to dismantle an analogy. This method is central to kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارض بعض مَن يقول بهذا : بما لا يوجد الشيء بلا لون، ثم لا يجب أن يكون لونًا. وذلك لا معنى له، لما أن الحدث هو وصف الكون بعد أن لم يكن، فإذا وُجِدَ غيره غير مفارقة - وفيه هذا الوصف - لزمَهُ هذا الحكم؛ وليس اللون بلون المعنى يوجد في المتلون به، لذلك اختلفا؛ لكن جملة الجسم إذ لا يخلو من الألوان فلا يسبقها ولكن يسبق واحدا فواحدا، وكذلك أمر الإحداث.
And some who say this objected by [saying] that a thing does not exist without color, but it is not necessary that it be a color. And that has no meaning, because temporality (al-hadath) is the description of existence after non-existence. So when something else exists inseparable from it - and this description is in it - this judgment becomes necessary for it. And color is not by the meaning of color that exists in the thing colored, therefore they differ. But the totality of the body, since it never lacks colors, does not precede them, but it precedes one after another. And likewise is the matter of bringing into existence.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
An objector claims: a thing cannot exist without color, but that does not mean the thing is itself a color. Maturidi replies that this analogy is invalid because temporality (hadath) is a description of existence after non-existence. If something exists inseparably from an accident that has the property of being temporally originated (hadith), then that thing also receives the judgment of temporality. Color, however, is not an accident that implies temporality in the same way; color is a quality that inheres in a colored thing. The body as a whole is never free of colors, so it does not precede colors as a species, but it can precede individual colors. Similarly, the process of bringing into existence (ihdath) applies to bodies through successive accidents.

Key Terms
لون
عرض
حدث
جسم
متلون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a thing always exists with some property, then that property and the thing share the same nature.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this is false: a body always exists with color, but color is not the same as the body. Temporality, however, is a description of existence after non-existence, and if an accident bears that description, the substance bearing the accident also becomes temporally originated.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the objection about color?
A) Because color is not an accident
B) Because temporality is about existence after non-existence, not about color
C) Because bodies have no color
D) Because color is eternal

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's refutation shows that temporality is a metaphysical property defined by existence after non-existence, not by physical properties like color.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth about Allah or His creation because you incorrectly compared it to something you experience in your daily life? What was that comparison, and how does Maturidi’s warning against false analogies challenge you to rethink it?
?
The objector here used the fact that a body cannot be without color to argue that temporality doesn't apply to it. Are there aspects of your faith where you rely on superficial similarities rather than deep definitions? What is one such aspect, and what will you do to correct it?
?
If temporality is simply 'existence after non-existence,' then every moment of your existence is a testimony to your dependence on the Creator. How does this truth change the way you start your day? Do you truly live as if your existence is given to you anew each instant?

The Rational Argument from Conflict Between Multiple Gods
Maturidi presents a rational proof against multiple gods using the impossibility of agreement and the inevitability of conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational proof that the existence of multiple gods would lead to contradiction and corruption, thereby establishing the necessity of tawhid.

Core Idea
Multiple gods cannot coexist because their conflicting wills would destroy the world's order.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise but powerful rational argument for God's oneness. He shows that if there were more than one deity, the world could only exist through their agreement—but such agreement would undermine their absolute sovereignty. Moreover, any two independent wills would inevitably conflict, leading to contradiction and corruption. The fact that the world exists in a coherent order points to a single Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage quietly, then its English translation. Let the argument's flow settle before analyzing it.
Notice the initial 'ب' is a section marker; the proof begins with 'ثم دلالة العقل'.
2
Identify the two sub-arguments
Maturidi gives two reasons why multiple gods are impossible. Find the first one about 'agreement' (اصطلاح) and the second about 'contradiction' (تناقض).
The first is introduced by 'لو كان أكثر من واحد...' and the second by 'ومعنى آخر...'.
3
Analyze each argument
For the first: why does agreement corrupt lordship? For the second: why do contradictory wills prove only one God can exist? Think through the logic.
Focus on the concepts of 'فساد الربوبية' and 'تناقض وتناف'.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Notice how the existence of a coherent world (اتسق تدبيره) serves as evidence. How does the argument prove that the Creator is one?
The conclusion is stated clearly: 'فدل الوجود على أن محدث العالم واحد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب ثم دلالة العقل أنه لو كان أكثر من واحد ما احتمل وجود العالم إلا بالاصطلاح، وفي ذلك فساد الربوبية. ومعنى آخر أن كل شيء يريد أحد ممن ينسب إليه الإلهية إثباته يريد الآخر نفيه، وما يريد أحدهما إيجاده يريد الآخر إعدامه؛ وكذلك في الإبقاء والإفناء؛ وفي ذلك تناقض وتناف. فدل الوجود على أن محدث العالم واحد، فاتسق تدبيره.
B. Then the rational proof: if there were more than one, the existence of the world would not be possible except by mutual agreement, and in that is corruption of lordship. And another meaning: every thing that one of those to whom divinity is attributed wills to affirm, the other wills to negate; and what one wills to bring into existence, the other wills to annihilate; and likewise in preservation and annihilation; and in that is contradiction and opposition. So existence indicates that the originator of the world is one, and His administration is coherent.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for tawhid. The author states that if there were multiple gods, the world could only exist if they agreed, but such agreement would imply imperfection in each. Furthermore, if two gods have opposing wills, then one would want to create something while the other wants to destroy it, leading to contradiction. The fact that the world exists in a coherent order shows that the Creator must be one and His management is consistent.

Key Terms
دلالة العقل
اصطلاح
ربوبية
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple gods could simply cooperate to create a harmonious world.
Correction: Maturidi shows that cooperation would imply each god is not fully independent, which corrupts the concept of absolute divinity. Moreover, cooperation cannot eliminate inherent potential for conflict.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi mean by 'فساد الربوبية' (corruption of lordship) in this argument?
A) That lordship would be destroyed if gods agreed
B) That agreement among multiple gods would imply each is not fully sovereign
C) That the world would be corrupted if gods disagreed
D) That polytheism is inherently corrupt

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's rational proof shows that multiple gods would require agreement and cause conflict, but the world's coherent order proves the Creator is one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the existence of a single, coherent administration in the universe proves that there is only one God, then what does the chaos and contradiction in your own life—when you serve multiple masters like career, reputation, and pleasure—reveal about your actual allegiance? Where today will you submit one decision solely to God to restore coherence?
?
Maturidi argues that multiple gods would inevitably conflict because each wants to affirm what the other negates. Think of the conflicting desires and loyalties within you: how often do you find yourself simultaneously wanting to obey God and to indulge your ego? What is one specific idol in your heart—a relationship, an ambition, a comfort—whose will clashes with God's command? Are you willing to negate that idol's claim today and affirm God's will instead?
?
The proof shows that any attempt at agreement between gods would corrupt the very concept of lordship. Consider your own life: do you try to 'negotiate' with God—bargaining obedience in some areas while preserving your autonomy in others? How does this partial submission corrupt His lordship in your heart? What would it practically mean for you to acknowledge Him as the absolute, undivided Lord over every aspect of your life starting now?
