The Three Views on How the Witnessed Indicates the Unseen
Classification of three opinions on how the shahid indicates the gha'ib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three opinions among theologians on how the witnessed world indicates the unseen divine reality, and why the third opinion is considered strongest.

Core Idea
The witnessed world does not simply mirror the unseen; it points to difference as much as likeness.

Imam al-Maturidi presents a classification of three theological views on how the observed world (shahid) indicates the unseen (gha'ib). This is a foundational issue in arguments for God's existence and attributes. As you read, notice how each view uses analogy differently, and how the third view emphasizes dissimilarity to avoid anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get the overall structure of the three opinions.
Pay attention to the key terms: دلالة, شاهد, غائب, مثل, خلاف.
2
Identify the three opinions
Find each opinion: (a) indication of likeness, (b) temporal limit argument, (c) indication of likeness and difference with emphasis on difference.
The third opinion is the longest and includes a proof.
3
Examine the proof for the third opinion
Analyze how the world's properties (origination, pre-eternity) are not like the world itself, leading to inference of difference (agent, attributes).
Notice the conclusion that the substance does not manifest its like when absent.
4
Reflect on the key terms
Review the Arabic terms: دلالة (indication), شاهد (witnessed/present), غائب (unseen/absent), مثل (likeness), خلاف (difference).
These terms are central to the classification.
5
Connect to broader kalam context
Consider how this classification frames rational arguments about God's nature and avoids anthropomorphism.
This leads into discussions of tawhid and divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم اختلف في وجه دلالة الشاهد على الغائب. فمنهم من يقول : على مثله، إذ هو أصل للذي غاب عنه، ولا يخالف الأصل فرعه؛ مع ما كان طريق معرفة الغائب الشاهد، وقياس الشيء مع نظيره فيه أثبتوا قدم العالم، إذ الشاهد يدل على مثله، فصار الغائب به عالَمًا أيضاً. ثم هو يدل في كل وقت على مثله قبله، وفي ذلك إيجاب القدم للكل . ومنهم من يقول : ما من وقت يُتوهّم فيه ابتداء العالم إلا وقد يتوهم قبله، فيبطل له الغاية. ومنهم من يقول: يدل على المثل والخلاف، ودلالته على الخلاف أوضح؛ لأن من شيئًا من العالم يدله على حدثه أو قدمه، وقدمه أو حدثه ليس هو مثله ولا نظيره؛ ثم يدله على محدثه أو كون بنفسه، وهما خلافه؛ ثم يدله على حكمة فاعله أو سفهه، واختياره أو طبعه، وكل ذلك خلاف لما شاهده ولا يدله على أن له مثلاً، إذ لو كان يدله لكان يجب أن يتوهم كل من عاين نفسه أن يكون كل العالم مثله، وذلك بعيد. فثبت أن الجوهر لا تحقق رؤيته مثله غائبا، وتُحقق أحد الوجوه التي ذكرناها.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Then they differed concerning the manner of indication of the witnessed upon the unseen. Some of them say: upon its likeness, because it is an origin for that which is absent from it, and the branch does not oppose the origin; with the fact that the way of knowing the unseen is the witnessed, and the analogy of a thing to its counterpart—by it they affirmed the pre-eternity of the world, since the witnessed indicates its like, so the unseen also becomes a world by it. Then it indicates in every time a world like it before it, and in that is the necessitation of pre-eternity for everything. And some of them say: there is no time in which the beginning of the world is imagined except that before it is imagined, so the limit is invalidated for it. And some of them say: it indicates both likeness and difference, and its indication of difference is clearer; because from something of the world it indicates its origination or its pre-eternity, and its pre-eternity or origination is not its like nor its counterpart; then it indicates its originator or its existence by itself, and both are contrary to it; then it indicates the wisdom of its agent or his foolishness, and his choice or his nature, and all of that is contrary to what he witnessed, and it does not indicate that it has a likeness, for if it indicated, it would be necessary that everyone who sees himself imagine that the whole world is like him, and that is far. So it is established that the substance does not achieve its being seen as its like when absent, and one of the aspects we mentioned is realized.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage classifies three different opinions on how the witnessed world indicates the unseen (the divine). The first opinion holds that it indicates a likeness, using analogy to argue for the world's eternity. The second opinion argues that every imagined beginning must have a prior, thus limiting is invalid. The third opinion argues that the witnessed indicates both likeness and difference, but indication of difference is clearer. The author then provides a proof for the third view: from the world we infer its origination or pre-eternity, but these are not like the world; similarly, inference leads to the agent, which is contrary to the world. If the world indicated a likeness, then every person would have to imagine that everything is like himself, which is absurd. Therefore, the substance does not manifest its like when absent.

Key Terms
دلالة
شاهد
غائب
مثل
خلاف
قدم
حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third opinion completely denies any analogy between the world and God.
Correction: The third opinion acknowledges both likeness and difference but emphasizes that indication of difference is clearer, and it does not deny analogy entirely.

Checkpoint
According to the third opinion, why does the shahid indicate the opposite rather than the likeness?
A) Because the world is complex
B) Because the world's properties (origination, pre-eternity) are not like the world itself
C) Because the world is eternal
D) Because the world is created

Key Takeaway
The correct view on how the witnessed indicates the unseen is that it indicates both likeness and difference, with difference being clearer and more foundational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world, do you unconsciously project your own traits onto God? How does the third view's insistence on difference challenge you to stop making God in your image?
?
If the world indicates God's difference more than His likeness, how must you adjust your worship and trust? Are you willing to serve a God who is truly 'other'?
?
The passage warns that assuming pure likeness would lead everyone to think the whole world is like themselves. Where in your life have you assumed that your own experiences and limitations apply to God? What will you do today to actively remember His transcendence?

Qualifying the Likeness: From Witnessed to Unseen
Qualification that reporting quality allows recognition but not full likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reporting the quality of a witnessed thing to an absent person indicates similarity but does not prove exact likeness.

Core Idea
Reporting quality reveals likeness, not identity: the unseen is like the witnessed, not identical.

Imam al-Maturidi continues his discussion on analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen. He now introduces a crucial qualification: while reporting the quality of a witnessed thing can inform an absent person about something similar, this does not establish that the unseen is exactly like the witnessed. This distinction preserves the transcendence of Allah while allowing meaningful inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the careful phrasing: 'it is known that it is like him' and 'not that that establishes the likeness'.
The passage uses 'مثله' (like him) not 'هو هو' (identical).
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that if the quality of a witnessed thing is known and reported to an absent person, that person can know that the witnessed thing is similar to something they know—but this does not prove exact likeness.
This is a qualification of analogical reasoning.
3
Analyze the example
Notice the example: knowing a particular body and fire allows recognition of all bodies and fires. This illustrates how quality-based inference works in everyday knowledge.
The example shows limited similarity, not full identity.
4
Connect to the broader discussion
Ask yourself: How does this prevent the claim that the Creator is exactly like creation? The passage safeguards tanzih (transcendence) while allowing some knowledge through attributes.
This lesson builds on the previous one about analogical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن إذا عُرفت كيفية المشاهد، وأخبرت بتلك الكيفية لغائب، عُلمت أنه مثله ، لا أنّ ذلك يحقق المثل، بل قد يجوز أن يدل على مثله بهذا الوجه وبما عرف مثلاً الجسم والنار اللذين شاهدهما، فيعرف كل جسم ونار وإن لم يشهده. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
But if the quality of the witnessed is known, and one informs an absent person of that quality, it is known that it is like him—not that that establishes the likeness; rather, it may indicate its like in this way and by what is known, for example, the body and fire which he witnessed, so he knows every body and fire even if he does not witness them. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage qualifies the previous conclusion. It states that if the specific quality of a witnessed thing is known and reported to someone who has not witnessed it, that person can know that the witnessed thing is similar to his own experience—but this does not prove that the unseen is exactly like the witnessed. For example, knowing a particular body and fire allows one to recognize other bodies and fires. However, this is not the same as claiming that the world as a whole indicates a Creator who is similar to it.

Key Terms
كيفية
مشاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reporting the quality of a witnessed thing to an absent person proves that the unseen thing is exactly identical to the witnessed thing.
Correction: The passage clarifies that such reporting only allows recognition of similarity, not exact likeness. The unseen may be like the witnessed in some way but is not identical.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does reporting the quality of a witnessed thing to an absent person establish?
A) Exact identity between the witnessed and the unseen
B) That the unseen is like the witnessed, but not that it is exactly the same
C) That the unseen cannot be known at all
D) That only fire and body can be known

Key Takeaway
Reporting the quality of a witnessed thing allows an absent person to recognize similarity, but not to claim exact likeness with the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes like His hearing or sight, do you catch yourself assuming they are exactly like your own? Which attribute do you most struggle to avoid likening to creation?
?
The passage ends with 'There is no power except with Allah.' Where in your life have you been relying on your own analogies about the unseen rather than on Allah's guidance? How can you shift that reliance today?
?
If you describe Paradise or Hell to someone, do you speak as if you have witnessed them fully? How can you keep the balance of reporting what is known without claiming exact likeness?

Impossibility of imagining temporal things as eternal
Maturidi refutes the imagination of eternity for temporal things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that imagining temporal things as eternal does not make them possible in eternity, and how this refutes those who claim the world is eternal.

Core Idea
Imagination does not make the impossible possible.

In this brief but powerful passage, Imam Maturidi tackles a common fallacy: the idea that because we can imagine something, it might be possible. He gives concrete examples to show that imagining a temporal thing as eternal is logically absurd, and uses this to argue against the notion of an eternal world. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the examples Maturidi gives.
2
Identify the analogy
Maturidi uses an analogy between imagining an old man as eternal and imagining a temporal event in eternity. Find the key sentence that makes this comparison.
Look for 'فإن قلت: ذَا محال، فمثله كون الحدث في الأزل محال.'
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that just as it is impossible for a temporal event to occur in eternity, so it is impossible for the world to be eternal. This refutes the imagination of the eternity of the world.
The phrase 'والله الموفق' is a prayer for divine guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذُكر من التوهم فإنه قد يُتوهّم في كل شيخ في أوّل ما شاخ بقدمه، وفي كل مولود بقدمه ، وفي كل من أتى مكانًا بقدمه ، ولم يجب به الوصف في الأزل، وكذا في كل حركة وسكون وتفرق واجتماع. فإن قلت: ذَا محال، فمثله كون الحدث في الأزل محال. والله الموفق.
And what was mentioned of imagination: indeed, one might imagine in every old man at the beginning of his old age that he is by his own eternity, and in every newborn that he is by his own eternity, and in everyone who comes to a place that he is by his own eternity, but the description in eternity is not necessitated thereby. And likewise for every motion and stillness and dispersal and harmony. For if you say: that is impossible, then likewise the occurrence of a temporal event in eternity is impossible. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is an argument against those who imagine that temporal things could be eternal. Maturidi gives examples: one might imagine an old man as being eternally old, or a newborn as eternally newborn, or someone arriving at a place as eternally arriving. But this imagining does not make it true in eternity. Similarly, one might imagine motion, stillness, dispersal, or harmony as eternal. If one objects that such imagination is impossible, Maturidi says that it is equally impossible to imagine a temporal event (such as the creation of the world) occurring in eternity. This is an argument by analogy: just as it is absurd to say that a temporal thing like old age or birth is eternal, so it is absurd to say that the world's creation is eternal. The phrase 'والله الموفق' is a prayer.

Key Terms
التوهم
الشيخ
المولود
الحركة
السكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that because we can imagine something, it is possible in reality.
Correction: Maturidi shows that imagination does not confer actual possibility, especially in matters of eternity. Imagining an old man as eternal does not make him so.

Checkpoint
What is the main analogy in this passage?
A) The analogy of a natural cause and its effect
B) The analogy of imagining an old man as eternal and imagining a temporal event in eternity
C) The analogy of light and darkness
D) The analogy of knowledge and will

Key Takeaway
Imagination does not make the impossible possible; temporal things cannot be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the example of an old man imagined as eternal. What 'old man' in your life—a comfortable habit, a status, or a possession—have you been imagining as permanent? What step can you take today to detach from it?
?
If you truly internalized that nothing temporal can be eternal, how would your daily priorities and worries change? Is there a specific attachment you need to break because of this truth?
?
Maturidi's argument shows that imagination does not make the impossible possible. In what area of your faith are you most guilty of living as if imagination could override reality? What would repentance look like in that area?

The Logical Contradiction in the Objector's Stance
Maturidi points out the logical contradiction in the objector's stance regarding 'thing' and 'body'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi exposes a logical contradiction when the objector attempts to apply the term 'body' to Allah while also affirming that He is a 'thing not like things'.

Core Idea
Claiming both 'thing not like things' and 'body not like bodies' leads to a contradiction.

Maturidi concludes his argument by showing that the objector's own position is internally inconsistent. The objector tries to have it both ways: he agrees that Allah is a 'thing unlike things', but then asks why we should not say 'body unlike bodies'. Maturidi demonstrates that if we accept the second phrase, the first becomes meaningless. Let's examine how this contradiction arises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the brevity and directness of the final refutation.
The passage is the conclusion of a longer discussion.
2
Identify the two positions
The objector first accepts 'thing not like things' but then proposes 'body not like bodies'. Notice how Maturidi shows that saying 'body' changes the meaning of the original phrase.
The contradiction hinges on the relationship between 'thing' and 'body'.
3
Understand the contradiction
If 'body' is said, 'thing not like things' becomes 'thing not like bodies', which is a category error because 'thing' includes 'body'. The objector cannot consistently hold both.
This demonstrates the logical incoherence of the objector's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المعارضة عند التحصيل يتناقض؛ لأنه قال: إذ قلتم: شيء لا كالأشياء لم لا قلتم: جسم لا كالأجسام؟ فإذا قلنا: جسم، يصير قولنا: «شيء لا كالأشياء» هو «شيء لا كأجسام».
Then the objection, upon analysis, contradicts itself; because he said: Since you say 'thing not like things', why do you not say 'body not like bodies'? So if we say 'body', our statement 'thing not like things' becomes 'thing not like bodies'.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This brief passage highlights a logical contradiction. The objector's own position is that Allah is a 'thing not like things'. But if he insists that Allah can also be called 'body not like bodies', then the meaning of his earlier phrase collapses: 'thing not like things' would reduce to 'thing not like bodies', which is a category mistake and inconsistent with his earlier affirmation. The passage is a final refutation showing that the objector cannot consistently maintain both statements.

Key Terms
معارضة (muʿāraḍa)
تناقض (tanāquḍ)
تحصيل (taḥṣīl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that both phrases 'thing not like things' and 'body not like bodies' can be used simultaneously without contradiction.
Correction: Maturidi shows they are semantically incompatible: if 'body' is affirmed, the original phrase 'thing not like things' reduces to 'thing not like bodies', which is a category error and inconsistent with the initial concession.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does Maturidi point out in the objector's argument?
A) The objector changes his position midway
B) If 'body' is said, then 'thing not like things' becomes 'thing not like bodies', which is inconsistent because 'thing' includes 'body'
C) The objector uses wrong grammar
D) There is no contradiction

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that attempting to combine 'thing not like things' with 'body not like bodies' creates a logical contradiction that undermines the objector's own stance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector tried to force an analogy between 'thing' and 'body' to argue against divine transcendence. Do you ever find yourself imagining Allah by comparing Him to created things, even though you know He is incomparable? What is the harm in such comparisons, and how can you train your mind to avoid them?
?
This lesson reveals a subtle logical trap: affirming 'thing not like things' while considering 'body not like bodies' leads to contradiction. Have you ever held two beliefs about Allah that, upon honest reflection, pull in opposite directions? What are they, and what step will you take to resolve them today?
?
Maturidi’s refutation shows that consistency in our statements about Allah is not just intellectual but a matter of true tawhid. Are you willing to examine your own understanding of Allah’s attributes for hidden contradictions, even if it means letting go of comfortable but flawed ideas? What is one specific belief you will check and correct right now?

Proof that the World is by Choice and Establishment of Power and Will
Maturidi argues that the world's order indicates it is by God's choice, then derives power and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues from the custom of supplication to prove that the world is by God's choice, and how that leads to establishing divine power and will.

Core Idea
Supplication proves the world is by choice, not compulsion.

Maturidi begins with a universal human experience: when in distress, people turn to God in supplication. This instinctive act, he argues, reveals something profound about the nature of the world—it is not self-caused but shaped by God's deliberate choice. Through this observable reality, we can infer God's power and will.

Guided Path
1
Observe the universal custom of supplication
Read the opening lines noting the inherited custom of calling upon Allah for relief and acknowledging His interventions. This sets the stage for the argument.
2
Identify the conclusion about choice
Follow how Maturidi moves from these observations to the conclusion that the world is by God's choice (ikhtiyār). Notice the phrase 'دل ذلك على أن العالم باختياره'.
3
Trace the derivation of power and will
Once choice is established, Maturidi shows that it necessitates power and will. See how he argues that God's actions are by power and choice, and that this is the basis for knowing the unseen from the witnessed.
He also clarifies that power here means the ability to create with order, not chaotic force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك وتعالى مع ما جرى التعارف المتوارث من الخلق بالدعوات والتضرع إلى الله تعالى بالفرج، وأنه قهر كذا ونصر كذا، وأعان فلانًا وخذل فلانًا، وأن كل ذي قوة يفعل بقوة أنشأها. ولا ينال شيء من ذلك بالمضطر، ولا يرغب فيه؛ دل ذلك على أن العالم باختياره. فإذا ثبت الاختيار ثبتت له القدرة على الخلق والإرادة، لكونه على ما هو عليه؛ لا قدرة له يخرج الذي يكون منه مضطربا فاسدا، ولا يملك الشيء وضده. فثبت أن ما كان منه، بقدرة كان واختيار؛ وذلك أمارات الفعل الحقيقية في الشاهد الذي هو أصل للعلم بالغائب. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
That and Exalted is He. With what has been the inherited mutual custom of creation of supplications and earnest entreaty to Allah the Exalted for relief, and that He subdued such-and-such and aided such-and-such, and helped so-and-so and abandoned so-and-so, and that every possessor of power acts with a power He originated. And nothing of that is attained by the compelled, nor is it desired; that indicates that the world is by His choice. So when choice is established, His power over creation and will are established, because He is upon what He is; He has no power that produces what would be disordered and corrupt from Him, nor does He possess a thing and its opposite. So it is established that what is from Him is by power and choice; and that are the true signs of action in the witnessed realm, which is the basis for knowledge of the unseen. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the witnessed custom of supplication and divine intervention to prove that the world is by God's choice, and then derives divine power and will. Maturidi begins by observing the inherited custom among people of making supplications and earnest prayers to Allah for relief, acknowledging that He subdues enemies, aids allies, helps some, and abandons others. He notes that every being with power acts by a power that God has originated in it. These actions cannot be attained by someone compelled or forced, nor are they desired by such a person. From this, he concludes that the world is by God's choice (ikhtiyār). Once choice is established, divine power (qudra) over creation and will (irāda) are also established, because God is upon His attribute of being able to act with order and wisdom. He clarifies that if God had no power in the sense of producing disordered and corrupt outcomes, then He would not possess a thing and its opposite. Therefore, whatever comes from God is by power and choice. These are the true marks of action in the witnessed realm (shāhid), which serves as the basis for inferring knowledge of the unseen (ghā'ib).

Key Terms
اختيار (ikhtiyār)
قدرة (qudra)
إرادة (irāda)
شاهد (shāhid)
غائب (ghā'ib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the argument from supplication implies God changes His mind in response to prayers.
Correction: Maturidi's argument does not suggest change in God; rather, it shows that the world is consistently governed by God's deliberate choice, power, and will.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the custom of supplication and divine intervention indicate?
A) That God is compelled to respond
B) That the world is by God's choice
C) That people are free to create their own actions
D) That God has no power over creation

Key Takeaway
The universal human practice of supplication reveals that the world is not random but created by God's deliberate choice, power, and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you genuinely expect that your supplication is met by a God who acts by deliberate choice? Or do you sometimes feel it's a shot in the dark? How would your prayer life change if you truly internalized that every response is by His perfect power and will?
?
The passage says every power you have is a power that Allah originated. If you truly believed that your intelligence, strength, and influence come only from Him, would you still be proud? What would you need to let go of to remain humble?
?
If the world is by God's choice, then the hard things you are facing right now are not random. Are you willing to accept that your current trials are part of His deliberate plan, and what would it mean to submit to that choice today?

The Mu'tazila Proof and Its Dilemma
Maturidi presents and initially refutes the Mu'tazila proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument for the world's creation and how Maturidi exposes a fatal flaw: the same logic would prove God created.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila proof for creation applies to God Himself.

Maturidi begins by presenting the Mu'tazila proof that the world, being subject to accidents, must have a producer. He then highlights a critical inconsistency: the same reasoning, when applied to God's attributes like Creator or Merciful, would imply that God too underwent change—thus becoming created. This sets up his fuller refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how Maturidi first states the Mu'tazila argument, then immediately pivots to its implication for God.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فصيّروه في أول أحوال...' – it's the turning point.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila premise
Find the exact reason the Mu'tazila give for the world's creation: it is not free of a producer because it is not free of accidents.
3
Recognize Maturidi's critique
Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila themselves treat God's attributes (Creator, Merciful) as temporal events, so the same cause (non-emptiness from events) would make God created. See if you can trace this reasoning.
The phrase 'فصار السبب...' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدلت المعتزلة وغيرهم على حدث العالم بما لا يخلو عن محدث؛ ولم يكن دليلهم على سوى وجود العالم، فأوجبوا حدثه وقالوا في ذلك بمحدث. ثم قالت المعتزلة في الله سبحانه: أن كان غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا رحيم، وهو اليوم كذلك. فصيّروه في أول أحوال ما وقع للخلق به العلم غير خال عن الحوادث، كالعالم الذي وجدوه بالحس غير خال عنها؛ فصار السبب الذي عرفوا حدث العالم به هو الذي به عرفوا حدث الخالق الرحمن. والله قديم لم يزل. ثم بعد هذا وجهان أحدهما أنه إذا لزم القول في جملة العالم بالحدث - وإن لم نشهده بوجودنا ما شهدنا منه غير خالٍ من الأحداث للزم ذلك في الصانع الخالق، لوجودنا له ما به نُسمّيه حدثًا. والثاني أنه قد وجب قدم ذاته، مع ما لا يُعلم وجوده إلا بحوادث، لم لا وجب القول بقدم جملة العالم وإن كان غير خالٍ عن الحوادث؟ وبعد، فإن معرفة احتمال الحوادث فيما لا يُحس من العالم بما أضيف إليه من الاجتماع والافتراق والتحرك والسكون. فالله على قولهم يضاف إليه الرحمة والصنع والإبداع والإعادة، وكل هذا عندهم حوادث، فيجب القول فيه بما يجب في العالم؛ ويكون لمن هو بهذا الوصف خالق صانع خارج من ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the Mu'tazila and others argued for the creation (ḥudūth) of the world on the basis of what is not free of a producer (muḥdith); and their proof was only the existence of the world, so they necessitated its creation and spoke in that of a producer. Then the Mu'tazila said about Allah the Exalted: that He was not a creator nor merciful nor compassionate, and He is today thus. So they made Him in the first of states that occurred to creation by knowledge not free of accidents (ḥawādith), like the world which they found by sense not free of them; thus the cause by which they knew the creation of the world became that by which they knew the creation of the Creator, the Merciful. And Allah is eternal, never ceasing. Then after this are two aspects: one is that if the statement about the entire world being created is necessary – even if we do not witness it by our existence, what we have witnessed from it is not free of events – then that is necessary for the Maker, the Creator, because we exist for Him that by which we call it an event. The second is that His essence has been necessitated as eternal, along with what is not known except by events, why should not the statement about the entire world being eternal be necessary even if it is not free of events? And further, the knowledge of the possibility of events in what is not sensed of the world, by what is attributed to it of composition and separation and motion and rest. For Allah, according to them, are attributed mercy, making, origination, and return, and all this according to them are events, so the statement about Him must be like what is necessary in the world; and whoever is with this description is a creator, a maker, outside of that. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a complex refutation of the Mu'tazila argument for the createdness of the world. Maturidi first states their argument: the world cannot be free of a producer (muḥdith) because it is not free of accidents (ḥawādith). But he notes that their proof only relies on the existence of the world, not on any specific observation. Then he quotes their claim that God in pre-eternity was not Creator, Merciful, or Compassionate, and is so now. This implies that God's attributes are temporal events (ḥawādith). Maturidi then points out the contradiction: the same reasoning they use to prove the world's creation would prove that God is also created, because they consider His attributes as events. He then gives two detailed aspects (wajhān): First, if the world's non-emptiness from events proves its creation, then God's attributes (which are also events) would prove His creation. Second, if God's essence is eternal despite being known only through events, then why cannot the world be eternal despite being full of events? He concludes that the Mu'tazila, by attributing events to God such as mercy, creation, and origination, are forced to apply the same judgment to God as to the world, which is absurd.

Key Terms
حدث
حوادث
محدث
صانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila consistently argued that God has no attributes.
Correction: This passage shows they did attribute temporal attributes to God (Creator, Merciful), which Maturidi uses against them.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the flaw in the Mu'tazila proof?
A) The world has no accidents
B) The same reasoning would prove God created
C) The Mu'tazila did not believe in a creator
D) The world is eternal

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argument for the world's creation, when applied consistently, undermines God's eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila let their logic lead them to say that God was not merciful before creation. Have you ever let your own reasoning diminish your conviction about God's eternal attributes? What attribute of God do you need to reaffirm as eternal today?
?
This passage shows how even a small misstep in reasoning about God can lead to serious contradiction. In your own life, where might you be applying worldly patterns of thought to Allah, forgetting that He is beyond time and change? What practical step will you take this week to remind yourself that Allah is al-Qadim, the Eternal?
?
The Mu'tazila's error was to treat God's attributes as if they were created events. Do you ever act as if God's mercy or justice are conditional on your actions, as if they begin and end? How can you live today with a deeper trust in His unchanging attributes?

Maturidi's Twofold Refutation
Maturidi presents two detailed aspects against the Mu'tazila and concludes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two specific aspects Maturidi uses to refute the Mu'tazila argument and the conclusion he draws.

Core Idea
If God is eternal though known by events, the world can be eternal too.

Having pointed out the basic inconsistency, Maturidi now deepens his refutation with two detailed aspects (wajhān). The first argues that if the world's non-emptiness from events proves its creation, then God's attributes—also events—would prove His creation. The second turns the argument around: if God's essence is eternal despite being known only through events, why can't the world be eternal? He concludes that the Mu'tazila are forced to apply the same judgment to God as to the world, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the second half
Read from 'ثم بعد هذا وجهان' to the end. The translation will help. Note that Maturidi presents two distinct arguments.
These are labeled 'one' and 'two' in the translation.
2
Analyze the first aspect
The first aspect: if the world's being filled with events proves it must be created, then God, who has events (attributes), must also be created. See how Maturidi states this.
The phrase 'للزم ذلك في الصانع' is key.
3
Analyze the second aspect
The second aspect: God's essence is eternal, yet we come to know Him through temporal events (like creation). So why can't the world be eternal despite containing events? This is a direct rebuttal of the Mu'tazila premise.
Look for 'لم لا وجب القول بقدم جملة العالم'.
4
Consider the conclusion
Maturidi ends by noting that the Mu'tazila, by attributing events like mercy and creation to God, force themselves to judge God as they judge the world. This is an absurdity, showing their proof fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدلت المعتزلة وغيرهم على حدث العالم بما لا يخلو عن محدث؛ ولم يكن دليلهم على سوى وجود العالم، فأوجبوا حدثه وقالوا في ذلك بمحدث. ثم قالت المعتزلة في الله سبحانه: أن كان غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا رحيم، وهو اليوم كذلك. فصيّروه في أول أحوال ما وقع للخلق به العلم غير خال عن الحوادث، كالعالم الذي وجدوه بالحس غير خال عنها؛ فصار السبب الذي عرفوا حدث العالم به هو الذي به عرفوا حدث الخالق الرحمن. والله قديم لم يزل. ثم بعد هذا وجهان أحدهما أنه إذا لزم القول في جملة العالم بالحدث - وإن لم نشهده بوجودنا ما شهدنا منه غير خالٍ من الأحداث للزم ذلك في الصانع الخالق، لوجودنا له ما به نُسمّيه حدثًا. والثاني أنه قد وجب قدم ذاته، مع ما لا يُعلم وجوده إلا بحوادث، لم لا وجب القول بقدم جملة العالم وإن كان غير خالٍ عن الحوادث؟ وبعد، فإن معرفة احتمال الحوادث فيما لا يُحس من العالم بما أضيف إليه من الاجتماع والافتراق والتحرك والسكون. فالله على قولهم يضاف إليه الرحمة والصنع والإبداع والإعادة، وكل هذا عندهم حوادث، فيجب القول فيه بما يجب في العالم؛ ويكون لمن هو بهذا الوصف خالق صانع خارج من ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the Mu'tazila and others argued for the creation (ḥudūth) of the world on the basis of what is not free of a producer (muḥdith); and their proof was only the existence of the world, so they necessitated its creation and spoke in that of a producer. Then the Mu'tazila said about Allah the Exalted: that He was not a creator nor merciful nor compassionate, and He is today thus. So they made Him in the first of states that occurred to creation by knowledge not free of accidents (ḥawādith), like the world which they found by sense not free of them; thus the cause by which they knew the creation of the world became that by which they knew the creation of the Creator, the Merciful. And Allah is eternal, never ceasing. Then after this are two aspects: one is that if the statement about the entire world being created is necessary – even if we do not witness it by our existence, what we have witnessed from it is not free of events – then that is necessary for the Maker, the Creator, because we exist for Him that by which we call it an event. The second is that His essence has been necessitated as eternal, along with what is not known except by events, why should not the statement about the entire world being eternal be necessary even if it is not free of events? And further, the knowledge of the possibility of events in what is not sensed of the world, by what is attributed to it of composition and separation and motion and rest. For Allah, according to them, are attributed mercy, making, origination, and return, and all this according to them are events, so the statement about Him must be like what is necessary in the world; and whoever is with this description is a creator, a maker, outside of that. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a complex refutation of the Mu'tazila argument for the createdness of the world. Maturidi first states their argument: the world cannot be free of a producer (muḥdith) because it is not free of accidents (ḥawādith). But he notes that their proof only relies on the existence of the world, not on any specific observation. Then he quotes their claim that God in pre-eternity was not Creator, Merciful, or Compassionate, and is so now. This implies that God's attributes are temporal events (ḥawādith). Maturidi then points out the contradiction: the same reasoning they use to prove the world's creation would prove that God is also created, because they consider His attributes as events. He then gives two detailed aspects (wajhān): First, if the world's non-emptiness from events proves its creation, then God's attributes (which are also events) would prove His creation. Second, if God's essence is eternal despite being known only through events, then why cannot the world be eternal despite being full of events? He concludes that the Mu'tazila, by attributing events to God such as mercy, creation, and origination, are forced to apply the same judgment to God as to the world, which is absurd.

Key Terms
قدم
إبداع
إعادة
اجتماع
افتراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Maturidi is arguing for the world's eternity.
Correction: He is not; he is using a hypothetical to show the Mu'tazila's argument is self-defeating. He believes the world is created, but by a different proof.

Checkpoint
In the second aspect, what does Maturidi say about God's essence?
A) It is created
B) It is eternal despite being known through events
C) It has no attributes
D) It is subject to change

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's two aspects show that the Mu'tazila argument, if consistent, either creates a contradiction about God or fails to prove the world's creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute actions to God like 'creating' or 'providing', do you ever subconsciously think of them as temporal events that would imply change in God? How does this lesson confront that assumption personally?
?
The Mu'tazila's logic would force them to consider God as created because they see His acts as events. Are there areas in your own understanding of God where your reasoning leads to an inconsistency you haven't faced? What will you do to resolve it?
?
Maturidi ends with 'And there is no power except with Allah.' In what specific way does this lesson reveal your own need for divine assistance to hold correct belief without falling into false analogies? Commit to one practical step to seek that help.

The Rational Necessity of Command and Prohibition
Maturidi enumerates proofs for the necessity of command and prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational proof for the necessity of divine command and prohibition, grounded in the knowledge of God, rational ethics, and the wisdom of creation.

Core Idea
Command and prohibition are necessary for humans to fulfill the purpose of creation.

Maturidi begins by linking the evidence for command and prohibition to the very knowledge of the Commander. Since God distinguished humans with reason and the capacity to know Him, neglecting them without guidance would contradict divine wisdom. This lesson explores that foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the first half of Maturidi's argument focusing on command and prohibition. Let the author's own sequence guide you through the three primary proofs.
Pay attention to the logical flow from knowledge to wisdom.
2
Identify the three proofs
Find the three distinct arguments: (1) from knowledge of the Commander, (2) from rational ethics, and (3) from divine wisdom and non-futility of creation. Notice how each builds on the previous.
3
Reflect on the consequence of removal
Consider Maturidi's point that removing obligation would make creation futile and end in mere annihilation. Ask yourself why this would be unworthy of a wise Creator.
This connects to the broader theme of wisdom in creation.
4
Summarize the necessity
In your own words, state why command and prohibition are necessary according to Maturidi.
You can write a short paragraph.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ودليل الأمر عندنا والنهي معرفة الآمر والناهي؛ إذ خص الله البشر من بين البهائم في تعرف ذلك، لم يُحتمل إهمالهم عن ذلك، كما لا يحتمل شيء مما فيه النفع إهماله عنه . وبما في العقل حُسن كل حَسَن وقبح كل قبيح، ثم في الفعل يقبح فعل القبيح، ويحسن فعل الحسن، فلزم الأمر والنهي لمكان ما به الأمر والنهي. ولأن الله خلق خلقا يدل على وحدانيته وحكمته، فلم يجز إخلاء الخلق عن معرفة ذلك، فيصير خلقه عبثًا. ولما في رفع الكلفة زوال حكمة الخلقة، إذ حصلت للفناء ، وكل بان شيئًا للنقض لا غير فهو عابت غير حكيم. ثم الوعد والوعيد للترغيب والترهيب؛ إذ لولا ذلك يذهب نفع الائتمار وضرر العصيان، ولم يكن لمن خُلق في فعلهم نفع . فإذا لم يكن للمؤتمر نفع ولا للعاصي ضرر يبطل معنى الأمر والنهي، إذ ليس لنفع الآمر والناهي ; فلذلك لزم الوعد والوعيد في الحكمة . مع ما في الأمر والنهي مجاهدة النفس وحملها على ما يكرهه الطبع، والذي يكرهه تنفر عنه النفس فلا يجد الممتحن على قهرها وصرفها إلى ما يريده ويؤمر به سبيلاً إلا بإحضار الوعد والوعيد، حتى إذا رأى ذلك سهل عليه ترك الملاذ، وهان عليه تحمل المؤَنِ العظام. وبعد، فإن البشر خُلق خلقا قبح عليه فعل الذي لا يُقصد به نفع العواقب، أو لا يُتَّقَى به ضرر العواقب، فلا بد أن يجعل لأعماله ذلك، وذلك حق الوعد والوعيد. ولولا ذلك لكان يستوى عواقب العدو والولي، وعلى ما تفاوتا هما بحيث الاختيار والإيثار، يجب تفاوت عواقبهما. وبالله التوفيق.
Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: The evidence for command and prohibition in our view is the knowledge of the commander and prohibitor; for God distinguished humans from beasts in recognizing that, it cannot be tolerated that they be neglected from that, just as nothing that contains benefit can be neglected concerning it. And because in the intellect there is the goodness of every good and the badness of every bad, then in action it is bad to do the bad, and good to do the good, so command and prohibition become necessary because of that which necessitates them. And because God created a creation that indicates His oneness and wisdom, it is not permissible to leave creation without knowledge of that, for then His creation would become futile. And because the removal of obligation would cause the disappearance of the wisdom of creation, since it would end in annihilation, and everything that is established for dissolution only is the act of one who is deficient, not wise. Then promise and threat are for encouragement and deterrence; for without that the benefit of obedience and the harm of disobedience would be lost, and there would be no benefit for those created in their actions. If there is no benefit for the obedient and no harm for the disobedient, the meaning of command and prohibition is nullified, since they are not for the benefit of the commander and prohibitor. Therefore, promise and threat are necessary in wisdom. Along with that, in command and prohibition there is struggle of the soul and forcing it to what nature dislikes, and what it dislikes the soul recoils from, so the one tested cannot find a way to overcome it and turn it to what he wants and is commanded except by presenting promise and threat, until when he sees that, leaving pleasures becomes easy for him, and bearing great hardships becomes light. Moreover, humans are created in such a way that it is bad for them to do an action that does not aim at benefit in consequences or that does not avoid harm in consequences, so it is necessary that their actions be assigned that, and that is the right of promise and threat. Without that, the outcomes of the enemy and the friend would be equal, and since they differ in terms of choice and preference, their outcomes must differ. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a systematic proof for the necessity of command, prohibition, reward, and punishment, given by Maturidi himself. He begins by stating that the evidence (دليل) for command and prohibition is knowledge of the Commander and Prohibitor. God distinguished humans from animals by granting them the ability to recognize Him; therefore, it is impermissible to neglect them without guidance. Just as anything beneficial cannot be neglected, so too human guidance is necessary. Then he appeals to rational ethics: the intellect perceives good and bad, and in action, doing bad is bad and doing good is good. Thus, command and prohibition are necessary because of this moral distinction. Next, he argues from divine wisdom: God created a creation that points to His oneness and wisdom, so it cannot be that He leaves them ignorant of that, otherwise creation would be futile. Removing obligation (رفع الكلفة) would nullify the wisdom of creation, leading to annihilation—something unworthy of a wise Creator. Then he turns to reward and punishment: without promise and threat, the benefit of obedience and harm of disobedience would be lost, and there would be no benefit for the created beings in their actions. If obedience yields no benefit and disobedience no harm, the very meaning of command and prohibition is invalidated, since they are not for God's benefit. Thus reward and punishment are wisdom-based. Additionally, command and prohibition involve struggling against the soul's natural inclinations; the soul recoils from what it dislikes, so the moral agent cannot overcome it except by the motivation of promise and threat. Once these are presented, leaving pleasures and bearing hardships become easier. Finally, humans are naturally averse to actions with no beneficial consequence or no avoidance of harmful consequence; so actions must have consequences. Otherwise, the outcomes of the enemy (disobedient) and the friend (obedient) would be equal. Since they choose differently, their outcomes must differ. The passage ends with 'And God is the grantor of success.'

Key Terms
دليل الأمر والنهي
المعرفة
العقل
الحكمة
الكلفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen if obligation (الكلفة) were removed entirely?
A) Humans would become more virtuous.
B) Creation would become futile and aimless.
C) God's power would be diminished.
D) Reason alone would suffice for guidance.

Key Takeaway
Command and prohibition are rationally and teleologically necessary: they stem from the knowledge of God, rational ethics, and the wise purpose of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that divine commands are rationally necessary for life to have meaning, how would your attitude toward obeying them change starting today?
?
Consider something you know is wrong but still do. How does Maturidi's argument that the intellect perceives badness as bad demand an immediate change from you, not just intellectual assent?
?
What does it reveal about your relationship with Allah if you view His commands as burdens rather than the very structure that gives your life wisdom and purpose? What one step can you take tonight to shift that perspective?

Rational Judgments

The Wisdom of Reward and Punishment
Maturidi argues for the necessity of reward and punishment from psychological and consequentialist angles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reward and punishment are necessary in divine wisdom, based on human psychology and the nature of actions.

Core Idea
Without reward and punishment, command and prohibition lose their meaning and efficacy.

Having established the necessity of command and prohibition, Maturidi now turns to the role of promise and threat. He argues that without the motivation of reward and punishment, obedience and disobedience would have no consequences, rendering the very act of commanding pointless. This lesson unpacks that argument, including the psychological struggle and the need for differential outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on promise and threat
Read the second half of Maturidi's argument, focusing on reward and punishment. Notice how he connects them to the effectiveness of command and prohibition.
2
Examine the psychological argument
Maturidi explains that command and prohibition involve struggling against the soul; only the motivation of reward and punishment enables one to overcome natural inclinations. Reflect on this insight.
Consider how this relates to everyday moral experience.
3
Understand the consequentialist reasoning
Humans naturally consider outcomes; actions without beneficial or harmful consequences are deemed bad. Therefore, actions must have differential consequences for obedience and disobedience.
This leads to the conclusion that reward and punishment are wise.
4
Contrast with equal outcomes
Maturidi argues that without differential reward and punishment, the outcomes of the obedient and disobedient would be equal, which is absurd given their different choices. Appreciate the force of this reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen if there were no promise and threat for command and prohibition?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you genuinely feel the weight of divine threat? Or do you act as if consequences are distant? Maturidi says without threat, commands lose meaning. Examine your heart: do you truly believe God's warnings? This week, before every action, pause and ask: 'Would I do this if I fully believed in the reward and punishment?'
?
You know that leaving a pleasure for God's sake is hard. The passage says reward and punishment make it easy. When was the last time you felt that ease? If not, are you really engaging with the promise and threat? Commit today to memorize one verse about paradise and one about hell, and recite them daily for a month to reignite that motivation.
?
If you were judged right now, would your outcomes differ from those who openly disobey? The passage says they must, given your choices. But do your actions truly reflect a different outcome? Are you living as if your reward is guaranteed? Repent of any complacency and take one concrete step this evening to act on a command you have been neglecting.

Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
The Rational Proof for Divine Command and Prohibition
Maturidi argues that intellect's recognition of good/evil necessitates divine commands/prohibitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the intellect's recognition of good and evil to prove the necessity of divine commands and prohibitions.

Core Idea
The intellect's judgment of good and evil requires a Divine Commander.

Imam Maturidi presents a purely rational argument for why humans cannot be left without divine guidance. He begins by noting that Allah distinguished humans from animals with the capacity to know the Commander. Then he shows that the very nature of good and evil as perceived by the intellect necessitates that some actions be commanded and others prohibited. This short but dense passage lays the foundation for a key principle in Maturidi theology: the moral law is knowable by reason.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Maturidi states that the proof for command and prohibition is knowledge of the Commander. He argues that humans are distinct from animals in having this capacity, so they cannot be neglected.
Note the phrase 'knowledge of the Commander' could refer to rational knowledge of God.
2
Identify the rational judgment
Next, he says that in the intellect, there is goodness of every good act and ugliness of every ugly act. This is the concept of al-husn wa al-qubh al-aqli (rational good and evil).
3
Connect judgment to action
Then he applies this: in action, performing the ugly act is ugly and performing the good act is good. So there is an objective moral quality to actions.
4
Derive the necessity
Because actions have objective moral qualities, it becomes necessary to have commands (to do good) and prohibitions (to avoid evil). This is the conclusion: command and prohibition are necessitated by the very nature of good and evil.
This shows that divine law is not arbitrary but grounded in rational morality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ودليل الأمر عندنا والنهي معرفة الآمر والناهي؛ إذ خص الله البشر من بين البهائم في تعرف ذلك، لم يُحتمل إهمالهم عن ذلك، كما لا يحتمل شيء مما فيه النفع إهماله عنه . وبما في العقل حُسن كل حَسَن وقبح كل قبيح، ثم في الفعل يقبح فعل القبيح، ويحسن فعل الحسن، فلزم الأمر والنهي لمكان ما به الأمر والنهي.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The proof for command and prohibition with us is the knowledge of the commander and the prohibitor. For Allah distinguished humans from among animals in recognizing that; it is not permissible to neglect them regarding that, just as it is not permissible to neglect any matter that contains benefit. And because in the intellect there is the goodness of every good act and the ugliness of every ugly act, then in action the performance of the ugly act is ugly, and the performance of the good act is good. So command and prohibition became necessary on account of that by which command and prohibition exist.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of divine commands and prohibitions. Imam Maturidi begins by stating that the evidence for commands and prohibitions is the knowledge of the Commander and Prohibitor (God). Allah distinguished humans from animals by giving them the capacity to recognize the Commander, so they cannot be neglected in this matter—just as any matter containing benefit cannot be neglected. Then he appeals to the rational judgment of good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh al-aqli): the intellect perceives the goodness of good acts and the ugliness of ugly acts. Therefore, in practice, performing the ugly act is ugly, and performing the good act is good. As a result, commands (to do good) and prohibitions (to avoid evil) are necessary because of the very nature of good and evil that they address.

Key Terms
الأمر والنهي
العقل
حسن وقبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine commands and prohibitions are only known through revelation and have no rational basis.
Correction: Maturidi argues that human reason itself recognizes the goodness and ugliness of actions, necessitating commands and prohibitions from a Commander who knows this.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, what makes command and prohibition necessary?
A) The human need for social order.
B) The intellect's judgment that good acts are good and evil acts are evil.
C) The commands of previous prophets.
D) The consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
The rational recognition of good and evil by the intellect logically requires divine commands to enjoin good and prohibitions to forbid evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You acknowledge that your intellect tells you that lying is ugly and honesty is good. Yet, when you choose to lie, whom are you disregarding? The Commander who commanded truthfulness? How does this make you feel about your accountability to Him?
?
If you truly believed that every good deed is witnessed by the Commander, would you ever abandon a good act out of laziness? What does your laziness reveal about the strength of your conviction?
?
The passage says it is not permissible to neglect humans regarding commands and prohibitions. Do you neglect seeking knowledge of what Allah commands and prohibits? If so, what excuse will you offer to the One who gave you intellect?

Impossibility of all things being eternal
Maturidi argues against universal eternality and explains eternal formation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational proof that not all things can be eternal, and why the attribute of power and action cannot apply to contingent beings.

Core Idea
Eternal power and temporal change are incompatible.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi presents a concise rational argument against the possibility of all things being eternal. He shows that if everything were eternal, then the attribute of power (qudra) and action (fi‘l) would have to be removed from contingent things. Instead, God's formation of things is based on His eternal knowledge, and He is described by an eternal act of formation. The argument concludes by comparing the contingent world to a manufactured object, reinforcing its contingency. Let's examine the text closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if everything were eternal, then...'
2
Understand the conditional argument
Identify the premise (if all eternal) and the conclusion (then power and action would be removed from the contingent). Ask yourself: why does Maturidi say this? The commentary will explain.
3
Grasp the concept of eternal formation (takwīn)
Maturidi distinguishes between the eternal attribute of formation in God and the temporal things formed. Notice how he says formation is based on eternal knowledge.
This is key to understanding how God's action relates to the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان الكل قديما لكان وصف القدرة والفعل يزول عنه في الحادث. بل كان تكوينه أن يكون كل شيء على ما علم أن يكون ؛ ويريد بتكوين لم يزل به موصوفا، إذ هو يتعالى عن اعتراض الحوادث فيه، بما يصير بمعنى العالم الذي دل إحاطة الأحداث به على حدثه". فمثله [صنع] الصانع. والله الموفق.
If everything were eternal, then the description of power (qudra) and action (fi‘l) would be removed from it in the contingent (al-ḥādith). Rather, its formation (takwīn) is that everything is as He knew it would be; and He intends by an eternal formation (takwīn) by which He is described, for He is exalted above the incidence of contingencies in Him, such that it becomes like the world—the encompassing of contingencies indicates its contingency. So it is like the [work] of the maker. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof that not everything can be eternal. Maturidi argues that if all things were eternal (qadīm), then the attribute of power (qudra) and action (fi‘l) would have to be removed from contingent things (ḥādith), because contingent things cannot possess eternal attributes. Rather, God's formation (takwīn) of things is based on His eternal knowledge—everything occurs as He knew it would be. God is described by an eternal act of formation, unaffected by any temporal change. The argument concludes by comparing the contingent world to a manufactured object; just as a work of a maker is contingent, so the world must be contingent. The proof ends with the phrase 'And God is the granter of success' (wa-Allāh al-muwaffiq).

Key Terms
القديم
الحادث
القدرة
الفعل
التكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God is eternal, His actions must also be eternal in the sense that the things He creates are eternal.
Correction: This passage clarifies that only the attribute of power and the act of formation are eternal in God, while the things He forms are contingent and temporal.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, what would happen if all things were eternal?
A) The attribute of power and action would belong to everything.
B) The attribute of power and action would be removed from contingent things.
C) God would not be able to create new things.
D) Everything would become similar to God.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that not all things can be eternal by showing that contingent things cannot possess eternal attributes of power and action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that God's power and formation are eternal, while everything in your life is temporary and contingent. Do you truly live with that awareness, or do you cling to people and possessions as if they will never end? What would it look like to hold them loosely, acknowledging their imminent passing away, and to trust only in the Eternal?
?
Maturidi argues that God is exalted above temporal changes. Yet when you face a crisis, do you find yourself expecting God to react within your timeline, as if He were subject to time? How can you reshape your du'ā' and reliance to honor His transcendence, while still pouring out your heart to Him?
?
The passage teaches that everything occurs exactly as God eternally formed it—including your current trials and blessings. Is there a specific situation in your life where you struggle to accept that God's eternal formation of that event is perfectly wise? What concrete step will you take this week to surrender to His decree without resentment or despair?

Three Outcomes of Reflection on the Senses
Maturidi enumerates three possible outcomes of reflection when a thought arises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible outcomes of reflection when a thought arises, and why reflection is always profitable according to Maturidi.

Core Idea
Reflection always leads to profit—whether to faith, warning, or peace.

Maturidi addresses a potential concern: what if someone reflects and still does not reach faith? He shows that reflection never fails to produce some benefit. In this passage, he enumerates exactly three possible outcomes, each with its own consequence, and concludes that the one who reflects fairly always gains.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to understand the gist.
Notice Maturidi's structured enumeration using 'أما... أو... أو'.
2
Identify the three outcomes
List the three outcomes as described: (a) knowledge leading to piety, (b) denial leading to punishment, (c) suspension of judgment leading to peace.
Each outcome has a specific consequence in this life and the next.
3
Understand Maturidi's conclusion
Read the final sentence: 'in every aspect he is at a profit.' Why does Maturidi say that? Because even denial or agnosticism yields awareness.
This shows the value of reflection regardless of result.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this teaching encourages sincere reflection without fear of failure. The profit is in the process itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كفها عن الوجوه الضارة واستعمالها بالوجوه النافعة، فالعقل والنظر الذي بهما تعرف المنافع والمضار أحق أن لا يُهملا. وما كان الناظر عند ورود الخاطر لا يعدو خصالاً ثلاثة : (أ) إما أن يُفضى به نظره إلى العلم بحدثه وأن له محدثًا يجزيه بالإحسان ويعاقبه بالإساءة، فيجتنب ما يُسخطه ويُقبل على ما يرضيه فيُسعد جده وينال شرف الدارين؛ (ب) أو يفضى به إلى نفي ما ذكرنا فيتمتع بصنوف اللذات . أما العقاب فينتظره في الآخرة؛ (ج) أو يفضى به إلى العلم باستغلاق باب العلم بحقيقة ما دعى إليه، فيستريح قلبه ويزول عنه الوجل الذي يعتريه إذا فزعته الخواطر. فيعلم إذا أنصف أنه على ربح في نظره من كل وجه . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Restrain them from harmful aspects and use them in beneficial aspects. So the intellect and reflection by which benefits and harms are known are more deserving not to be neglected. And what (the observer) when a thought occurs does not exceed three characteristics: (a) Either his reflection leads him to knowledge of its origination and that it has an originator who rewards him for good and punishes him for evil, so he avoids what displeases Him and turns to what pleases Him, thus his fortune is blessed and he attains the honor of the two abodes; (b) Or it leads him to deny what we mentioned, so he enjoys various pleasures—as for punishment, it awaits him in the Hereafter; (c) Or it leads him to the knowledge that the door of knowledge of the reality to which he is called is closed, so his heart rests and the fear that overcomes him when thoughts frighten him disappears. Then he knows, if he is fair, that he is at a profit in his reflection from every aspect. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of the three possible outcomes of reflection when a thought (خاطر) occurs to a person. Maturidi begins by reiterating the importance of restraining the senses from harmful uses and employing them beneficially, emphasizing that intellect and reflection—the tools for discerning benefit and harm—are even more deserving not to be neglected. He then states that the observer (الناظر), upon the arrival of a thought, does not exceed three outcomes. First: reflection leads to knowledge of the origination of the world (حدوثه) and that it has a Creator who rewards and punishes, so the person seeks to please God and attains happiness in this world and the next. Second: reflection leads to denial of God and afterlife, so the person indulges in pleasures, but punishment awaits him in the Hereafter. Third: reflection leads to the conclusion that the truth of the matter is unknowable, so the person's anxiety ceases. In all cases, if the person is fair, he realizes that his reflection is profitable from every angle. The passage ends with the formula 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Key Terms

الخاطر
الخصال الثلاثة
العقل
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reflection can be fruitless or even harmful if it leads away from faith.
Correction: According to Maturidi, reflection is always profitable in some way—whether it leads to faith, warns of consequences, or brings peace.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following is NOT one of the three outcomes of reflection?
A) Knowledge of the world's origination and its Creator, leading to piety and reward.
B) Denial of God and the afterlife, leading to temporary pleasure but ultimate punishment.
C) Certainty of disbelief, leading to eternal damnation.
D) Recognition that the truth is unknowable, leading to peace of mind.

Key Takeaway
Reflection on the senses and thoughts always yields profit: either guidance to faith, a warning of consequences, or peace from agnosticism.

?
Maturidi says the one who denies God enjoys worldly pleasures but punishment awaits. Does your current lifestyle reflect a denial of accountability? If so, what are you willing to change starting today?

Maturidi's Defense of Motion as an Accident
Maturidi defends motion as an accident by answering an objection about knowing its 'how'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi answers the objection that we cannot know motion's 'how' and yet still prove it is an accident through observed temporal succession.

Core Idea
We prove motion's accidental nature not by its essence but by its temporal behavior.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi engages with an objector who challenges the proof that motion is an accident. The objector argues that since we do not know the intrinsic 'how' of motion, we cannot use analogies to classify it. Maturidi's reply distinguishes between knowing the temporal modality of motion and knowing its ontological otherness, grounding the proof in observable succession. This lesson unpacks that dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
First, read the objector's challenge in the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the claim that the 'how' of motion is unknown because it is our own action.
The objector uses the term 'kayfiyya' (how).
2
Examine Maturidi's answer
Read the response: 'Rather, the how of precedence and posteriority which is our action is known...' Identify the two kinds of 'how' being distinguished.
One 'how' is temporal (taqaddum wa ta'akhkhur), the other is essential (ghayriyya).
3
Understand the logical move
Notice that Maturidi appeals to the fact that some people who deny otherness end up affirming temporal change in the body. This shows that even they admit succession.
This is an argument from consequence: denial leads to contradiction.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The final sentence: since temporal succession is established and the body does not precede it, motion's occurrence as an accident is proven.
The argument does not require knowing motion's quiddity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب المعارض له أن كيف هي، إذ هي فعلكم، ولم يعرف كيفية فعله قبله، فلم استدللتم على الحركة بالمقاييس؟ قيل : إنما يعرف أن كيف التقدم والتأخرُ الذي هو فعلنا ، لا أن يعرف غيريتها لنا، وإنما أقمنا الدلالة على الغيرية ألا ترى أن قوما أنكروا الغيرية للجسم على اثبات القول بالتقدم والتأخر. ثم إذ ثبت حدث ما ذكر - والجسم لا يسبقه - ثبت حدثه .
And he answered the objector: that its 'how' is [unknown], for it is your action, and one does not know the modality of his action before performing it, so why did you use analogies to infer motion? It was said: Rather, the how of precedence and posteriority which is our action is known, not that its being other than us is known. We only established the evidence for otherness. Do you not see that some people denied otherness for the body, thereby affirming the statement about precedence and posteriority. Then, since the occurrence of what was mentioned is established—and the body does not precede it—its occurrence is established.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer dialogue concerning the proof that motion is an accident (ʻaraḍ) distinct from the body. The objector argues: you claim motion is an accident, but you do not know its 'how' (kayfiyya)—since it is your own action, and you only know the action after performing it—so how can you use analogies (maqāyīs) to infer motion? The answer replies: the 'how' we know is that of temporality (taqaddum and taʼakhkhur), which is our action; we do not need to know its intrinsic nature (ghayriyya). Our evidence for otherness is that some people who deny that motion is other than the body end up affirming that the body has temporal priority and posteriority—i.e., they admit change. Thus, since the occurrence of what we mentioned (the temporal succession) is established, and the body does not precede that occurrence, the occurrence of motion as an accident is established. The argument relies on the observed fact of temporal change to infer the accidental nature of motion.

Key Terms
حركة
عرض
غيرية
الكيفية
التقدم والتأخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: To prove that motion is an accident, we must know its essential nature or quiddity.
Correction: Maturidi argues that we only need to know the temporal behavior of motion (precedence and posteriority), not its intrinsic 'how'.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's response, what do we know about motion that allows us to prove it is an accident?
A) Its essential nature as a substance
B) Its temporal precedence and posteriority
C) Its physical composition from atoms
D) Its definition from the Quran

Key Takeaway
Motion's status as an accident is proven not by knowing its essence, but by observing its temporal succession, which the body does not precede.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience change in your own actions every day, but do you truly accept that these changes are not you—they are accidents occurring in you? How does this affect your understanding of your own identity and accountability?
?
Maturidi argues that we can know an effect is real without knowing its essence, simply through its temporal behavior. Where in your life are you waiting to fully understand the 'how' before submitting to truth? What would it cost you to accept based on the evidence you already have?
?
The objector demanded certainty about the nature of motion before accepting the proof. Are there truths in your faith that you hesitate to embrace because you want to know more than God has revealed? What is holding you back from submitting to the evidence that is already clear?
