Refuting the view that motion is identical to the body
Maturidi refutes the claim that motion is a body using interpenetration and initial state arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational arguments demonstrating that motion cannot be identical to the body, including the interpenetration argument and the critique of the opponent's 'first state' principle.

Core Idea
Motion cannot be a body; otherwise, interpenetration would lead to infinite absurdity.

In this passage, Maturidi engages with an objection that motion is a body. He presents two lines of reasoning: first, the objection is irrelevant for those who affirm the body's pre-eternity, since motion is perceived as an accident. Second, he constructs a powerful impossibility argument based on interpenetration, showing that if motion were a body, movement would require an infinite regress of interpenetrating bodies—an absurdity. He then turns the tables on the opponent by showing that their own principle about the body's initial state undermines their case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the two parts: first, a quick dismissal of the objection for those who hold the body as pre-eternal; second, the detailed interpenetration argument; and third, the critique of the opponent's 'first state' statement.
The argument is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two refutation strategies
Notice how Maturidi first says the objection doesn't apply to someone who already holds the body as pre-eternal. Then he employs an impossibility proof based on interpenetration. Separate these two in your mind.
3
Understand the interpenetration absurdity
Work through the logic: if motion were a body, then for a body to move, it would need to interpenetrate its location. But interpenetration requires another motion, leading to an infinite chain. This is absurd, like the whole world fitting into an egg.
This shows that motion cannot be a body.
4
Analyze the critique of the opponent's principle
Maturidi points out that the opponent's claim 'the body in its first state is neither moving nor at rest' actually refutes their own position. If there is no first state, then the body cannot be pre-eternal without contradiction.
This reveals inconsistency in the opponent's position.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how Maturidi uses both direct argument and dialectical critique to defend the Ash'ari position that motion is an accident, not a body.
The argument is a model of rational theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عورض بالحركة أنها جسم، فقال: ذا لايسأل عنه من يقول بقدم الجسم، لأنها حدثت بالحس. وقال للإحالة / أن يكون في المكان الأول لا على التداخل، وفي المداخلة [٧] إيجاب حركة أخرى للانتقال، فتكون غير جسم مع ما لو جعلت الثانية متداخلة يلزم تداخل الأجسام بلا نهاية، ولو جاز ذا الجاز تداخل الدنيا في بيضة. ومثله أجاب في التلاقي. وذلك كله تطويل بلا نفع ولو أنصف لوجد ما يمنعه عن دليله، وهو قوله : «الجسم في أول حاله ليس بساكن ولا متحرك»؛ فأخلاه عما ذكر، وفي ذلك سبق عن الذي وصف. لكن من يقول بقدمه لا يثبت له حال الأولية، إذ في ذلك القول بحدثه؛ فلزم [ه] الذي وُصف .
Then it was objected that motion is a body. He said: This is not asked of one who affirms the pre-eternity of the body, because it [motion] is perceived by sense. And he spoke about impossibility: that it be in the first place not by interpenetration, and in interpenetration [there is] necessitation of another motion for transition, so it would be other than the body, along with the fact that if the second [motion] were made interpenetrating, it would necessitate interpenetration of bodies infinitely, and if that were permissible, it would be permissible for the world to interpenetrate an egg. And similarly he answered in the case of meeting [talāqī]. All of that is lengthy without benefit. If he were fair, he would find what prevents him from his proof, which is his statement: 'The body in its first state is neither at rest nor in motion'—thus he emptied it of what was mentioned, and in that there is precedence over what he described. But whoever affirms its pre-eternity does not affirm for it a state of 'firstness', because in that is the affirmation of its temporality; so what was described becomes necessary for him.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that motion is identical to the body. An objector suggests that motion is a body. The response has two parts. First, this objection is irrelevant to someone who already holds the body to be pre-eternal (qadīm), because motion is perceived by the senses as something that occurs. Second, he argues by impossibility (iḥāla): if motion were a body, then for a moving body to occupy a place, one would need either interpenetration or non-interpenetration. If interpenetration is assumed, then a second motion (the transition itself) would be required, which would be other than the body. Moreover, if the second motion were also seen as interpenetrating, it would lead to infinite interpenetration of bodies—an absurdity, like the world fitting into an egg. He then points out that the opponent's own principle—that the body in its first state is neither resting nor moving—actually undermines his case. By denying an initial state, the opponent avoids the proof that motion is an accident, but then he cannot affirm the body's pre-eternity without contradiction, because asserting a 'first state' for the body would entail its temporality (ḥudūth). Thus the opponent is forced into inconsistency.

Key Terms
حركة
جسم
تداخل
قدم
حالة أولية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is a body, i.e., motion is not an accident but a substance.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if motion were a body, it would lead to absurdities like infinite interpenetration, and the opponent's own assumptions about the body's initial state contradict this view. Motion is an accident, distinct from the body.

Checkpoint
In the interpenetration argument, why does the author argue that motion cannot be the body?
A) Because motion is a body and bodies cannot move
B) Because if motion were a body, interpenetration would lead to infinite regress
C) Because the body is pre-eternal and motion is not
D) Because the senses prove motion is an accident

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the view that motion is a body by showing it leads to infinite interpenetration and contradicts the opponent's own assumptions about the body's pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a belief that seems plausible but you haven't examined its logical consequences, how do you respond? This passage shows that Maturidi did not accept a claim at face value; he examined its implications. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold that you have never subjected to such rigorous scrutiny? What might you discover if you did?
?
The opponent gave a lengthy answer that Maturidi considered useless. Do you sometimes produce or accept long explanations to avoid admitting a weakness in your own position? How can you cultivate the humility to admit when your assumptions are flawed?
?
The argument hinges on the concept of interpenetration—something that seems impossible for bodies. Yet we often hold beliefs that logically entail contradictions. In what area of your faith or practice might you be holding onto a contradiction without realizing it? Will you commit to re-examining that area this week?

Maturidi's Proof that Rest is an Accident
Maturidi proves that rest is an accident through the example of being in a house and the persistence of rest over time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that rest (sukūn) is an accident distinct from the body, based on the example of being in a house and the persistence of rest over time.

Core Idea
Rest is not the body itself; it leaves while the body remains.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a simple yet profound proof that rest (sukūn) is an accident (ʿaraḍ) separate from the body. He uses the everyday example of saying 'He is in such a house' to show that rest cannot be identical to the body, because the body exists even when rest is absent. The lesson also addresses a possible objection about the duration of rest. By the end, you will grasp how Maturidi grounds this key distinction in observable experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the flow from the proof to the commentary to the objection and answer.
Notice that the author uses a very direct style with the example of being in a house.
2
Understand the initial proof
Identify how the example 'He is in such a house' shows that rest is distinct from the body. If there were only body and house, the person would not be 'in' the house without rest. Also, rest leaves when motion begins, while the body remains – this proves distinction.
This is the core argument: change in rest ≠ change in body.
3
Examine the objection and answer
Read the objection: someone might say rest is always with the body, but its duration varies. The author responds that this does not contradict the proof – rest is still an accident that can vary. Understand why the answer is considered obvious.
The objection tries to explain away the distinction, but fails.
4
Reflect on the key terms
Review the Arabic terms: سكون (rest), عرض (accident), غيرية (otherness), جسمية (corporeality). Note how they are used in the passage to build the argument.
These terms are foundational for kalam discussions on attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل على أن سكون الجسم معنى غير الجسم بما يقال : هو في دار كذا». لو لم يكن سوى الجسم والدار لكان لا يكون في غير [ها] بموجود، والدار توجد وهو ليس بموصوف بالكون فيها. { قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : } وهذا أمر ظاهر لا يسأله أحد؛ إذ سكناه يزول وقت تحركه من غير زوال الجسمية عنه، فثبت أنه غير. ثم أجاب من قال: «لعل سكونه معه حيث كان بما ما قد يذكر مدة سكونه في مكان بزيادة ونقصان ثبت أن ثمة غير السكون الأول. وهذا مثل الأول لا يُسأل عنه، وجوابه ما بينا . والله المستعان .
And he argued that the rest of the body is a meaning other than the body by what is said: 'He is in such a house.' If there were nothing but the body and the house, he would not be in something other than them existing, and the house exists while he is not described as being in it. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: This is an obvious matter that no one asks about; for his rest leaves at the time of his motion without his corporeality leaving him, so it is established that it is other. Then he answered one who said: 'Perhaps his rest is with him wherever he is, as long as it is mentioned that the duration of his rest in a place increases and decreases, it is established that there is something other than the first rest.' And this is like the first; it is not asked about, and its answer is what we have explained. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage contains a direct proof that rest (sukūn) is an accident distinct from the body, followed by an objection and its answer. The proof uses the example of saying 'He is in such a house.' If rest were identical to the body, then saying 'He is in the house' would mean the body is in the house—but the house exists and the person is not described as being in it unless rest occurs. Thus rest is something additional. Imam Abu Mansur comments that this is obvious: rest leaves when motion begins, without the body ceasing to be, proving that rest is other than the body. Then an objection is raised: perhaps rest is always with the body, but its duration in a place varies; the objection claims that variation shows there is a second rest distinct from the first. The author rejects this as the same kind of misunderstanding, and says the answer is as already given—rest is an accident that changes, not a property of the body itself.

Key Terms
سكون
عرض
غيرية
جسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that rest (sukūn) is inherent to the body and not a separate accident, perhaps because rest seems to be a simple lack of motion.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that rest is an accident (ʿaraḍ) distinct from the body because it can leave while the body remains, and its duration can vary. The body itself does not change when rest begins or ends.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, how do we know that rest is not the body itself?
A) Because the body disappears when moving
B) Because rest leaves the body while the body remains unchanged
C) Because the house exists without the person in it
D) Because rest is a meaning that cannot be perceived

Key Takeaway
Rest (sukūn) is an accident distinct from the body, as proven by its departure during motion and its varying duration, while the body remains unchanged.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience rest and motion daily. When you feel restless or anxious, have you considered that these are accidents that come and go while your body—and more importantly, your soul—remains? How does knowing that your identity is not defined by these transient states affect the way you seek peace? Will you now turn to the Eternal One for true stillness, rather than clinging to created rests that vanish?
?
Maturidi argues that rest is not the body itself—it is an accident that leaves when motion comes. What 'rest' have you been holding onto as if it were part of you? A comfortable routine, a relationship, a status? Are you ready to let it go when Allah calls you to move, trusting that He is the source of all genuine rest?
?
The proof shows that rest varies in duration and intensity, proving it is an accident, not essential. You may have long 'rests' of ease and short 'rests' of hardship. Do you treat these differently, as if one is more real than the other? Can you see how both are temporary, and that your true rest lies not in circumstances but in submission to the One who gives and takes rest? The Argument from the Two Birds: Refuting an Infinite Past of Accidents
Using the example of two birds flying parallel, Maturidi refutes the idea that infinite accidents can coexist with eternal bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the example of two birds flying uniformly to argue that the world must have a beginning in time, refuting the claim that bodies are eternally accompanied by accidents.

Core Idea
An infinite past would erase all distinctions, but we see difference, so time must have a beginning.

Maturidi presents a clever counterexample to the objection that bodies are always accompanied by accidents. If the series of accidents were infinite, then two birds flying side by side from eternity would have no starting point—and yet they remain separate, not meeting. This shows the absurdity of an eternal past for such phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the argument come through before interpreting.
2
Analyze the bird example
Identify the logical structure: the opponent's claim, Maturidi's rejection, and the specific example. Notice how the bird example illustrates a contradiction.
Pay attention to the phrases 'without an end to their beginning' and 'meeting in uniformity'.
3
Grasp the larger implication
Understand that this argument supports the temporal beginning of the world (the world of bodies is not eternal).
This connects to broader discussions about the createdness of the universe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب من عارضه بما كذلك كانت الأجسام غير خالية عما ذكرت أبدا. فزعم أنه لا [۷] يجوز ، لما لا يثبت للكل شرط القدم إلا بوجود غير هو في ذلك، وفي ذلك / بطلان الوجود. واستدل بما سبق ذكره من دخول الدار. مع ما زعم في طيرين يطيران بينهما ذراع من جهة واحدة طيرانا مستوياً ، لم يحتمل أن يكونا كذلك من غير نهاية لأوليتهما، إذ ارتفاع النهاية يوجب الاجتماع بالاستواء وقد وجد التفاضل.
Then he answered one who opposed him by saying that bodies are never devoid of what was mentioned forever. So he claimed that it is not permissible, because the condition of pre-eternity is not established for the whole except by the existence of something other than it in that, and in that is the nullification of existence. And he argued by what was previously mentioned about entering the house, along with what he claimed about two birds flying with a cubit between them from one direction in a uniform flight: it is not possible that they be like that without an end to their beginning, because the removal of the end necessitates meeting in uniformity, but difference has been observed.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents a response to the objection that bodies are always accompanied by accidents (rest or motion) eternally. The opponent claims that if bodies always have accidents, then they must be eternal. The author argues that this is impossible because the condition for the entire set of accidents to be eternal would require something else to exist, which leads to the nullification of existence (i.e., a contradiction). He then uses the example of two birds flying parallel at a constant distance: if their flight had no beginning (i.e., backward infinite time), then there would be no infinite endpoint to their starting points, and they would eventually meet (since infinite time would eliminate all differences). But we observe that they do not meet (the term 'التفاضل' indicates inequality or difference in position), so the assumption of an infinite past is false. This argument aims to show that the world of bodies must have a beginning in time.

Key Terms
أجسام
أعراض
قدم
لا نهاية
طيران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies can be eternal because they are always accompanied by accidents (i.e., an infinite series of accidents is possible).
Correction: Maturidi shows that an infinite series of accidents would lead to contradictions, as illustrated by the two birds example; therefore, the world of bodies must have a beginning.

Checkpoint

C) That bodies require accidents to exist
D) That two bodies cannot occupy the same space

Key Takeaway
The two birds example shows that an infinite past is impossible because infinity would eliminate all distinctions, contradicting observed differences.

Maturidi argues that infinite past would erase all distinctions. In your daily routine, what do you take for granted that actually depends on a finite, purposeful creation? How can you cultivate gratitude for the beginning God gave you?

Refutation of Infinite Movements
Maturidi refutes infinite movements via logical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of the claim that there can be an infinite series of movements.

Core Idea
Every movement that ends must have a beginning.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise argument against the idea of an infinite regress of movements. He argues that if a movement series comes to an end, that end point marks the conclusion of a finite sequence, which necessarily implies a starting point. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical flow from the refutation statement to the conclusion.
Pay attention to the key terms: حركات, نهاية, فناء, انقضاء, ابتداء.
2
Identify the structure of the argument
The passage has three parts: (1) refutation of infinite movements, (2) premise about ended movements, (3) inference that such movements must have a beginning.
3
Understand the core logic
The argument is: If a movement has ended, it is the end of a series. Since the series ended, it cannot be infinite backward (no beginning). Therefore it must have a beginning.
This is a simple but powerful refutation of infinite regress.
4
Apply the reasoning
Consider any action or motion that has finished. According to Maturidi, its very finish proves it had a starting point. Try to apply this to daily examples.
Think of a race or a film: its end implies its beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله: أما القول بحركات لا نهاية لها فقد بينا فيما تقدم فسادها. ما لا يشك في حركة انقضت إلا أنها نهاية ما تقدم من الحركات، حتى لا يكون شيء مما تقدم هذه. وإذا ثبت نهاية الفناء لها والانقضاء لم يجز أن يتناهي الانقضاء لما لا يتناهي له الابتداء، ثبت لذلك الابتداء.
The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: As for the statement about movements having no end, we have already clarified its corruption. There is no movement that has passed of which one does not doubt except that it is the end of what preceded of movements, so that there is nothing that preceded these. And if the end of annihilation and passing away is established for them, it is not permissible that passing away ends for that which has no beginning; therefore, beginning is established for it.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a concise refutation of the claim that there can be an infinite regress of movements. The author begins by stating that he has already clarified the corruption of this idea earlier. The argument runs: any movement that has come to an end is the final point of the series of movements that preceded it; thus there is nothing preceding that series. If we establish that a movement ends (annihilation and passing away), then it cannot be that a movement with no beginning has an end. Hence, every movement must have a beginning. This proves that the series of movements is finite and has a starting point, contradicting the astrologers' claim of endless movements.

Key Terms
الحركات
النهاية
الفناء
الانقضاء
الابتداء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a movement ends, it could be part of an infinite series with no beginning.
Correction: Maturidi argues that an end implies a beginning; an infinite series cannot have an end.

Checkpoint

A) The movement is eternal.
B) The movement must have a beginning.
C) The movement is an accident.
D) The movement is caused by stars.

Key Takeaway
Every finite movement must have a beginning, refuting the claim of an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you pause today to thank Him for the beginning of each moment?

The argument shows that any series of movements that ends must have a beginning. Your own life is a series of movements from birth to death. If your life has an end, what does that imply about its beginning? How does this thought affect your sense of accountability to the One who gave you that beginning? Will you live today with the urgency that your life is finite?
?
You believe that the universe had a beginning. This passage refutes the idea of infinite past movements. When you look at the world around you, do you see the signs of a finite creation that requires a Creator? How does this logical proof confront the assumption that things simply 'always existed'? Are you ready to use this reasoning to strengthen your certainty in Allah's existence when doubts arise?

Argument from Varying Bodies to Creation
Maturidi argues that differing limits of bodies prove their createdness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that the differing limits of substances prove their createdness and the necessity of a beginning.

Core Idea
Observable differences in bodies demand a starting point—they cannot be eternal.

Imam Maturidi presents a rational argument based on the observable world. He notes that all substances have varying limits and sizes. From this variation, he deduces that these bodies must have had a beginning. He also addresses the possibility of eternal movements, showing it leads to contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Be aware of high OCR risk; some phrases may be unclear but the overall argument is sound.
2
Identify the first argument from differing limits
Trace Maturidi's reasoning: we see substances with different limits. He argues that such difference cannot exist without a smallest difference from which the majority results, implying a beginning.
He uses the concept of 'smallest state' (أصغر حال) to show the progression.
3
Understand the second argument about circular movements
The author introduces an additional argument: if movements were eternal, then their annihilation would also be eternal, leading to a contradiction of simultaneous existence and non-existence.
This reinforces the need for a beginning.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Synthesize the two sub-arguments to grasp Maturidi's overall position: the observable world necessitates a temporal beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنا رأينا الجواهر كلها في رأى العين متفاوتة الحدود. لا يحتمل أن تكون هي كذلك على غير أن يكون كذلك إلا عن أقل متفاوت يظهر في أكثره ذلك؛ فثبت أن كذلك. كذلك عن أصغر حال يكون عِظَمه وكثافته بعد أن لم يكن. ولطف عظمه وكثافته في الكون بعد أن لم يكن لا بشيء تقدمه؛ لأن في التقديم إيجاب الاستواء، وقد ثبت التفاوت. فثبت أن الذي تقدم هو حدث بعد أن لم يكن؛ إذ هو في معنى ما ما هو مع ما لو كانت الحركات - إذ هي مستديرة - لو جعلت مستقيمة من جهة ليكون بعضها على إثر بعض، وفي وجود بعضها فناء البعض، ولو وجب قدم الحركات ليجب قدم فنائها، فتكون في الأزل معدومة موجودةً، وذلك متناقض؛ إذ لا يجوز اجتماع الوجود والفناء في حال، فكذا في كل الأحوال، وفي ذلك لزوم الابتداء.
And after that, we see that all substances are, in the eye's view, differing in limits. It is not possible that they are thus without being so except from a smallest difference that appears in its majority; so it is established that it is thus. Thus from the smallest state its bigness and density become after it was not. And the subtlety of its bigness and density in being after it was not is by nothing preceding it; because preceding would necessitate equality, and difference has been established. So it is established that what preceded is an occurrence after it was not; since it is in the meaning of what is with what if movements—since they are circular—if they were made straight from a direction so that some are after others, and in the existence of some is the annihilation of others, and if the eternity of movements were necessary, then the eternity of their annihilation would be necessary, so they would be in pre-eternity non-existent and existent, and that is contradictory; since it is not permissible to combine existence and annihilation in a state, so likewise in all states, and in that is the necessity of beginning.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument for the createdness of bodies based on their observable differences. The author states that we see all substances (jawahir) have differing limits. It is impossible that they were always like this without some initial minimal difference from which the majority results. This establishes that bodies came into existence from a prior non-existence. Furthermore, the transition from small to large (bigness and density) is a change that requires a cause preceding it. If one tried to posit eternal movements, a contradiction arises: circular movements, if straightened, would be sequential, each annihilating the previous. If movements were eternal, then their annihilation would also be eternal, leading to the absurdity of something being both non-existent and existent in pre-eternity. Thus, a beginning is necessary.

Key Terms
الجواهر
الحدود
الحدث
القدم
الحركات المستديرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument uses 'atoms' (juz' la yatajaza) and focuses on indivisible parts.
Correction: The passage uses 'substances' (jawahir) and focuses on observable limits (hudud), not atomistic theory.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the differing limits of substances?
A) They are eternal.
B) They must have a beginning.
C) They are controlled by stars.
D) They are accidents.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses rational observation of varying limits to prove that all created things must have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen that every difference in bodies demands a beginning. Look at the differences in your own life—your skills, your weaknesses, your relationships. Are you treating any of these as if they are permanent fixtures that you cannot change? What would it mean to accept that each is a new creation from Allah every moment, and that you have the power to reshape them?

When you examine your deepest assumptions about yourself—the habits you think are 'just the way I am'—are you idolizing them as eternal truths? Will you today question one such assumption and ask: 'If this too is created, can it be transformed through my intention and Allah's will?'
?
Maturidi refutes the idea that movements (actions) could be eternal without beginning. Consider the actions you repeat daily—prayer, work, speech. Are you performing them as mindless routines that feel like they have always been there, or are you fully aware that each act is a new beginning, a fresh opportunity to seek Allah's pleasure? Muhammad ibn Shabib's Objection and the Principle of Sensory Variation
Maturidi's response to Ibn Shabib's objection about seeing one thing as two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi response to the objection that sensory variation (e.g., seeing one thing as two) implies no objective reality.

Core Idea
Variation in sense perception reflects the condition of the sense, not the reality of the object.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi responds to an objection raised by Muhammad ibn Shabib: if one person sees a single object as two and another sees it as one, how can we know which perception is true? The objection challenges the reliability of sensory knowledge. Maturidi provides a principle that explains such variation, affirming that objective reality remains intact.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the full passage carefully. Notice the structure: the objection, then the principle, then an example.
2
Identify the principle
Find the sentence that begins 'الأصل في هذا ونحوه أن علم الحس يختلف باختلاف أحوال الحس'. This is the key principle.
This principle is the foundation of the response.
3
Analyze the honey example
Consider how the honey example illustrates the principle. The jaundiced person's defect in taste perception does not change the reality of honey's sweetness.
This shows that the variation is in the perceiver, not the object.
4
Relate to the denial of reality
Understand how this response refutes the Sophist position that sensory variation proves no objective truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسأل محمد بن شبيب نفسه بما يرى [المرء] الشيء الواحد شيئين، وآخر يرى شيئا واحدا، فأيهما الحق ؟ فزعم أن الأول حسبه كذلك لنظره يُبصره من جهته، يرى بكل عين غير الجهة التي يرى بالأخرى؛ دليله أنه لو أغور لا يرى . قال الفقيه رحمه الله : } والأصل في هذا ونحوه أن علم الحس يختلف باختلاف أحوال الحس. يعلم ذو الحاس ما به من الآفة، فيعلم أن الآفة حجاب. فبالحاسة يعلم خلاف الحقيقة عند الآفة، وحقيقته عند ارتفاعها . وذلك يكون في الوقت الذي وقعت عليه الحاسة : من لطافة أو بعد أو ستر الجو بما يغشاه ؛ ومرة يكون في البصر. وعلى ذلك شأن كل حاسة. وذلك كله معلوم بالحواس، فلا نقيض عليه مع ما أنه على هذا القول يبطل القول بالخلاف، وبه يَحتج أو يثبت؛ فتبطل قوله بنفي الحقيقة إذ ثبت الاختلاف. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ويعلم الذي يُذكر بالقرب منه " أو بالزيادة من الضوء ليعلم حقيقته؛ إن ضعف بصره عن إدراكه بالآفة ففي مثل هذه الأحوال يظهر. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وجوابنا في صاحب الصفراء الذي يجد العسل مُراً هذا، مع ما يعلم هو من نفسه الآفة فيما يجد به الطعم. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وقال ابن شبيب أختلف فيه . قال قوم: في العسل مرارة، فاذا اتصل بما في ذائقه فيقوى [المرارة ] فيجده [مُراً ]. وقال قوم: إن في ذائق صاحب الصفراء مرارة المرّة الصفراء، فلما اتصلت حلاوة / العسل بالمرّة التي في الذائق وتحركت في ذائقه وجد حسها كذلك.
And Muhammad ibn Shabib asked himself: how does [a person] see one thing as two, and another sees one thing, so which of them is the truth? He claimed that the first one only sees it that way because he looks at it from his direction; he sees with each eye the direction other than the direction he sees with the other; his proof is that if he becomes one-eyed he does not see [double]. The faqih said, may Allah have mercy on him: The principle in this and similar cases is that sensory knowledge differs with the differing states of the sense. The possessor of the sense knows what defect is in him, so he knows that the defect is a veil. Thus through the sense he knows contrary to reality when there is a defect, and its reality when the defect is removed. That occurs at the time the sense is applied: from subtlety, distance, or covering of the atmosphere by what veils it; and sometimes it occurs in sight. And such is the case of every sense. And all that is known through the senses, so there is no contradiction therein, because according to this statement the claim of contradiction is invalidated, and by it arguments are overturned or established; thus his claim of denying reality is invalid since difference is established. And there is no power except with Allah. And the one who is mentioned learns by approaching it or by increasing the light to know its reality; if his sight is too weak to perceive it due to a defect, then in such states it becomes apparent. And there is no power except with Allah. And our answer concerning the jaundiced person who finds honey bitter: this, along with his own knowledge of the defect in that by which he tastes. And there is no power except with Allah. And Ibn Shabib said: They differed about this. Some said: There is bitterness in honey, so when it contacts what is in the taster, the bitterness becomes strong and he finds it bitter. And some said: In the taster of the jaundiced person there is the bitterness of yellow bile, so when the sweetness of honey contacts the bitterness in the taster and it is stirred in his taster, he perceives its taste thus.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from Muhammad ibn Shabib and the response from al-Faqih (likely Maturidi). The objection is: if one person sees a single object as two (due to crossing eyes or similar), and another sees it as one, which perception is correct? Ibn Shabib claims that the first person sees double because each eye sees from a different direction; the proof is that if he becomes one-eyed, he no longer sees double. The response begins by stating the principle: sensory knowledge varies with the conditions of the sense organ. The one who has a defect knows that the defect is a veil, so through the sense he perceives contrary to the true reality when the defect is present, and perceives the true reality when the defect is removed. This variation occurs due to factors like subtlety, distance, or atmospheric covering, and applies to all senses. All such perceptions are known through the senses, so there is no real contradiction; the claim of contradiction is invalidated, and the Sophist's denial of reality is overturned because difference (due to conditions) is established. The response then gives a practical example: a jaundiced person finds honey bitter. The person knows his own defect, so the taste he perceives is due to the defect. Ibn Shabib then mentions two opinions on the cause: some say honey has bitterness that becomes strong when contacting the taster's bile; others say the taster's bile imparts bitterness to the honey's sweetness. The point is that sensory variation does not negate the reality of things; it only shows the effect of conditions.

Key Terms
علم الحس
الآفة
الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensory variation (e.g., seeing double) proves that there is no single objective reality.
Correction: The variation is due to defects or conditions in the perceiver; the object itself has a fixed reality.

Checkpoint
What is the main principle given in response to the objection about seeing one thing as two?
A) Sensory knowledge is always the same regardless of conditions
B) Sensory knowledge varies with the differing states of the sense
C) The senses are unreliable and should be ignored
D) Only the sense of sight is subject to variation

Key Takeaway
Sensory variation is a result of the condition of the sense, not a contradiction of objective reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have been certain about something and later discovered you were wrong because your perception was clouded by a personal bias or emotion, did you blame the truth or your own condition? How does this lesson compel you to re-evaluate your certainty today?
?
If your own senses can deceive you due to a defect, how do you know that your current understanding of God, through the signs in creation, is not also affected by a hidden 'defect' in your heart? What would you be willing to change in yourself to remove that veil?

The Three States of Sensory Perception
Maturidi explains the three states of perception: turning direction, removal of barriers, and confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's account of the three states of sensory perception and how variations in perception arise from the perceiver's conditions, not from the object.

Core Idea
Sensory perception varies due to the perceiver's state, not the object's reality.

Maturidi begins by establishing a foundational principle: perception depends on the alignment of directions between the perceiver and the perceived. He then identifies three possible states of perception, showing that differences in perception are due to changes in the perceiver, not contradictions in the object. This understanding safeguards the reliability of sensory knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Align directions with object
Maturidi explains that each direction of the perceiver corresponds to a direction of the perceived. Perception occurs only when they face each other. Understand this foundational principle.
This is a key concept for the rest of the lesson.
2
Identify the three states
The passage lists three possible states: turning the direction, establishing with barriers removed, and confusion. Note how each affects perception.
The third state is a mixture of the first two.
3
Draw the epistemological conclusion
Maturidi emphasizes that all these variations are real and known by sense, and do not contradict the reality of the object. This shows that sensory knowledge is reliable when conditions are proper.
This counters the skepticism that differing perceptions imply no fixed truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } والأصل في هذا أن الإنسان إذا اشتمل على حدود وجهات، فكل جهة منه تقابل جهة من المدرك لا يدرك بتلك الجهة غير الجهة التي قابلته. فإذا اعترضت الآفة في جهته التي بها يدرك مقابلها أو غشى مقابلها شيء ستره فيذهب مقدار من الجهة ،ومقابلها فيكون كالإدراك بغير الجهة التي لذلك النوع من الإدراك. فتكون الأحوال ثلاثة : تقليب الجهة لا يدرك به شيئًا ألبتة، وتقريرها مع ارتفاع السواتر كلها فيدرك به حقيقة المدرك، أو الاختلاط؛ فعلى تفاوت ذلك يتفاوت الدرك. وكل ذلك حق في الحس معلوم بالحس؛ فلم يرد في علم الحس اختلاف ألبتة في الحقيقة. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The Shaykh said, may Allah have mercy on him: And the principle in this is that when a human being comprises limits and directions, each direction of his corresponds to a direction of the perceived object; he does not perceive through that direction other than the direction that faces it. So if a defect intervenes in his direction by which he perceives its opposite, or something covers its opposite that veils it, then a portion of the direction goes away, and its opposite, so it becomes like perception through other than the direction that belongs to that kind of perception. Thus the states are three: turning the direction does not perceive anything at all; establishing it with the removal of all barriers, so he perceives through it the reality of the perceived object; or confusion. So according to the variation of that, perception varies. And all that is true in sense, known by sense; so there has never occurred in sensory knowledge any contradiction at all regarding reality. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the principle of sensory perception from Maturidi. He states the principle: a human being has various directions (e.g., different parts of the eye), and each direction corresponds to a direction of the perceived object. Perception occurs only when the appropriate direction faces the appropriate direction of the object. When a defect (آفة) affects the perceiving direction, or something veils the object, then the effective direction is altered, resulting in a different perception. Maturidi then enumerates three possible states of perception: (1) turning the direction away — no perception at all; (2) establishing the direction with all barriers removed — perception of the true reality; (3) confusion (اختلاط) — a mixture of conditions leading to a distorted perception. Perception varies according to which state obtains. Maturidi emphasizes that all these variations are real and known by the senses themselves; therefore, there is no actual contradiction in sensory knowledge regarding the true nature of the object. The variations are due to changes in the conditions of the perceiver, not in the object itself. This reinforces the reliability of sensory knowledge when conditions are proper.

Key Terms
الجهة
الآفة
السواتر
الاختلاط
الدرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That different perceptions of the same object imply that the object has no fixed reality.
Correction: Maturidi shows that differences arise from the perceiver's conditions (defects, barriers, confusion), not from the object itself. The object remains real and unchanged.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how many states of perception are there?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that sensory perception varies according to the perceiver's state, but the object itself remains unchanged, preserving the reliability of sensory knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Recall a disagreement you had with someone about what you both saw or experienced. Could it be that the difference was in your respective 'directions' or 'barriers' rather than the object itself? How would acknowledging that change your posture toward that person?
?
What 'defects' or 'veils' in your own heart or mind might be distorting your perception of truth in a matter you consider settled? Are you willing to name them and seek removal?
?
If perfect perception requires removing all barriers, what is one barrier you know you are actively maintaining? What would it cost you to let it go, and what would you gain?

How We Know Dreams Are Not Real: Maturidi's Argument from Rational Impossibility
Maturidi responds to an objection questioning the reliability of waking perception by distinguishing it from dreams through rational possibility and necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that waking perception is verified by necessity because it is free from the rational impossibilities found in dreams.

Core Idea
Dreams contain rational impossibilities; waking perception does not — that is how we know it is real.

How do we know that what we see when awake is real, while dreams are not? Maturidi faces an objection: if dreams can seem real, maybe waking life is also deceptive. In this passage, he responds by pointing out that dreams often present things that are logically impossible — like seeing yourself dead — and that waking perception is confirmed by necessity (idṭirār). Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The passage begins with an objection: the sleeper sees things contrary to reality, so perhaps waking perception is also unreliable. Read this part carefully.
2
Identify the distinguishing criterion
Maturidi's answer: in dreams we see things that are rationally impossible, such as a dead person perceiving. This cannot happen in wakefulness.
3
Address the further objection
A second objection asks how one can even imagine the impossible. Maturidi explains that the impossibility arises from the conjunction of contradictory states, not from the elements individually.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that waking knowledge is acquired (kasb) and verified by necessity. The faqih adds that the sleeper has a defect (al-āfa) and that waking sensation is necessary and involuntary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسته بما يرى النائم فيخرج على ضد ما يرى، فلعل أمر اليقظان على هذا، أو ما يعلم ذا من ذا . فزعم أن الذي يفرق بين الأمرين أنه يرى ما لا يصح في العقل في حال النوم، نحو أن يرى نفسه ميتا - والميت لا يعلم - أو يرى رأسه ملقى في حجره ومثله لا يحتمل رؤية اليقظان.
فإن قيل : كيف يتوهم النائم المحال، وهو لا يثبت في الوهم؟ قيل : عند ما يرى نفسه في المنام لا يعتقدها حية ميتة ، وذلك هو المحال ؛ وكذلك إذا رأى رأسه ملقى لا يتوهمه في مكانين. وزعم أن العلم بصحة ما في اليقظة وفساد ما في النوم اكتساب، دليله ما ذكرت. قال: وقد يرى في المنام ما يصح؛ ذلك إنها يكون بملك يريه أو بما ذلك في الإصحاء ، أو بعض ذلك .
{ قال الفقيه رحمه الله : } والأصل في هذا ما في الأول؛ إن النائم ذو آفة يعرفها بما يعلم به يقظته، وذلك حق الحس. إنه يرى في النوم مضطرا، وفي اليقظة لا . وكذلك يبقى في ألم ما يضرب في حال اليقظة، ويعرف لذة ما به يغتذى . وليس بيننا وبين هؤلاء في هذه الأحوال مسألة، إنما بيننا إلزام حق اليقظة وتحقيقه بضرورة بما ذكرنا . ثم تغير ذلك إنما ذلك للآفات التي تعترض. وجملته أن الطبيعة أو النجوم أو الأغذية لا يحتمل أن تولد ذلك، ولا فيها ما يوجب ذلك، وأن لكل شيء من ذلك مضرة ومنفعة . وما به الغلبة والاعتدال فلا يحتمل وجود مثله بالطبع ولا بالنجم ، من حيث خروج ذلك على ما فيه من الحكمة والإتقان؛ وما يوجبه الطبع لا يحتمل ذلك. وقد مربيان ذلك . والله الموفق.
Then he opposed himself with what the sleeper sees, for it comes out contrary to what he sees, so perhaps the state of the wakeful person is like this, or one does not know this from that. He claimed that what distinguishes between the two matters is that he sees what is not valid in the intellect in the state of sleep, such as seeing himself dead – and the dead do not perceive – or seeing his head placed in his lap, and the like of that cannot be seen by the wakeful. If it is said: How does the sleeper imagine the impossible, while it is not established in imagination? He said: When he sees himself in the dream, he does not believe it to be living and dead simultaneously; that is the impossible. Likewise, if he sees his head placed, he does not imagine it to be in two places. He claimed that knowledge of the truth of what is in wakefulness and the falsity of what is in sleep is acquired (kash), and its proof is what I mentioned. He said: He may see in the dream what is true; that is because it is through an angel showing him, or through something in wakefulness, or some of that. The jurist (faqih) said: The basis of this is what is in the first: the sleeper has a defect that he knows by what he knows of his wakefulness, and that is the truth of sensation. He sees in sleep involuntarily, but in wakefulness not. Likewise, he retains the pain of what strikes him in the state of wakefulness, and knows the pleasure of what he consumes. There is no issue between us and these people in these states; rather, between us is the obligation of the truth of wakefulness and its verification by necessity through what we mentioned. Then that change is only due to the defects that arise. In summary: nature, stars, or foods cannot generate that, nor is there in them what necessitates that; and everything has harm and benefit. That which has predominance and balance cannot have its like exist by nature or by the star, given its coming forth with wisdom and precision; and what nature necessitates cannot have such. And they have taught us that. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit concerning the reliability of waking perception versus dream perception. It begins with an objection: the dreamer sees things contradicting reality, so perhaps waking perception is equally unreliable. The response distinguishes the two: in a dream, one sees things rationally impossible, such as seeing oneself dead (a dead person cannot perceive) or seeing one's head in one's lap. A further objection asks how the dreamer can even imagine the impossible if it is not established in the imagination. The answer explains that when seeing himself dead in the dream, he does not simultaneously believe himself alive and dead; the impossibility lies in the conjunction. Similarly, seeing his head in his lap does not entail it being in two places at once. The author then states that knowledge of waking truth and dream falsity is acquired (kasb), with the proof already mentioned. He also notes that sometimes a dream contains truth, which comes through an angel or something from wakefulness. The jurist (faqih) then comments, affirming that the sleeper has a defect known by contrast with wakefulness; waking sense perception is true because it is necessary and involuntary (mudtarran). He emphasizes that the real issue is affirming the truth of wakefulness by necessity. He rejects alternative explanations (nature, stars, food) because they cannot produce the balanced, wise order observed; nature's effects are not so precise. The passage ends by stating that these points have been taught, and success is from Allah.

اليقظة
الوهم
العقل
الاضطرار
الاكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dream perceptions are as reliable as waking perceptions because both feel real.
Correction: The passage argues that dreams contain rational impossibilities that waking perception does not, and waking perception is verified by necessity (idṭirār), so they are not equally valid.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between dream perception and waking perception?
A) Dream perception is always false, waking perception always true.
B) In dreams, one sees things that are rationally impossible, while waking perception does not present such contradictions.
C) Dream perception is acquired, waking perception is innate.
D) The difference is merely subjective and cannot be established.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes the reliability of waking perception by showing it lacks the rational impossibilities found in dreams and by grounding it in necessity (idṭirār).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a vivid dream that feels real, do you ever question whether your waking life is also a dream? How does Maturidi's argument about rational impossibility force you to confront that doubt? What would it take for you to actually believe that your current state might be a dream?

You often trust your senses without thinking. But have you ever ignored a clear sign from Allah because you convinced yourself that your perception was unreliable? What is stopping you from accepting that your waking perception is necessary and true, and what step will you take today to act on that certainty?
?
Maturidi says that knowledge of waking truth is acquired (kasb). What have you personally done to acquire that certainty? If you were to die tonight, would you be confident that your beliefs are built on a foundation as solid as waking perception, or on something as flimsy as a dream?

Critique of Dualists' Lack of Knowledge and Demand for Proof
Maturidi criticizes dualists' judgment and demands proof for their claims about infinity and the world's beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the dualists' lack of knowledge and his demand for proof for their claims about infinity and the world's beginning.

Core Idea
The dualists make claims about what they cannot know; they must provide proof.

Maturidi identifies a fundamental flaw in dualist reasoning: they speak as if they know the history of light and darkness, yet admit these substances themselves had no knowledge of their mixing or separation. He then turns the tables, demanding evidence for their specific claims about infinity and the world's origin. This passage shows Maturidi's method of exposing internal inconsistency and demanding rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's tone come through.
Notice the word 'عجيب' (astonishing) — Maturidi is surprised.
2
Identify the two parts of the critique
The passage has two main sections: first, a critique of dualists' lack of knowledge about the states of light and darkness; second, a demand for proof for their claims about infinity and the world's beginning.
Look for the transition at 'ثم يُطالب' (then they are required).
3
Analyse the lack-of-knowledge argument
Maturidi points out that the dualists do not inform about actual past states, and moreover, they admit light and darkness themselves had no prior knowledge of mixing or separation. This shows their judgment is based on ignorance, not knowledge.
4
Understand the demand for proof
Maturidi then requests proof for two specific claims: cutting off infinity (i.e., that there is no infinite regress) and the beginning of the world. He also mentions mixing and separation. The goal is to demonstrate their obstinacy when they cannot provide evidence.
The phrase 'ليعلموا تعنّتهم' means 'so that they may know their obstinacy'.
5
Reflect on the method
Consider how Maturidi uses internal inconsistency and burden of proof to critique his opponents. This is a common theological method.
Think about why this critique is effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن حكمهم هذا عجيب؛ لأنهم لا يخبرون عن أحوال كانت ويكون على ما عندهم من جوهر هذين، ولم يكن لهما علم من قبل بالامتزاج، ولا علم بكيفية الفراق . والله الموفق. ثم يُطالب على كل فصل مما قالوا من قطع النهاية وما قالوا من ابتداء العالم، دون أن يكون عالم على أثر عالم بلا نهاية، وذلك يكون بالدليل، وكذلك الامتزاج والانفصال، ليعلموا تعنّتهم.
Moreover, their judgment is astonishing; for they do not inform about states that were and will be according to their conception of the substance of these two (light and darkness), nor did these two have prior knowledge of mixing, nor knowledge of the manner of separation. And God gives success. Then they are required for every point they made about the cutting off of infinity (qatʿ al-nihāya) and what they said about the beginning of the world, without there being a world upon the trace of a world without end, and that is by means of proof, and likewise mixing and separation, so that they may know their obstinacy.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the dualists' epistemological stance. Maturidi points out a fundamental flaw: their judgment about the states of light and darkness is not based on any actual knowledge. They speak as if they know the history of these substances, yet they admit that light and darkness themselves had no prior knowledge of their mixing or separation. This is an internal inconsistency. Then he shifts to a demand for proof (yuṭālab). They must provide evidence for two key claims: (1) the cutting off of infinity (i.e., the world has no beginning, or that there is an infinite series of worlds?), and (2) the beginning of the world. Specifically, he challenges the idea that there could be a world upon the trace of a world ad infinitum without a first cause. They need proof for such a claim. Similarly, they need evidence for their statements about mixing and separation. The purpose is to expose their obstinacy (taʿannut).

Key Terms
حكم
علم
امتزاج
فراق
قطع النهاية
ابتداء العالم
دليل
تعنت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi is demanding proof for God's existence.
Correction: In fact, he is demanding proof for the dualists' specific claims about the world's beginning and infinity, not about God.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what did the dualists lack regarding the mixing of light and darkness?
A) They had no empirical evidence.
B) They had no prior knowledge of mixing.
C) They had no logical argument.
D) They had no textual authority.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi critiques dualists for making claims without knowledge and demands proof for their assertions about the beginning and infinity of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you hold firm beliefs about Allah’s creation or decrees without having clear evidence from revelation or reason? Are you willing to examine those beliefs like Maturidi examined the dualists?
?
Have you ever judged others or situations as ‘astonishing’ when you actually lacked full knowledge? How can you submit to your limited knowledge and seek proof before forming judgments?
?
The dualists claimed to know what even their own substances did not know. Is there any claim you make about the unseen (e.g., about life after death, wisdom in trials) that you assert without Allah’s guidance? What would it cost you to admit ignorance and seek proof from the Quran and Sunnah?

Reason's Moral Judgment: Basis of Divine Command
Maturidi grounds commands and prohibitions in reason's judgment of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi grounds the obligation of divine commands and prohibitions in reason's inherent moral judgments, and how reward and punishment follow from that rational structure.

Core Idea
Reason itself commands good and forbids evil; revelation confirms it.

Imam Maturidi begins by asserting that reason inherently judges certain actions as good or evil, such as truth and justice vs. lying and injustice. This rational judgment gives rise to an obligation to act accordingly, which forms the basis for divine commands and prohibitions. In this passage, he explains how reason's moral verdict leads to reward for those who follow it and punishment for those who follow their whims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Maturidi's concise argument.
2
Identify the rational judgments
Notice that Maturidi states reason deems truth and justice as good, and lying and injustice as evil. These are the basic moral categories.
3
Trace the logical flow
Follow how reason's judgment leads to command/prohibition, then to reward/punishment.
Maturidi uses 'so' (ف) to indicate logical consequence.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that commands are arbitrary. Maturidi shows they are rationally necessary.
This passage guards against voluntarism in ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه قد حسن في العقول الصدق والعدل وقبّح فيها الجور والكذب، فجعل الفريق الأول عظيما في القلوب كريما، والثاني حقيراً مهينا، فتصير العقول آمرة بكسب ما يُعلى شرف من رزق منها، وناهية عما فيه هَوانُ صاحبها؛ فيجب الأمر والنهي بضرورة العقل ثم الثواب، لتتم الكرامة لمن اختار سبلها والقيام بوفائها، والعقاب لمن آثر هواه على إشارة العقل.
And after, truth and justice are deemed good by minds, and injustice and lying are deemed bad by them. So He made the first group great in hearts, honorable, and the second contemptible, abased. Thus minds become commanding of acquiring that which raises the honor of one who is provided with them, and forbidding of that which contains disgrace for its possessor. So command and prohibition become obligatory by the necessity of reason, then reward, so that honor may be perfected for one who chooses its paths and fulfills its obligations, and punishment for one who prefers his whim over the indication of reason.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage establishes the rational foundation for commands and prohibitions. Maturidi states that reason (al-'uqul) inherently deems truth (sidq) and justice ('adl) as good, and injustice (jawr) and lying (kadhib) as evil. Consequently, God made the first group (truth and justice) great and honorable in hearts, and the second group contemptible and abased. Thus, reason itself commands acquiring what elevates honor and forbids what brings disgrace. Therefore, command and prohibition (al-amr wa al-nahy) are obligatory by the necessity of reason (bi-darurat al-'aql). Then, reward (thawab) is [obligatory] to perfect honor for those who choose its paths and fulfill its duties, and punishment ('iqab) for those who prefer their whim (hawa) over reason's indication. This is a clear statement of the rational obligation of moral law, with reward and punishment following from that rational structure.

Key Terms
حسن وقبح عقلي
أمر ونهي
ضرورة العقل
ثواب وعقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine commands are arbitrary and not grounded in reason.
Correction: Maturidi shows that commands and prohibitions are rationally necessary, based on reason's inherent moral judgments.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what makes command and prohibition obligatory?
A) Divine revelation alone
B) The necessity of reason ('aql) that deems good and evil
C) The opinions of scholars
D) Social customs

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the very structure of reason entails moral obligation, making divine commands and prohibitions rationally necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral choice, do you pause to listen to your reason’s inherent judgment of truth and justice, or do you rush to follow your desire and rationalize later? What would it cost you today to act on reason’s command instead of your whim?
?
The passage says hearts naturally honor truth and justice. Yet we often find ourselves attracted to dishonorable acts. Is there a specific area in your life where your heart has become desensitized to what is good? What concrete step will you take this week to re-sensitize it?
?
Maturidi argues that reason itself commands and forbids, making obligation necessary. Do you live as though morality is a burden imposed from outside, or as the dictate of your own rational nature? How would your decisions change if you fully owned that your reason is already commanding you?

The Rational Dilemma: Intellect Alone vs. Prophetic Guidance
Maturidi presents a dilemma leading to necessity of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument that human reason alone cannot resolve all truth, and that a divinely sent messenger with proof is necessary.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot resolve all truth; a messenger with proof is needed.

Maturidi here presents a powerful logical dilemma that forces us to choose between universalizing individual intellects (which leads to contradiction) and accepting a divinely guided messenger. This argument establishes the rational basis for prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma's two options
Read the passage and identify the two alternatives: either rely on one's own intellect or accept what a superior intellect shows.
The phrase 'لا يخلو الأمر من أن يرجع إلى ما يدعوه إليه عقله' sets up the dilemma.
2
See why the first option fails
Maturidi argues that if everyone's intellect were correct, contradictory opinions would all be true—an impossibility. This refutes pure rational individualism.
Notice the phrase 'وفي ذلك شهادة بإصابة كل ذي دين' which he says is impossible.
3
Recognize the implication for the second option
If we accept a superior intellect, that person's intellect functions like a messenger. Then we need a proof to validate that messenger, just as we would for any claim of truth.
The key point: 'فلا فصل بين دليل يقوم بصدقه فيما يخبر عن الله أو بإصابة الحق'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل آخر، إنه لا يخلو الأمر من أن يرجع إلى ما يدعوه إليه عقله، أو يلزم على بعض الصدور عما أراه غيره ممن هو أرجح منه عقلاً. فإن كان الحق هو الأول ليجب الجمع بين العقول والقولُ لكل بالإصابة إذا قال بما أراه عقله، وفي ذلك شهادة بإصابة كل ذي دين اعتمد على عقله، وذلك محال لتناقض الآراء والأقوال . وإن كان الوجه الثاني فيصير عقله كرسول يأتيهم من عند الله، فيحتاج ذلك إلى دليل يعلمنا شخصه . ثم لا فصل بين دليل يقوم بصدقه فيما يخبر عن الله أو بإصابة الحق في كل ما ينطق به عن عقله . والله الموفق.
And another proof: The matter is free from either returning to what his intellect calls him to, or [the necessity] that some accept what another of superior intellect has shown them. If the truth is the first, then it would require combining all intellects and declaring everyone correct when he says what his intellect shows him, and in that is a testimony to the correctness of every religious person who relied on his intellect, and that is impossible due to the contradiction of opinions and statements. And if the second aspect, then his intellect becomes like a messenger coming to them from Allah, and that requires a proof to inform us of his person. Then there is no distinction between a proof establishing his truthfulness in what he reports about Allah or establishing correctness in everything he utters from his intellect. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument that presents a dilemma. It begins with two possibilities: either humans rely solely on their own intellect for guidance, or they must accept what someone of superior intellect (like a potential messenger) shows them. The author quickly dismisses the first option: if everyone's intellect were correct, then all contradictory opinions would be true—an impossibility. Therefore, the second option is necessary. However, if one relies on another's intellect, that person's intellect effectively serves as a messenger. But a true messenger must be validated by a proof (like a miracle). The argument concludes that there is no difference between needing a proof for a messenger and needing proof for any person claiming superior intellect. This leads to the necessity of divinely sent messengers with clear proofs. The phrase 'Allah is the Grantor of success' ends the proof.

Key Terms
عقل
رسول
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument dismisses reason entirely and promotes blind faith.
Correction: Maturidi does not reject reason; he shows that reason alone cannot resolve all truths, especially when contradictory claims arise. Reason itself leads to the need for divine guidance validated by proof.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the first option of relying on one's own intellect?
A) Because intellect is never correct.
B) Because it would lead to accepting contradictory statements as all true.
C) Because everyone has the same intellect.
D) Because intellect is a gift from God that cannot be wrong.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's dilemma shows that relying solely on individual intellects leads to contradiction, necessitating a divinely appointed messenger with proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you insisted on your own reasoning in a matter of faith, ignoring clear prophetic guidance—and what was the result? Will you now repent and submit?
?
If your intellect can lead you to contradict another sincere believer on a core truth, how can you be certain you are right without referring to revelation? What specific step will you take this week to test your assumptions against the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
Maturidi says the person with superior intellect requires a proof. Who in your life do you trust as an intellectual authority in religion, and have you verified that person has the divine proof (such as following the Prophet ﷺ)? If not, how will you investigate?

Intellect's Limits: The Senses Analogy
Maturidi compares intellect to sensory faculties to show their common limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the intellect, like the senses, has inherent limits that necessitate revelation.

Core Idea
The intellect, like the senses, has a limited range and cannot reach everything alone.

In this passage, Maturidi builds on his earlier arguments about the need for messengers. He now uses a powerful analogy: just as each sense has its own instrument and limitations, so too the intellect is a created cause with fixed boundaries. This sets the stage for understanding why reason alone is insufficient for ultimate truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Note the opening statement about the need for messengers.
Maturidi uses a very compact style; the translation will help clarify.
2
Identify the key analogy
Find where Maturidi compares the intellect to sensory perception, especially sight. Notice the details about how senses are limited by distance and smallness.
This analogy is the core of the argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
After establishing the analogy, Maturidi directly states that the intellect likewise has a limit and is affected by obscurity. Consider how this supports the need for divine guidance via prophets.
The end of the passage points to the highest human wisdom being that which has proof—revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا بحمد الله حاجة العقول للرسل والقول بهم، وعجز العقول عن الإحاطة بالكل. والأصل في ذلك وجهان؛ أحدهما أن الله تعالى جعل لكل مدرك آلة بها يدرك، ثم كل من هؤلاء يحيط لذاته دون أسباب تتصل به ثم مع ذلك تعترضه آفات يلزمه إنفادها بالمواد له من الأعوان والحفظ له من الأضداد التي هي أعداء تمنع، ليشهد بذلك بحق الإدراك؛ على العلم بمنع البعد واللطافة عن العمل حق العمل. فعلى ذلك العقل، إذ هو سبب مخلوق، له حد كغيره من أسباب الإدراك، يعترضه ما يعترض غيره، مع غموض الأشياء واستغلاقها. ومن مادته النظر في الأسباب؛ أعلى ذلك مما يُسمع من كلام الحكماء، وأحقهم من له على حكمته برهان. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And we have explained, by the praise of Allah, the need of intellects for messengers and the affirmation of them, and the inability of intellects to encompass the whole. The basis of that is two aspects: one that Allah most high has appointed for every perceiver an instrument by which it perceives, then each of these perceives by itself without causes connected to it, yet it is affected by defects that necessitate its expenditure of provisions for helpers and preservation from opposites that are enemies preventing [it], so that it may bear witness thereby to the rightness of perception; upon the knowledge that distance and fineness prevent true action. So likewise the intellect, for it is a created cause, having a limit like other causes of perception, and what affects others affects it, along with the obscurity and inaccessibility of things. And its matter is reflection upon causes; the highest of that from what is heard of the sayings of the wise, and the most worthy of them is he who has a proof for his wisdom. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage uses an analogy from sensory perception to argue for intellectual limitations. Maturidi first states that he has already established the need for messengers and the inability of intellect to comprehend everything. The basis is twofold: first, Allah has given each faculty an instrument (e.g., eyes for sight), and each operates within its own domain without external causes, yet it is subject to defects that require support and protection from hindrances. For example, sight is affected by distance and small size. The same applies to the intellect: as a created cause, it has a fixed limit like other faculties, and it faces similar obstacles, compounded by the complexity and subtlety of what it seeks to know. The intellect's material is reflection on causes, and the best among the philosophers are those whose wisdom is supported by proof (i.e., revelation). Therefore, intellect alone cannot attain ultimate truth without divine assistance via messengers.

Key Terms
آلة الإدراك
عقل
سبب مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intellect is completely useless and cannot achieve any knowledge without revelation.
Correction: Maturidi's analogy shows intellect has limits, not that it is useless; it can perceive many things but cannot encompass everything without divine guidance.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to show the limitation of intellect?
A) Intellect is like a horse that needs a rider.
B) Intellect is like the sense of sight, which is limited by distance and size.
C) Intellect is like a fire that consumes everything.
D) Intellect is like a river that flows without end.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the analogy of sensory limits to demonstrate that the intellect, as a created cause, cannot fully comprehend without prophetic guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on your own reasoning to decide a matter of faith, what 'defect'—like distance or fineness—might be blocking your perception? Will you submit your conclusion to the proof of revelation today?
?
Maturidi says the highest wisdom is from one who has a proof. Have you ever adopted a belief about Allah without checking if it is supported by divine proof? What will you do to ensure your beliefs are built on revelation?
?
The intellect is a created cause with limits. What is one assumption you hold that might be a 'defect' preventing you from recognizing truth? Will you commit to examining it against the Quran and Sunnah this week?
