Two Ways of Knowing the Unseen
Maturidi explains the two means of perceiving the unseen and their implications for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of the two means of knowing the unseen (inference and report) and how this demonstrates the necessity of prophethood.

Core Idea
The unseen is known either by inference from the seen or by report from a truthful messenger.

Maturidi presents a fundamental epistemological framework: all knowledge of things beyond sensory perception comes through one of two channels. One is rational inference from observable connections, the other is reliable testimony. This framework, he argues, is universally accepted by rational people. He then applies it to religious knowledge, showing that divine commands and prohibitions—having no sensory evidence—can only be known through prophetic report, thereby establishing the necessity of messengers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall structure.
Notice the clear division into two methods.
2
Identify the two methods
Find the description of inference (istidlal) and report (khabar). Note the examples given for each: smoke-fire, sun-light, writing-builder for inference; distant lands, changing conditions for report.
Why does Maturidi say these are known to all rational people?
3
Trace the argument to prophethood
Observe how Maturidi applies the second method (report) to matters of command, prohibition, promise, and threat. He concludes that since these have no sensory evidence, they require a report from a messenger. This establishes the necessity of prophethood.
This is a key argument in kalam for the need for prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجه آخر، إن الله جل ثناؤه جعل السبب الذي به دَرْك كل خارج عن الحس وجهين. أحدهما الاستدلال بالذي عاين إذا اتصل الغائب بالذي عاين، كاتصال الدخان بالنار، وضياء الشمس بها، وكاتصال أثر الفعل بالفاعل نحو الكتابة والبنيان ونحو ذلك. والثاني الخبر ينبئ عن حال ذلك، نحو البلدان النائية والأحوال المتغيرة والأمور النازلة، معروف ذلك عند جميع العقلاء. وبذلك معرفة الانسان الأجناس والفصول والأنواع، وأنواع الطب واللسان وعلوم الصناعات والحروب وغير ذلك. ثم تعرّف الأمر والنهي، والوعد والوعيد، فيما ليس بمحسوس دليله، لا وجه لإدراكه إلا بالخبر، وذلك نحو المباح والمحظور، وما فيه عادة كل شيء من مختلف الأحوال، فيلزم في نحو هذا القول بالخبر. وفيه إيجاب القول بالرسالة.
Another aspect: Indeed Allah, glorious is His praise, made the means by which everything outside the senses is perceived two ways. One is using what is witnessed as evidence when the unseen is connected to the witnessed, like the connection of smoke to fire, and its light to the sun, and like the connection of the trace of an action to the actor, such as writing and building and the like. The second is report that informs about the state of that, like distant lands, changing conditions, and occurring events; that is known among all rational people. And by that, one knows genera, species, types, and the types of medicine, language, sciences of crafts, warfare, and so on. Then one comes to know command and prohibition, promise and threat, in matters whose evidence is not sensory; there is no way to perceive them except through report, such as the permissible and the prohibited, and what is customary for each thing in various states. So it is necessary in such matters to affirm report, and in it is the affirmation of the messenger.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage classifies knowledge of the unseen into two legitimate methods. The first is inference (istidlal) from observed phenomena when there is a necessary connection, like smoke indicating fire. The second is testimony (khabar) from reliable informants, used for things beyond direct experience, like distant places or past events. Maturidi then applies this to religious knowledge: for matters that have no sensory evidence, such as divine commands, promises, and threats, the only way to know them is through prophetic report. This includes rulings of permissible and prohibited, and the customary practices in different situations. Since these cannot be inferred from nature alone, valid report from a messenger becomes obligatory. Thus, the necessity of prophethood is established from the structure of human knowledge.

Key Terms
استدلال
خبر
رسالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi argues that all knowledge comes from report.
Correction: He clearly distinguishes between inference (for connected unseen) and report (for disconnected or non-sensory matters). Report is only needed when inference is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how can one know matters like divine commands and prohibitions?
A) By logical inference from natural phenomena.
B) Only through reliable prophetic report.
C) By using the senses directly.
D) By consensus of the community alone.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that for non-sensory religious knowledge, valid prophetic report is the only source, thus affirming the necessity of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a religious ruling you accept without fully understanding its wisdom. Does this lesson satisfy you that report is sufficient, or do you still secretly long for a sensory proof? What would it take for you to fully submit to the report alone?
?
This lesson shows that inference works for causal connections like smoke to fire. When you look at creation—your own heartbeat, the order of the universe—do you infer Allah's existence from these signs, or do you only rely on what you've been told? Pause today and identify one clear sign in nature that points to its Creator.
?
If the only way to know what is truly forbidden and permissible is through prophetic report, how seriously do you take the reports you have? Are there areas of your life where you act as if the report is uncertain, waiting for a tangible proof before obeying? What would change if you treated every prophetic command as the only possible knowledge?

The Three Rational Categories: Necessary, Impossible, Possible
Maturidi divides all things into necessary, impossible, and possible, and shows role of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's threefold classification of all things into necessary, impossible, and possible, and why revelation is needed in the realm of the possible.

Core Idea
The possible is the realm where revelation guides reason.

Imam Maturidi begins by laying the foundational classification that governs all of theology: everything that can be rationally considered falls into one of three categories—necessary, impossible, or possible. This division is not just abstract; it determines where reason can decide alone and where revelation must intervene. The passage you are about to read is compact but pivotal for understanding the scope of reason and the necessity of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the text speak for itself before analyzing.
Notice the three terms: ممتنع (impossible), واجب (necessary), ممكن (possible).
2
Identify the three categories
Find how Maturidi names the three categories: impossible, necessary, and the 'middle' which is possible. Understand their basic definitions.
The term 'واسط' means the intermediate or middle category.
3
Understand the role of intellect
See that for necessary and impossible matters, the intellect judges absolutely—revelation cannot contradict it. For possible matters, the intellect does not mandate one course over another.
4
Understand the role of revelation
Note that in possible matters, messengers come to clarify 'the better' (الأولى) in each case. This shows the necessity of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصول ثلاثة ممتنع وواجب وواسط وهو الممكن، وعلى ذلك جميع أمر العالم. فالواجب في العقل على جهة لا يجوز مجيء الخبر بغيره، وكذلك الممتنع ؛ ويجئ في الممكن، إذ هو المنقلب من حال إلى حال ويد إلى يد وملك إلى ملك . وفي ذلك ليس في العقل إيجاب جهة ولا امتناع من جهة، فتجئ الرسل ببيان الأولى من ذلك في كل حال. والله الموفق.
Then the fundamentals are three: impossible, necessary, and the middle which is the possible, and upon that is the entire affair of the world. The necessary in the intellect is in a manner that report cannot come with other than it, and likewise the impossible. But [report] comes in the possible, for it is that which changes from state to state, from hand to hand, and from kingdom to kingdom. And in that, the intellect does not necessitate a particular course nor prevent a particular course. So the messengers come to clarify the better (al-awla) of that in every case. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage introduces a threefold classification that is foundational in kalam: the necessary (wajib), the impossible (mumtani'), and the intermediate possible (mumkin). Maturidi states that this classification governs all matters of the world. For necessary and impossible matters, reason determines them absolutely, and revealed reports cannot contradict them. However, in the realm of the possible—things that can change, be transferred, or vary—reason alone does not mandate one option over another. The intellect cannot decide which course is obligatory or prohibited in these contingent matters. Therefore, messengers are sent to explain which possibility is better (al-awla) in each situation—whether it be commanded or prohibited. Thus, revelation guides human choice within the open possibilities.

Key Terms
واجب
ممتنع
ممكن
أولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that reason is irrelevant in possible matters and that revelation commands arbitrarily.
Correction: Maturidi teaches that in possible matters reason does not necessitate a particular course, but it can still discern 'better' choices; revelation clarifies the best option, but reason remains active.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, in which category does revelation provide guidance?
A) Necessary matters, because reason cannot know them.
B) Impossible matters, to show they are impossible.
C) Possible matters, where reason does not necessitate one path.
D) All matters equally.

Key Takeaway
Revelation is essential for guiding human choice in the realm of the possible, where reason alone cannot mandate a single course of action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often rely on your own reasoning to decide what is better in uncertain situations. But the passage says that in the realm of the possible, the intellect does not mandate a course. How does this affect your confidence in your decisions without revelation? Will you actively seek divine guidance before acting?
?
The messengers were sent to clarify the better (al-awla) in every possible matter. When you choose between permissible options, do you truly consider which is better in Allah's sight, or do you just follow your desires? How can you change this?
?
Maturidi says the entire affair of the world is governed by these categories. Does your life reflect that you see necessity in what Allah commands and possibility in what He leaves open? Are you treating some possible matters as if they were necessary, or vice versa? What would it mean to accept that revelation alone clarifies the better for you?

5 min
The Three Categories: Impossible, Necessary, Possible
Maturidi classifies things into impossible, necessary, and possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's threefold classification of realities—impossible, necessary, and possible—and how it establishes the rationality of prophethood.

Core Idea
The three rational categories structure all knowledge and open the door for prophetic guidance.

Imam Maturidi begins by laying the foundational classification that governs all rational discourse about God and the world. These three categories—impossible, necessary, and possible—are not mere abstractions; they determine what can be true, what must be true, and what is open to change. This passage sets the stage for why prophetic revelation is needed within a rational framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the author's words sink in before analyzing.
The Arabic is concise; each term carries weight.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three principles (uṣūl) Maturidi lists: impossible (mumtaniʿ), necessary (wājib), and the intermediate—the possible (mumkin).
Note that the possible is described as an intermediate (wāsiṭ).
3
Understand the role of the intellect
Observe how Maturidi describes the intellect's judgment: for the necessary and impossible, no report can contradict; for the possible, the intellect does not dictate a direction.
This sets limits on what revelation can affirm.
4
Connect to prophethood
Reflect on why messengers come: to clarify the best course (al-ūlā) in matters of possibility, where the intellect is neutral.
Thus prophethood complements reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصول ثلاثة ممتنع وواجب وواسط وهو الممكن، وعلى ذلك جميع أمر العالم. فالواجب في العقل على جهة لا يجوز مجيء الخبر بغيره، وكذلك الممتنع؛ ويجئ في الممكن، إذ هو المنقلب من حال إلى حال ويد إلى يد وملك إلى ملك. وفي ذلك ليس في العقل إيجاب جهة ولا امتناع من جهة، فتجئ الرسل ببيان الأولى من ذلك في كل حال. والله الموفق.
Then the principles are three: the impossible (mumtaniʿ), the necessary (wājib), and the intermediate which is the possible (mumkin). And upon this is the entire affair of the world. That which is necessary in the intellect is on a basis such that it is not permissible for a report to come contrary to it; likewise the impossible. But [a report] may come concerning the possible, for it is that which changes from state to state, hand to hand, and dominion to dominion. In that, the intellect has no requirement of a particular direction nor prohibition of a direction, so the messengers come to clarify the best [course] in every situation. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental classification in Maturidi's epistemology and ontology. He states that the principles (al-uṣūl) are three: the necessarily impossible (mumtaniʿ), the necessarily necessary (wājib), and the intermediate—the possible (mumkin). The entire world order is based on these categories. What is necessary in the intellect (e.g., God's existence) is such that no report can contradict it; the same for the impossible (e.g., God being a body). However, regarding the possible—things that can change from one state to another—the intellect alone does not dictate a specific direction or prohibition. Therefore, prophets are sent to clarify the best (al-ūlā) course in every situation. This establishes the rationality of prophethood within the framework of the three modal categories.

Key Terms
الممتنع (al-mumtaniʿ)
الواجب (al-wājib)
الممكن (al-mumkin)
الأصول (al-uṣūl)
الأولى (al-ūlā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Revelation can override or contradict rationally necessary truths.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that revelation cannot contradict what is necessary or impossible in the intellect; it only operates in the realm of the possible.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, in which category can a prophetic report come to clarify the best course?
A) The necessary
B) The impossible
C) The possible
D) All three

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's three categories—impossible, necessary, and possible—form the rational foundation for understanding God, the world, and the role of prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision that could go either way—a 'possible' matter—do you instinctively seek prophetic guidance (revelation) or rely on your own reasoning? What does your answer reveal about your trust in the Messenger?
?
By accepting that certain truths (like God's existence) are necessary and beyond debate, are you willing to submit your intellect to those truths even when your lower self finds them uncomfortable? What practical change would that submission require today?
?
If you truly believed that prophets are sent to clarify the 'best' course in every possible situation, would you still hesitate to follow the sunnah in your daily choices—your speech, business, or relationships? What is holding you back from full surrender?

Refutation of al-Warraq's First Objection
Refutes the first objection concerning eloquence disparity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the first objection by arguing that the Arabs' failure to produce a text like the Quran proves it is inherently inimitable, and that the challenge in Q. 17:88 would be meaningless if the Quran were human speech.

Core Idea
The Quran's inimitability is proven by the Arabs' natural incapacity, not their lack of effort.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses the first objection claiming that the Arabs could have matched the Quran but simply refrained from doing so due to rivalry. He counters by pointing out that if they had the ability, their failure to produce something similar even after the challenge indicates that their avoidance was natural—meaning it was impossible for them. He then strengthens his argument by citing the Quranic challenge that would otherwise be meaningless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the two distinct arguments: the first about 'nature' and the second about the verse.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the first argument: from nature
The objector said the Arabs refrained 'after effort'. Maturidi says their abandonment despite the effort shows it was not in their natural ability (ṭibāʿ), i.e., impossible for them to produce something like the Quran.
This shifts the burden from lack of effort to inherent incapacity.
3
Understand the second argument: from the verse
If the Quran were human speech, the challenge in Q. 17:88 would be false because no human could match such linguistic knowledge, making the challenge absurd.
This shows the Quran must be divine.
4
Synthesize the arguments
Both arguments together prove the Quran is not human speech but divine revelation. The first shows the Arabs' inability, the second shows the challenge would be meaningless otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الأول فإنه لو كان ما قال ليمتنعون عن ذلك بعد الجهد، فدل تركهم دونه أنهم تركوه طباعا. وأيضاً إنه لو كان كذلك لم يحتمل مثله ممن يقول: لئن اجتمعت الإنس والجن [الإسراء، 88/17] الآية، أن يكون أحد من البشر يبلغ علمه باللسان ذلك.
As for the first, if what he said were the case—that they would refrain from that after effort—then their abandoning it despite it indicates that they left it by nature. And also, if it were so, it would not be plausible that one who says, 'If mankind and jinn gathered together' [al-Isra, 88], that any human could reach such knowledge of the tongue.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage refutes the first objection by arguing that if the Arabs had been capable of producing a text like the Quran after effort, their failure to do so despite the challenge shows that it was not within their natural ability—they left it 'by nature' (ṭibāʿ), implying it was impossible for them. The author then adds that if the Quran were human speech, the challenge in Q. 17:88 would be meaningless, because no human could attain such linguistic knowledge. Thus the Quran's inimitability is established.

Key Terms
طباعا
الإنس والجن
علم اللسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Arabs could have produced a text like the Quran but didn't because of laziness or pride.
Correction: Maturidi argues that their failure despite the challenge proves it was not within their natural ability, not a matter of will.

Checkpoint
How does the author argue that the Quran could not be human speech?
A) The Arabs were too busy with wars
B) The Quran includes a challenge that would be false if it were human
C) The Arabs were not eloquent enough
D) The Quran was revealed in heaven

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is established by the Arabs' natural incapacity and the impossibility of the challenge if it were human speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the Arabs admitted their helplessness before the Quran. Is there a verse or passage of the Quran that you approach with the same humility, or do you read it as if you have mastered it? Will you today read one verse as if you are standing before its Author, asking Him to show you your limits?
?
Maturidi argues that if the Quran were human, the challenge would be false. Have you ever considered the possibility that the Quran might be from a human mind, then examined the evidence to reject it? If you are certain it is from Allah, does your life reflect that certainty—or do you treat it as optional?
?
The Arabs 'left it by nature'—they could not produce even the smallest surah. If you were put to the same test today, with all your learning, do you think you could produce something like the Quran? And if not, what does that inability demand from you in terms of submission?

The Historical Argument for Inimitability
Imam Maturidi presents a historical argument based on the Arabs' poetic efforts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Arabs' inability to produce a similar Quran, despite their effort in poetry, supports the argument for its divine origin.

Core Idea
The Arabs' failure to produce a Quranic verse proves its unique divine source.

This lesson examines the first part of Imam Maturidi's refutation of al-Warraq's fourth objection. Al-Warraq suggested the Prophet might have had a special power enabling him to produce the Quran. Imam Maturidi argues that if the Arabs could have produced similar speech, they would have, given their intense hostility and willingness to sacrifice everything. Their failure is evidence of the Quran's inimitability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument unfold.
Focus on the mention of 'labored over a poem for a year'.
2
Identify the historical context
Understand that the Arabs were renowned for poetry and could spend a year perfecting a single poem. This shows their linguistic capability.
3
Understand the argument from effort and hostility
The passage states that if the Arabs could have produced similar to the Quran, they would have, especially since they sacrificed lives and wealth to oppose Islam. Their failure despite opportunity is key.
Notice the mention of 'twenty years before the wars' as ample time.
4
Connect to the concept of inimitability (I'jazz)
This historical evidence is used to prove that the Quran is beyond human capacity, thus a miracle from Allah. This refutes the idea that the Prophet had a special enabling power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع قد تكلفوا المجاوبات لأقوام معروفين في فن حتى اجتهدوا في قصيدة حولاً، فلو كان يحتمل وسعهم أو يرجون البلوغ بطرق ما احتمل تركهم، وفي ذلك تشنيع على القوم، وقد بذلوا مهجهم ودنياهم في إطفاء هذا النور. والفصل الثاني، لا يحتمل الذي ذكر، لما بذلك غنى لهم عن بذل المهج، ولما أُمهلوا قريبا من عشرين سنة قبل الحروب. وجواب الرابع أن الله تعالى إذا خص أحدا بقوة لا يشاركه فيها أحد يمنعه عن دعوى النبوة باللفظ، كما منع من يظفر بحجر المغناطيس، ولو علم أنه يدعى لا يعطيه. والثاني أن لا أحد، في شيء له فضل قوة إلا طمع غيره استتمام ذلك أو عمل ذلك النوع بقدر قوته، والدليل ما يخرج عن الطباع. وبعد، فإنه لو كان له في ذلك فضل قوة بها عمل لكان لا يتمكن نيلها بهم، وليست لهم، إذ لا يوجد مثل ذا في شيء من الأمور؛ دل أن الله جعله فيه ليكون آية لقوله. وسنذكر جمل هذه التأويلات بعد الفراغ من فصوله.
As for the fourth, they undertook responses to known peoples in an art until they labored over a poem for a year. So if it had been within their capacity or they hoped to achieve it by means, their abandonment of it would not be plausible, and that would be a disgrace upon the people, and they had sacrificed their lives and their worldly goods to extinguish this light. And the second section: what was mentioned is not plausible, because that would have sufficed them from sacrificing lives, and they were given respite nearly twenty years before the wars. The answer to the fourth: that if Allah the Exalted distinguishes someone with a power in which no one shares with him, He prevents him from claiming prophethood by speech, just as He prevents one who obtains a magnetic stone—if he knew that it would be claimed, he would not give it. And the second: there is no one who has an excess of power in anything except that another hopes to complete that or do that kind to the extent of his power, and the evidence is what goes beyond natural dispositions. Moreover, if he had an excess of power in that by which he acted, he would not be able to attain it through them, and they do not have it, since the like of this is not found in any matter; it indicates that Allah placed it in him to be a sign for his statement. And we will mention the sum of these interpretations after finishing its sections.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage refutes the fourth objection, which claimed the Prophet might have had a special power enabling him to produce the Quran. The author first argues from history: the Arabs exerted great effort in poetry, laboring for a year on a single poem. If they could have produced something like the Quran, they would have, especially given their hostility and sacrifice to extinguish Islam. The author then gives a second argument: the claim that the Prophet had a unique power is itself a recognition of inimitability—such a power is a divine sign, not a natural endowment. Using the analogy of a magnetic stone, the author explains that if someone were given a unique power, that power would prevent them from claiming prophethood by speech, because it would be obviously miraculous. The author concludes that such a power cannot be attained by others, proving it is a sign from Allah. The passage ends with a note that further interpretation will follow.

Key Terms
المجاوبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claimed the Prophet had a special power that made the Quran easy for him.
Correction: Imam Maturidi shows that if such a power existed, it would still be a divine sign, and the Arabs' failure to imitate proves the Quran's uniqueness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the Arabs do that shows their capability in poetry?
A) They wrote many poems quickly.
B) They labored for a year on a single poem.
C) They ignored poetry completely.
D) They only wrote short verses.

Key Takeaway
The Arabs' inability to produce a Quranic verse, despite their poetic skill and hostility, is historical evidence of its divine nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Arabs labored for a year on a single poem in their attempt to match the Quran. How many hours this week have you spent truly laboring over the meaning of a single verse?
?
They were willing to sacrifice their lives and possessions to extinguish the light of Islam. What comforts or priorities are you unwilling to sacrifice to keep that light burning in your own heart?
?
They were given nearly twenty years of respite, yet they failed. You have been given years of your life to respond to the Quran's challenge—have you, even once, poured your full effort into reflecting on its words as they did on their poems?

The Rational Argument: The Magnetic Stone Analogy
Imam Maturidi uses the magnetic stone analogy to explain why a unique power is a miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogy of the magnetic stone and how it refutes the claim that the Prophet's power was natural.

Core Idea
A unique power is a divine sign, not a natural ability.

In this lesson, we explore the second part of Imam Maturidi's response. He uses the analogy of a magnetic stone to argue that if someone had a unique power, that power would itself prevent them from claiming prophethood by ordinary means, because it would be an obvious miracle. Moreover, such a power cannot be attained by others, proving it is from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy and condition
Read the first part: if Allah gives a unique power, He prevents the person from claiming prophethood by ordinary speech. This sets up the condition.
The phrase 'يمنعه عن دعوى النبوة باللفظ' is key.
2
Understand the magnetic stone analogy
Compare with a magnetic stone: if someone finds such a stone, he would not be given it if it were known he would misuse it. Similarly, the unique power prevents false claims.
3
Consider the second argument: universal hope for similar power
Whenever someone has an excess of power, others hope to achieve the same. But this power is beyond natural dispositions, showing its divine origin.
The evidence is 'ما يخرج عن الطباع' (what goes beyond natural dispositions).
4
Conclude that it is a divine sign
Since such a power is not found in any other matter, it indicates that Allah placed it as a sign for the Prophet's truthfulness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع قد تكلفوا المجاوبات لأقوام معروفين في فن حتى اجتهدوا في قصيدة حولاً، فلو كان يحتمل وسعهم أو يرجون البلوغ بطرق ما احتمل تركهم، وفي ذلك تشنيع على القوم، وقد بذلوا مهجهم ودنياهم في إطفاء هذا النور. والفصل الثاني، لا يحتمل الذي ذكر، لما بذلك غنى لهم عن بذل المهج، ولما أُمهلوا قريبا من عشرين سنة قبل الحروب. وجواب الرابع أن الله تعالى إذا خص أحدا بقوة لا يشاركه فيها أحد يمنعه عن دعوى النبوة باللفظ، كما منع من يظفر بحجر المغناطيس، ولو علم أنه يدعى لا يعطيه. والثاني أن لا أحد، في شيء له فضل قوة إلا طمع غيره استتمام ذلك أو عمل ذلك النوع بقدر قوته، والدليل ما يخرج عن الطباع. وبعد، فإنه لو كان له في ذلك فضل قوة بها عمل لكان لا يتمكن نيلها بهم، وليست لهم، إذ لا يوجد مثل ذا في شيء من الأمور؛ دل أن الله جعله فيه ليكون آية لقوله. وسنذكر جمل هذه التأويلات بعد الفراغ من فصوله.
As for the fourth, they undertook responses to known peoples in an art until they labored over a poem for a year. So if it had been within their capacity or they hoped to achieve it by means, their abandonment of it would not be plausible, and that would be a disgrace upon the people, and they had sacrificed their lives and their worldly goods to extinguish this light. And the second section: what was mentioned is not plausible, because that would have sufficed them from sacrificing lives, and they were given respite nearly twenty years before the wars. The answer to the fourth: that if Allah the Exalted distinguishes someone with a power in which no one shares with him, He prevents him from claiming prophethood by speech, just as He prevents one who obtains a magnetic stone—if he knew that it would be claimed, he would not give it. And the second: there is no one who has an excess of power in anything except that another hopes to complete that or do that kind to the extent of his power, and the evidence is what goes beyond natural dispositions. Moreover, if he had an excess of power in that by which he acted, he would not be able to attain it through them, and they do not have it, since the like of this is not found in any matter; it indicates that Allah placed it in him to be a sign for his statement. And we will mention the sum of these interpretations after finishing its sections.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage refutes the fourth objection, which claimed the Prophet might have had a special power enabling him to produce the Quran. The author first argues from history: the Arabs exerted great effort in poetry, laboring for a year on a single poem. If they could have produced something like the Quran, they would have, especially given their hostility and sacrifice to extinguish Islam. The author then gives a second argument: the claim that the Prophet had a unique power is itself a recognition of inimitability—such a power is a divine sign, not a natural endowment. Using the analogy of a magnetic stone, the author explains that if someone were given a unique power, that power would prevent them from claiming prophethood by speech, because it would be obviously miraculous. The author concludes that such a power cannot be attained by others, proving it is a sign from Allah. The passage ends with a note that further interpretation will follow.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Prophet's power was a natural ability.
Correction: The magnetic stone analogy shows it is a unique divine sign, not natural.

Checkpoint
What does the magnetic stone analogy illustrate?
A) That the Prophet had natural magnetism.
B) That a unique power is a divine sign preventing false claims.
C) That the Arabs were magnetized.
D) That magnetic stones are miraculous.

Key Takeaway
A unique power beyond natural means is a divine sign, proving the truth of the Prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed a unique ability or success solely to your own effort, forgetting that it could be a sign from Allah? How would viewing it as a divine sign change your gratitude and humility?
?
The magnetic stone analogy shows that Allah withholds powers from those who would misuse them. Is there a talent or opportunity in your life that you have used to draw attention to yourself rather than to Allah? What would it mean to redirect that as a sign for His message?
?
If miracles are beyond natural dispositions, why do you still doubt Allah's power to intervene in your life in ways that defy your expectations? What natural 'explanations' have you used to dismiss His hand at work?

Refuting Objections to the Quran's Inimitability
Refutes al-Warraq's second and third objections about war and ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes two objections to the Quran's inimitability: that the Arabs were busy with wars and that they lacked the knowledge to appreciate it.

Core Idea
The Arabs' failure to produce a Quran-like text despite their intelligence proves its divine origin.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses two skeptical objections head-on. Critics claimed that warfare preoccupied the Arabs or that ignorance prevented them from recognizing the Quran's miracle. Maturidi counters by showing their literary engagement and sharp intellect, turning their silence into a proof of the Quran's uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument against the second and third objections.
Notice the logical connectors like 'وبعد' and 'على أن'.
2
Identify the two objections and their refutations
Find where each objection is stated and how the author refutes it. The second objection concerns war preoccupation; the third concerns lack of knowledge.
The refutations use historical and rational arguments.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider why the fact that Arabs responded to poets but not the Quran is a decisive proof. This shows their capability yet inability to produce a similar text.
This connects to the core idea of inimitability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قريبا من عشرين سنة قبل الحروب، ولما فيه تقريع الجن والإنس، وإنما حارب قوم. وبعد، فإن المحاربة لم تمنعهم من مجاوبات بما سمعوا من رسول الله، فكذلك الإتيان بمثل القرآن، لو احتمل وسعهم. والثالث، لو كان كذلك لاستقبلوه بالأذكار والرفع، كفعل بالعرف، لا بالخضوع والامتناع. على أن العرب أذكى الناس عقلاً وأشدهم حمية، وقد قابلوا الشعراء بالأشعار أيضاً. وبعد، فإن التقريع كان به جميع البشر والجن، وقد انتشر أمره وظهر في الآفاق. وأيضا، فإن الذي حمله على ذلك بما جاء به نشوءه بينهم؛ وإن كان له معرفة ونظر مع نشوئه بينهم، فذلك أيضا آية له.
Nearly twenty years before the wars, and because it included a challenge to jinn and mankind, and only a group fought. Moreover, warfare did not prevent them from responding to what they heard from the Messenger of Allah, so likewise [they could have produced] the like of the Quran, if it were within their capacity. As for the third, if it were so, they would have greeted it with fame and elevation, as is customary, not with submission and refusal. Yet the Arabs were the sharpest in intellect and most zealous, and they confronted poets with poetry as well. Moreover, the challenge was to all mankind and jinn, and its matter spread and appeared in the horizons. Also, what compelled him [the Prophet] to bring it was his upbringing among them; and if he had knowledge and insight despite his upbringing among them, that too is a sign for him.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage addresses the second and third objections together. Against the second (that wars preoccupied the Arabs), the author notes that nearly twenty years passed before major battles, and the challenge was to all jinn and mankind; moreover, warfare did not stop the Arabs from responding verbally to the Prophet, so if they could have produced a similar Quran they would have. Against the third (lack of knowledge), the author argues that if the Arabs were incapable, they would have acknowledged the Quran's superiority with honor, not with stubborn rejection. The Arabs were intelligent and zealous; they even responded to poets with poetry. The fact that they did not produce a similar text shows they could not. Furthermore, the Prophet's own upbringing among them—without formal education—is itself a sign of divine origin.

Key Terms
تقريع الجن والإنس
الأذكار والرفع
أذكى الناس عقلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The wars mentioned here include the early skirmishes before the Hijra.
Correction: The author specifies 'nearly twenty years before the wars', referring to the major battles after the Hijra, not the minor earlier conflicts.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the fact that Arabs responded to poets prove the Quran's inimitability?
A) They were too busy with wars
B) They could produce poetry but not the Quran
C) They did not like poetry
D) The Quran is a poem

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is proven by the Arabs' failure to produce a similar text despite their literary prowess and the universal challenge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were among the Arabs who heard the Quran for the first time, knowing their intelligence and pride, would you have submitted or rejected it? What prevents you from acknowledging clear truth in your own life right now?
?
The Prophet grew up among his people without formal education, yet he brought a book they could not match. Do you recognize God's signs in the ordinary circumstances of your life? What sign are you currently ignoring because it comes from an unexpected source?
?
The Arabs responded to poets with poetry and could have produced a similar text if it were possible. When you face something beyond human explanation, do you humbly accept it or stubbornly resist? Where in your life is your pride keeping you from submitting to the truth?

Two Types of Good and Evil in Reason
Maturidi answers an objection by distinguishing two types of rational good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between unchangeable and context-dependent rational judgments of good and evil, and how this answers objections to divine commands.

Core Idea
Reason knows some goods and evils absolutely, others depend on context.

Maturidi encounters an objection: if God commands something reason deems evil, that would allow injustice. His answer is a crucial distinction. Not all rational judgments are fixed; some vary by outcome, introduction, or situation. This lesson explores that distinction and its role in defending prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to see the flow: an objection, then Maturidi's two-part answer.
The passage is cut off but the core distinction is complete.
2
Identify the two types
Notice that the answer divides rational good and evil into two types. Find the first type that never changes, and the second that changes based on context.
Examples given: gratitude to the benefactor (unchangeable good) and lying (unchangeable evil).
3
Understand the implication
Reflect on why this distinction matters. It shows that what reason deems good or evil can vary, so divine commands that seem objectionable may fall under the second type, serving a greater wisdom.
This rebuts the claim that a wise God cannot command something reason calls evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم من أنكر الرسل بما لا يأمر الحكيم بما يقبح في العقل؛ إذ لو جاز مجيء الخبر بمثله لجاز ذلك في إباحة الجور والكذب. فأجيب بأن ما حسنه العقل وقبحه نوعان، أحدهما لا يتغير، نحو شكر المنعم وقبح الكذب. والثاني هو الذي يحسنه العقل للعاقبة أو للمقدمة أو للحال، نحو ما يحسن
And one who denied the messengers claimed that the Wise does not command what is evil in reason; for if it were permissible for news to come with the like of that, then that would be permissible in permitting injustice and lying. So it was answered: That which reason deems good and evil is of two types. One of them does not change, like gratitude to the benefactor and the evil of lying. The second is that which reason deems good for the outcome, or for the introduction, or for the situation, like what is deemed good...
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection that a wise God cannot command something evil in reason, which would allow injustice and lying. The answer distinguishes two types of good and evil. The first type is unchangeable: reason always deems gratitude to the benefactor good and lying evil. The second type depends on context, outcome, or situation; reason may deem something good temporarily. The implication is that commands that seem objectionable may fall under the second type, serving a greater wisdom. The passage is cut off but presents the core distinction.

Key Terms
حسن العقل
قبح العقل
شكر المنعم
قبح الكذب
العاقبة
المقدمة
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if reason deems something evil, God can never command it; otherwise He would be unjust or unwise.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that some rational judgments are absolute (unchangeable), but others depend on context. God may command something that appears evil to reason in a context-dependent way, but it is actually good due to a greater wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which type of rational good and evil never changes?
A) Good for the outcome
B) Good for the introduction
C) Gratitude to the benefactor and evil of lying
D) Good for the situation

Key Takeaway
Reason knows some goods and evils absolutely, while others depend on context, allowing divine commands to be always wise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have I justified a sin by telling myself it was for a greater good? How does this lesson's distinction between unchanging and context-dependent goods confront that self-deception? What concrete step will I take this week to align my moral reasoning with God's unchanging standards?
?
This lesson shows that some goods are absolute, like gratitude to a benefactor. Who in your life—parents, teachers, or even God—have you been ungrateful to, rationalizing it as 'not required'? How will you express gratitude today, as the passage demands?
?
The objector assumed God cannot command what seems evil in reason. Have you ever doubted God's wisdom in a hardship, thinking 'this cannot be good for me'? How does the context-dependent type of good challenge that doubt, and what specific hardship are you currently refusing to see as potentially good?

Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
The Objection and Immutable Rational Judgments
Maturidi presents an objection and introduces the first type of rational judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a wise God cannot command what reason deems bad, and Maturidi's first type of rational judgment that is immutable.

Core Idea
Some moral truths are fixed by reason, regardless of outcome.

A common objection to divine revelation is that a wise God cannot command what reason considers bad. Imam Maturidi responds by distinguishing two types of rational judgments. The first type is absolute and unchanging. This lesson explores that objection and the immutable category.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify the denier's argument: the Wise would not command what reason considers bad, otherwise injustice and lying could be justified.
This is a key challenge to revelation.
2
See the answer's first type
Maturidi answers that rational good and bad are of two types. The first type never changes, like gratitude to the Benefactor and the ugliness of lying.
These are self-evident moral truths.
3
Reflect on why this matters
Understand that some moral judgments are absolute, providing a stable foundation for ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم من أنكر الرسل بما لا يأمر الحكيم بما يقبح في العقل؛ إذ لو جاز مجيء الخبر بمثله لجاز ذلك في إباحة الجور والكذب. فأجيب بأن ما حسّنه العقل وقبّحه نوعان، أحدهما لا يتغير، نحو شكر المنعم وقبح الكذب. والثاني هو الذي يُحسنه العقل للعاقبة أو للمقدمة أو للحال، نحو ما يحسن في العقل تأديب المنهمك في الفساد الباغي على وجه الانتقام، فجائز ورود الشرع بمثله. وعلى ذلك أمر الذبائح، ولو قدر الإباحة فيه، بما كان كل حي يموت، والذبح أروح إليه وأيسر عليه، فيكون بمعنى الأشياء المباحة. والثاني أن العدل في الجملة حسن والجور في الجملة قبيح، لكن من الأشياء ما يظهر قبحه بالنهي وحسنه بالأمر، وذلك نحو تقلب أحوال المرء وانتقاله، وعلى ذلك ذبح الحيوان، إذ جاءت به الرسل، وهم لا يأتون إلا بالعدل. وعلى ذلك أمر الثنوية بما أجازوا للنور ضرر الظلمة لما رأى من المصلحة.
And one who denied the messengers claimed that the Wise does not command what is deemed bad by reason; for if the coming of a report with the like of it were permissible, then that would permit injustice and lying. So it was answered: What reason deems good or bad is of two types: one that does not change, like gratitude to the benefactor and the ugliness of lying. The second is what reason deems good for the sake of an outcome, or a preliminary, or a situation, such as what reason deems good: disciplining one engrossed in transgression and oppression in the manner of retaliation, so it is permissible for revelation to come with the like of it. And upon that is the matter of slaughtering animals; even if permission were considered in it, since every living thing dies, and slaughter is more merciful and easier for it, so it is in the sense of permissible things. The second [point]: justice in general is good and injustice in general is bad, but some things appear bad by prohibition and good by command, such as the changing of a person's states and transitions, and upon that is the slaughter of animals, since the messengers came with it, and they only come with justice. And upon that is the matter of the dualists, by what they permitted for light [to be] harmed by darkness because of the benefit they saw.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from a denier of messengers and the response. The objection argues that a wise being cannot command what reason considers bad, otherwise it would justify injustice and lying. The answer distinguishes two types of rational good and bad. The first type is immutable: gratitude to the benefactor is always good, and lying is always bad. The second type is conditional: reason may deem something good or bad based on its outcome, context, or situation. For example, disciplining a transgressor through retaliation is deemed good by reason because of the beneficial consequence. Revelation can prescribe such acts. Similarly, slaughtering animals is permitted because it is more merciful than letting them die naturally. Then a second point is added: justice is good in general, but some things become good or bad only by command or prohibition, like changes in a person's state. The messengers only command what is just. Finally, the dualists (Manichaeans) themselves permit harm to light if they see an overall benefit, showing inconsistency in their objection.

Key Terms
حسّن العقل
قبّح العقل
نوعان
لا يتغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why can't the Wise command what reason deems bad?
A) Because reason is fallible
B) Because it would permit injustice and lying
C) Because revelation is unnecessary
D) Because reason is independent

Key Takeaway
The immutable type of rational judgment includes gratitude to the Benefactor and the ugliness of lying—these are never subject to change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified lying because it led to a good outcome? How does this passage's claim that lying is always rationally bad challenge your reasoning?
?
Think of someone who has benefited you greatly. Did you feel a duty to be grateful, or did you consider it optional? What would change if you viewed gratitude as an immutable rational obligation?
?
The objection suggests that if revelation could override reason, then injustice might be permitted. Does this make you uneasy about your own reliance on reason in matters of faith? How will you respond to that tension?

Conditional Rational Judgments and Their Applications
Maturidi explains the conditional type of rational judgment and its examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second type of rational judgment that depends on outcomes or context, and how it applies to examples like retaliation and animal slaughter.

Core Idea
Reason can deem acts good or bad based on their consequences or circumstances.

After establishing immutable moral principles, Maturidi explains a second type of rational judgment that is conditional. These judgments change based on outcome, context, or situation. This lesson examines this second type and its application to practical matters such as disciplining wrongdoers and slaughtering animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second type
Read the description: reason deems things good for the sake of an outcome, a preliminary, or a situation.
Contrast with the first type.
2
Examine the example of retaliation
Disciplining a persistent transgressor in the manner of retaliation is considered good by reason because of the beneficial outcome.
Revelation can command such acts.
3
Apply to animal slaughter
Slaughter is permitted because it is more merciful than natural death; thus it is like permissible things.
Context matters.
4
Understand the second point about justice
Justice is good overall, but some acts become good or bad only by command or prohibition, like changing states.
This reinforces the conditional nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم من أنكر الرسل بما لا يأمر الحكيم بما يقبح في العقل؛ إذ لو جاز مجيء الخبر بمثله لجاز ذلك في إباحة الجور والكذب. فأجيب بأن ما حسّنه العقل وقبّحه نوعان، أحدهما لا يتغير، نحو شكر المنعم وقبح الكذب. والثاني هو الذي يُحسنه العقل للعاقبة أو للمقدمة أو للحال، نحو ما يحسن في العقل تأديب المنهمك في الفساد الباغي على وجه الانتقام، فجائز ورود الشرع بمثله. وعلى ذلك أمر الذبائح، ولو قدر الإباحة فيه، بما كان كل حي يموت، والذبح أروح إليه وأيسر عليه، فيكون بمعنى الأشياء المباحة. والثاني أن العدل في الجملة حسن والجور في الجملة قبيح، لكن من الأشياء ما يظهر قبحه بالنهي وحسنه بالأمر، وذلك نحو تقلب أحوال المرء وانتقاله، وعلى ذلك ذبح الحيوان، إذ جاءت به الرسل، وهم لا يأتون إلا بالعدل. وعلى ذلك أمر الثنوية بما أجازوا للنور ضرر الظلمة لما رأى من المصلحة.
And one who denied the messengers claimed that the Wise does not command what is deemed bad by reason; for if the coming of a report with the like of it were permissible, then that would permit injustice and lying. So it was answered: What reason deems good or bad is of two types: one that does not change, like gratitude to the benefactor and the ugliness of lying. The second is what reason deems good for the sake of an outcome, or a preliminary, or a situation, such as what reason deems good: disciplining one engrossed in transgression and oppression in the manner of retaliation, so it is permissible for revelation to come with the like of it. And upon that is the matter of slaughtering animals; even if permission were considered in it, since every living thing dies, and slaughter is more merciful and easier for it, so it is in the sense of permissible things. The second [point]: justice in general is good and injustice in general is bad, but some things appear bad by prohibition and good by command, such as the changing of a person's states and transitions, and upon that is the slaughter of animals, since the messengers came with it, and they only come with justice. And upon that is the matter of the dualists, by what they permitted for light [to be] harmed by darkness because of the benefit they saw.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from a denier of messengers and the response. The objection argues that a wise being cannot command what reason considers bad, otherwise it would justify injustice and lying. The answer distinguishes two types of rational good and bad. The first type is immutable: gratitude to the benefactor is always good, and lying is always bad. The second type is conditional: reason may deem something good or bad based on its outcome, context, or situation. For example, disciplining a transgressor through retaliation is deemed good by reason because of the beneficial consequence. Revelation can prescribe such acts. Similarly, slaughtering animals is permitted because it is more merciful than letting them die naturally. Then a second point is added: justice is good in general, but some things become good or bad only by command or prohibition, like changes in a person's state. The messengers only command what is just. Finally, the dualists (Manichaeans) themselves permit harm to light if they see an overall benefit, showing inconsistency in their objection.

Key Terms
للعاقبة
الذبائح
حسّن العقل
قبّح العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All rational moral judgments are absolute and unchanging.
Correction: Maturidi shows that many rational judgments are conditional, depending on outcome, context, or command.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is disciplining a transgressor through retaliation considered good by reason?
A) Because it is inherently just
B) Because of the beneficial outcome for the community
C) Because reason is absolute
D) Because it is commanded by revelation

Key Takeaway
Rational judgments can be conditional, such as disciplining for the greater good or permitting slaughter for mercy, and some acts become good or bad only by divine command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions, do you ever justify a wrong by focusing on a 'good outcome'? How can you ensure your reasoning submits to divine command rather than self-interest?
?
This lesson teaches that some acts become good only by God's command. Is there an area in your life where you rely solely on your own reasoning instead of seeking Allah's guidance? What concrete step will you take to change that today?
?
The dualists allowed harm to light for perceived benefit. Examine your heart: have you ever excused harming someone (even verbally) because you thought it was for a greater good? How does this lesson's teaching on conditional judgments correct that tendency?

Al-Warraq's Objection and Ibn al-Rawandi's Response on Reason as Proof
Objection and response on the authority of reason in prophetic legislation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn al-Rawandi defends the role of reason by showing that rational judgments are context-dependent, not inherently contradictory to revelation.

Core Idea
Reason's judgments change with context; messengers do not contradict reason.

Al-Warraq raises a powerful objection: if reason is a proof from Allah, how can revelation sometimes command what seems contrary to reason? Ibn al-Rawandi responds with a careful analogy—just as a head may appear black then white due to a change in the object, not the sight, so too rational judgments may change based on circumstances without reason itself being faulty. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for the relationship between reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the structure of objection and response. Identify who is speaking in each part.
The passage begins with al-Warraq's claim and then Ibn al-Rawandi's counterargument.
2
Understand the objection
Al-Warraq argues that if messengers come contrary to reason, then since Allah made reason a proof, any change would require altering reason itself, which nullifies the discourse. Grasp the logical dilemma he presents.
3
Examine the analogy
Ibn al-Rawandi uses the example of a head seen as black then white. Ask: does the sight change or the object? He argues the object changes, not the faculty. Similarly, what reason deems just can change without reason being flawed.
Note the Arabic terms: 'أتغير بصره أو تغير الشيء على البصر'.
4
Apply to reason and revelation
Understand how the analogy applies: rational judgments about actions like standing or sitting can vary in goodness depending on context. Therefore, a messenger's command that seems contrary to a previous rational judgment is not a contradiction.
The examples of cupping, eating, drinking show actions can be good or bad in different situations.
5
Connect to everyday life
Ibn al-Rawandi gives examples from trade, medicine, and surgery where people endure hardships for beneficial outcomes. This shows that choosing a seemingly harmful action for a greater good is rational. Similarly, religious laws may involve such choices.
This reinforces that reason does not change; its application adapts to circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي مثله ذكر الورّاق أن الرسل لو جاءوا إلى التمسك بحجج العقول فهم منا، وإن جاءوا إلى خلافها فقد جعلها الله حججًا، لم يجز الغير إلا بالتغيير، وفي ذلك زوال الخطاب. عارضه ابن الراوندي بما يرى المرء الرأس أسود ثم يراه أبيض، أتغير بصره أو تغير الشيء على البصر، إذ ليس هو بأسود لما يراه البصر؛ فمثله أمر ما يراه العقل عدلاً للأمر، وكذلك هذا في القيام والقعود وكل الأحوال. ومثله الحجامة والأكل والشرب، قد تحسن هذه الأحوال على اختلافها، ولم يجب به تغير العقل حتى يحسن فيه الذي كان يحسن بخلافه، فمثله أمر الرسل. ثم قد يجوز تحمل المؤن العظام لعواقب محمودة واختيار المضار لسلامة محمودة نحو التجارات والإجارات والزراعات والأدوية وأنواع الجراحات، وكذلك اختيار ترك النفع لنفع أرجح منه، وعلى ذلك أمر الشرائع. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And similarly al-Warraq mentioned that if the messengers came adhering to the proofs of minds, then they are from us, but if they came contrary to them, then Allah has made them proofs, so it is not permissible to change except through alteration, and in that is the nullification of address. Ibn al-Rawandi countered him with what a man sees: the head black then he sees it white; did his sight change or did the thing change upon the sight, since it is not black as the sight sees it? So the like of that is the matter of what reason sees as just for the matter, and likewise this in standing, sitting, and all states. And its like is cupping, eating, and drinking; these states may be good in their differences, and it is not necessary that reason change so that what was good in its opposite becomes good in it. So the like of that is the matter of the messengers. Then it may be permissible to endure great hardships for praiseworthy outcomes and to choose harms for praiseworthy safety, like trades, leases, agriculture, medicines, and various surgeries, and likewise choosing to leave aside a benefit for a more preponderant benefit, and upon that is the matter of religious laws. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage contains a further objection from al-Warraq and a response. Al-Warraq argues that if messengers came adhering to rational proofs, they are like us, but if they came contrary to them, then since Allah made reason a proof, any change would require alteration of reason, which would nullify the discourse. Ibn al-Rawandi responds with an analogy: a person sees a head as black, then sees it as white; has his sight changed, or has the thing itself changed? The point is that the object changes color, not the sense of sight. Similarly, what reason deems as just may change based on context, without reason itself being faulty. He gives examples: standing, sitting, cupping, eating, drinking – these acts may be good or bad depending on the situation, but reason does not change its fundamental capacity. Therefore, messengers bringing commands that seem contrary to a previous rational judgment is not a contradiction. Moreover, people routinely endure hardships for beneficial outcomes (e.g., trade, medicine, surgery), and religious laws also involve such choices.

Key Terms
حجج العقول
الورّاق
ابن الراوندي
تغير العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If reason is a proof from Allah, then revelation cannot contradict it under any circumstances.
Correction: Ibn al-Rawandi shows that while reason as a faculty is a proof, its specific judgments are context-dependent. A command that seems contradictory in one context may be rational in another, so there is no actual contradiction.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Ibn al-Rawandi use to respond to al-Warraq's objection about reason and revelation?
A) A head that appears black then white, showing the object changes, not the sight
B) A tree that bears fruit in different seasons
C) A river that changes course
D) A mirror that reflects different images

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Rawandi defends the compatibility of reason and revelation by showing that rational judgments are sensitive to context, not fixed absolutes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a command of Allah that you have struggled with because your reason told you otherwise? Could it be that your reason's judgment is context-dependent and that the command is for a greater benefit you don't yet see? What would it take for you to trust that benefit?
?
Ibn al-Rawindi uses the analogy of enduring hardships for beneficial outcomes, like surgery or trade. Think of a difficult act of worship or a moral obligation you face. Are you willing to endure that perceived harm for the sake of a greater spiritual benefit? What is holding you back?
?
Al-Warraq’s objection suggests that if revelation contradicts reason, then reason is invalid. Have you ever felt that your own reason is the ultimate judge, and when it clashes with revelation, you feel a tension? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider that assumption? Will you honestly examine whether your 'rational' judgments might be context-bound rather than absolute truths?

Maturidi's Final Classification: Good in Itself vs. Good by Consequence
Maturidi classifies things into two moral types and refutes al-Warraq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's twofold classification of things into those that are good in themselves and those whose goodness depends on need and consequences, and how this refutes al-Warraq's rationalist objection regarding slaughtering animals.

Core Idea
Not all good is absolute; some acts depend on need and consequences.

In this concluding passage, Maturidi delivers his definitive classification of moral judgments. Al-Warraq had argued that reason alone could judge all acts, but Maturidi shows that some actions, like slaughtering animals, change their moral status based on consequences and divine purpose. This distinction lays the foundation for understanding why revelation is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Warraq's objection
Understand al-Warraq's argument: reason condemns harming the innocent and teaches the golden rule; slaughtering animals seems to violate both.
Al-Warraq uses intuitive rational principles.
2
Maturidi's initial response
Notice Maturidi's reply: al-Warraq isolated the act from its causes and consequences. When one reflects on the good results (merciful death, ease, benefit), the judgment changes.
3
The twofold classification
Identify the two types: (1) things good in themselves, whose opposite and all contraries are bad; (2) things whose good or bad depends on need and evidence of consequences.
This is the core teaching of the lesson.
4
Apply classification to slaughter
See that slaughtering animals falls into the second type – its goodness is not intrinsic but depends on need and the evidence of good consequences.
5
Implication for reason vs. revelation
Conclude that only Allah, who fully knows the states of praise and blame, can legislate in such matters. Reliance on reason alone leads to contradictions.
This refutes al-Warraq's rationalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيّد الوراق الذي بينا بأن في العقل ذم الإساءة إلى من لم يؤذ وأن لم يحب لغيره ما يحب المرء لنفسه. والذبائح خارجة من ذلك؛ فهذا لأنه توهمه مفردًا من العلل، فإذا تأمل حسن العواقب والسلامة مع عقيب المنافع وكذلك فضل راحة يتغير حكمه، وفي الذبائح ذلك. ونحن نقول وبالله التوفيق: إن الأشياء نوعان؛ أحدهما مما يَحْسُن لنفسه ويقبح ضده وكل خلافاته. والثاني ما يحسن الشيء وخلافاته على حسب الحاجة وقيام الدلالة من حمد العواقب وذمها. فلزم القول في هذا بمن يعرف أحوال الحمد والذم فيخرج الأمر عليه. على أنه لا بد لمن يكون يعتمد على عقله من الاختلاف المتناقض الذي ذلك سببه، أو
And al-Warraq supported what we stated, that in reason is the condemnation of harming one who has not harmed and that one does not love for others what one loves for oneself. And slaughtering animals is outside of that; this is because he imagined it isolated from causes. If one reflects on the good consequences and safety along with the accompaniment of benefits, and likewise the virtue of ease, its ruling changes. And in slaughtering animals is that. And we say, and with Allah is success: Things are of two types: one type is that which is good in itself and its opposite and all its contraries are bad. The second is that which is good, the thing and its contraries, according to need and the establishment of evidence from the praise of consequences and their blame. So the statement in this necessarily pertains to Him who knows the states of praise and blame, and the matter issues from Him. Moreover, it is inevitable that one who relies on his reason [??] from contradictory difference the cause of which is that, or
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage contains al-Warraq's final objection and Maturidi's conclusive response. Al-Warraq appeals to universal rational principles: reason condemns harming the innocent and teaches the golden rule (do not do to others what you would not want for yourself). He argues that slaughtering animals violates these principles. Maturidi responds that al-Warraq has imagined slaughter isolated from its causes and consequences. When one reflects on the good consequences (merciful death, ease, benefit), the judgment changes. Then Maturidi gives a final classification: things are two types. First: things good in themselves, whose opposites and all contraries are bad (like truth and gratitude). Second: things whose good and bad depend on need and evidence of consequences (like many actions). Therefore, only One who fully knows the states of praise and blame can legislate in such matters. This refutes the idea that reason alone can judge all acts without revelation. The passage ends abruptly but emphasizes that reliance on reason alone leads to contradictions.

Key Terms
ذم الإساءة
الذبائح
نوعان
يَحْسُن لنفسه
حمد العواقب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational principles like 'do not harm' are absolute and cannot be overridden, so slaughtering animals is always wrong.
Correction: Maturidi argues that when consequences and need are considered, acts like slaughter can be good, as their evil is outweighed by benefit.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's final classification, how are things divided?
A) Into acts of worship and worldly acts
B) Into what is good in itself (with all contraries bad) and what is good based on consequences
C) Into rational and revealed
D) Into obligatory and prohibited

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's twofold classification shows that not all moral judgments are absolute; some depend on consequences and need, requiring divine guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral dilemma where two goods seem to conflict, do you instinctively trust your own reasoning, or do you seek Allah's guidance through revelation and scholarship? Which type of thing from Maturidi's classification does your situation fall into?
?
Al-Warraq assumed that harming animals is always wrong without considering the benefits. In your own life, are there actions you have condemned as 'wrong' without fully considering their context and consequences? Will you now pause to reflect before judging?
?
Maturidi says that only the One who knows all consequences can legislate. How does this truth affect your willingness to submit to Allah's commands even when you don't fully grasp the wisdom behind them? What is one area of your life where you need to submit more fully?

The Rational Basis of Sacrifice
Maturidi argues for the rationality of sacrifice and the justice of prophetic commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi grounds the practice of animal sacrifice in rational principles of discipline, mercy, and the role of prophets in defining just actions.

Core Idea
Sacrifice is rationally defensible as a discipline and mercy, and prophets only command justice.

Maturidi begins by asserting that reason itself disciplines the wrongdoer, making it permissible for divine law to impose similar acts. He then applies this reasoning to animal sacrifice, arguing that since all living beings die, slaughter is more merciful and thus akin to permissible acts. He further explains that while justice is inherently good and injustice inherently evil, some actions become good or evil through divine command, as seen in the changing states of human life and the example of prophets who only bring justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on discipline and sacrifice
Carefully read the first part of the passage where Maturidi discusses reason's role in disciplining the wrongdoer and the analogy to sacrifice.
Notice how he uses the concept of mercy to justify slaughter.
2
Understand the justice framework
Identify the distinction between actions that are inherently good/evil and those that become so by command, using the example of changing human states.
This is key to his argument about prophets and sacrifice.
3
Consider the role of prophets
Maturidi asserts that prophets only bring justice. Reflect on how this connects the previous points.
4
Review the Dualist reference
Briefly note the mention of the Dualists who allowed harm to light for benefit, showing that rational arguments can support similar positions.
This ties to the earlier point about reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في العقل تأديب المنهمك في الفساد الباغي على وجه الانتقام، فجائز ورود الشرع بمثله. وعلى ذلك أمر الذبائح، ولو قدر الإباحة فيه، بما كان كل حي يموت، والذبح أروح إليه وأيسر عليه، فيكون بمعنى الأشياء المباحة. والثاني أن العدل في الجملة حسن والجور في الجملة قبيح، لكن من الأشياء ما يظهر قبحه بالنهي وحسنه بالأمر، وذلك نحو تقلب أحوال المرء وانتقاله، وعلى ذلك ذبح الحيوان، إذ جاءت به الرسل، وهم لا يأتون إلا بالعدل. وعلى ذلك أمر الثنوية بما أجازوا للنور ضرر الظلمة لما رأى من المصلحة. بحجج العقول فهم منا، وإن وفي مثله ذكر الوراق أن الرسل لو جاءوا إلى التمسك جاءوا إلى خلافها فقد جعلها الله حججًا، لم يجز الغير إلا بالتغيير، وفي ذلك زوال الخطاب. عارضه ابن الروندي بما يرى المرء الرأس أسود ثم يراه أبيض، أتغير بصره أو تغير الشيء على البصر، إذ ليس هو بأسود لما يراه البصر؛ فمثله أمر ما يراه العقل عدلاً للأمر، وكذلك هذا في القيام والقعود وكل الأحوال. ومثله الحجامة والأكل والشرب، قد تحسن هذه الأحوال على اختلافها، ولم يجب به تغير العقل حتى يحسن فيه الذي كان يحسن بخلافه، فمثله أمر الرسل. ثم قد يجوز تحمل المؤن العظام لعواقب محمودة واختيار المضار لسلامة محمودة نحو التجارات والإجارات والزراعات والأدوية وأنواع الجراحات، وكذلك اختيار ترك النفع لنفع أرجح منه، وعلى ذلك أمر الشرائع. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
In reason is discipline of the one engrossed in corruption and transgressing by way of vengeance, so it is possible for divine law to come with something similar. And upon that is the matter of sacrifices; even if permissibility were assumed therein, since every living being dies, slaughter is more merciful to it and easier upon it, so it becomes in the sense of permissible things. And the second: justice in general is good and injustice in general is evil, but among things there are those whose evil appears by prohibition and whose good by command, such as the changing of a person's states and his transition, and upon that is the slaughter of animals, since the messengers brought it, and they only bring justice. And upon that is the matter of the Dualists in that they permitted harm to light from darkness because they saw benefit. By arguments of intellects they understand from us, and indeed similarly al-Warraq mentioned that if the messengers came to adhere [to reason?] and came contrary to it, then God has made arguments, it would not be permissible for the other except by change, and in that is nullification of discourse. Ibn al-Rawandi objected with that a person sees the head black then sees it white: did his sight change or the thing change upon the sight? Since it is not black according to what the sight sees; likewise is the matter of what reason sees as just for the matter, and similarly this in standing, sitting, and all conditions. And similarly cupping, eating, and drinking: these conditions may be good in their difference, and it does not necessitate change in reason such that what was good contrary to it becomes good in it; likewise is the matter of the messengers. Then it may be permissible to bear great burdens for praiseworthy outcomes and choose harms for a praiseworthy safety, such as trades, leases, agriculture, medicines, and types of surgeries, and similarly choosing to forgo benefit for a more preponderant benefit, and upon that is the matter of divine laws. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a multifaceted argument defending the rationality of sacrifice and the role of prophets. The author begins by stating that reason itself disciplines the wrongdoer who seeks vengeance, so it is permissible for divine law to come with similar commands. He applies this to animal sacrifice: since every living being dies anyway, slaughter is more merciful and thus resembles permissible things. He then distinguishes between things inherently good or evil and those that become good or evil due to command—like changing human states. Slaughter of animals is an example, as prophets bring only justice. He references the Dualists who allowed harm to light from darkness due to perceived benefit. Then he reports al-Warraq's view that if prophets came to uphold reason, they could not contradict it without nullifying discourse. Ibn al-Rawandi objects using perception: if a head appears black then white, has the sight changed or the thing? Similarly, what reason sees as just might change. The author answers with examples: cupping, eating, drinking—these actions can be good in different circumstances without reason changing. He further gives analogies like trade, farming, medicine, where people endure hardship for beneficial outcomes, even forgoing something for a greater benefit. Thus, divine laws are analogous to such reasoned choices. The phrase 'There is no power except through God' ends the section, acknowledging divine aid.

Key Terms
عقل
عدل
جور
ذبيحة
رسل
ثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the slaughter of animals in sacrifice just?
A) Because all animals are harmful and must be killed
B) Because prophets commanded it, and prophets only bring justice
C) Because it is inherently good regardless of command
D) Because reason alone determines it is obligatory

Key Takeaway
Sacrifice is rationally defensible because it aligns with reason's disciplinary function and the prophetic role of commanding only justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a divine command that seems difficult or harsh, does your heart immediately trust that it is just, or do you hesitate? How does Maturidi's argument about prophets only bringing justice challenge you to submit even when you don't see the wisdom?
?
Have you ever rationalized a sin by claiming it was 'merciful' or 'easier'? Maturidi shows that true mercy aligns with divine commands. In what area of your life are you most tempted to replace God's command with your own idea of mercy?
?
If you truly believed that every prophetic command is inherently just, how would that change the way you approach the obligations you find hardest to fulfill? What one sacrifice or duty have you been avoiding that you will now embrace as an act of justice?
