Answering Ibn al-Rawandi's Objection
Maturidi refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's objection using analogies from perception and practical life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's skeptical argument that prophetic commands could alter rational moral judgments, using analogies from perception, daily activities, and practical choices.

Core Idea
Rational judgments remain constant; prophetic commands align with reason, not change it.

Ibn al-Rawandi objects that just as sight can perceive an object differently (black then white), reason might change its judgment of justice, allowing prophets to command what was previously considered unjust. Maturidi responds by pointing to activities like cupping, eating, and drinking, which are good in different circumstances without requiring a change in reason. He further illustrates with practical choices like trade and medicine, where people endure hardship for greater benefit. Thus, prophetic laws are analogous to these reasoned choices, not a departure from reason.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn al-Rawandi's objection
Read the section beginning with 'عارضه ابن الروندي' to understand the objection based on perception change.
He uses the example of seeing a head black then white.
2
Analyze Maturidi's response
Identify the examples Maturidi gives: cupping, eating, drinking, standing, sitting. Notice how these actions are good in different contexts without reason changing.
This shows that prophetic commands do not require altering reason.
3
Consider the practical analogies
Examine the analogies from trade, agriculture, medicine, and surgeries. How do they support the point about enduring hardship for a greater benefit?
This shows that divine laws are like wise choices.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi ends by affirming that divine laws are based on such rational considerations. Note the closing phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله'.
This emphasizes reliance on God.

Al-Warraq's Rational Principle and Sacrifices
Maturidi presents al-Warraq's rational principle and its application to sacrifices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Warraq's rational principle against harming innocents applies to sacrifices, and why sacrifices are not considered blameworthy when considering their beneficial outcomes.

Core Idea
Reason condemns harming the innocent, but sacrifices are justified by their beneficial outcomes.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi reinforces an argument from al-Warraq about a fundamental rational principle: reason itself condemns harming someone who has not harmed you. However, sacrifices seem to violate this principle. Maturidi explains that when we consider the full context—the causes and consequences—sacrifices are actually excluded from this blame. Let's examine this nuanced point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to get the gist of al-Warraq's argument.
Notice the key terms like 'الذبائح' (sacrifices) and 'العلل' (causes).
2
Identify the rational principle
The passage states that reason blames harming one who did not harm, and that one does not love for others what one loves for oneself. This is a universal moral principle.
This principle is independent of revelation—it is inherent in reason.
3
Understand why sacrifices appear problematic
Sacrifices involve causing pain or death to an animal, which seems to violate the principle of not harming. The passage says they are 'excluded' from that blame.
The apparent conflict arises when sacrifices are viewed in isolation.
4
See how considering outcomes changes the judgment
Maturidi explains that if one reflects on the good outcomes (حسن العواقب), safety, and benefits that follow, the judgment changes. Sacrifices are not blameworthy because they lead to greater goods.
This is not pure consequentialism; reason itself recognizes the shift when context is considered.
5
Apply to sacrifices specifically
The passage concludes that the same logic applies to sacrifices: they cause temporary harm but yield long-term benefits, so they are not a violation of the rational principle.
This shows that moral judgments in kalam are contextual, not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيّد الوراق الذي بينا بأن في العقل ذم الإساءة إلى من لم يؤذ وأن لم يحب لغيره ما يحب المرء لنفسه. والذبائح خارجة من ذلك؛ فهذا لأنه توهمه مفردًا من العلل، فإذا تأمل حسن العواقب والسلامة مع عقيب المنافع وكذلك فضل راحة [يتغير حكمه]، وفي الذبائح ذلك.
And al-Warraq supported what we have shown: that in reason is blame of harming one who did not harm, and that one does not love for others what one loves for oneself. And sacrifices are excluded from that; this is because he imagined it isolated from causes, but when one reflects on the goodness of outcomes and safety along with the sequel of benefits, as well as the virtue of ease, [its judgment changes], and in sacrifices that is the case.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This short passage presents al-Warraq's endorsement of the author's earlier argument. The author states that al-Warraq supports the view that reason inherently condemns harming someone who has not harmed you, and that the golden rule ('do not love for others what you love for yourself') is a rational principle. Sacrifices, however, are excluded from this condemnation because if one mistakenly views them in isolation from their causes and consequences, they seem harmful. But when one reflects on the beneficial outcomes—such as the safety and eventual relief that come from the act—its judgment changes. The same applies to sacrifices: they cause momentary pain but lead to greater benefits, so they are not blameworthy.

Key Terms

الإساءة
الذبائح
العلل
العواقب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might take this as a purely consequentialist argument where only outcomes matter, ignoring the intrinsic rational principle.
Correction: The passage maintains that reason itself recognizes the principle of not harming innocents; sacrifices are an exception due to their positive outcomes, but the principle remains valid.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why are sacrifices excluded from the rational principle of not harming others?
A) Because animals are not innocent
B) Because when considering the beneficial outcomes, the judgment changes
C) Because reason does not apply to animals
D) Because the prophets commanded it without reason

Key Takeaway
Sacrifices are not a violation of the rational principle against harming innocents because their beneficial consequences change their moral judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult decision that involves temporary hardship, do you automatically judge it as bad, or do you pause to reflect on the long-term benefits that might change its moral value? How can you practice al-Warraq's reflection in your life starting today?
?
Is there a person or situation you have condemned because of an initial painful action, without considering the full picture of outcomes? How does this lesson compel you to reevaluate that judgment before Allah?
?
Your love for yourself includes avoiding harm and seeking benefit. This lesson shows that even rational principles bend for greater good. Where in your life are you holding onto rigid rules, refusing to see God's wisdom in allowing temporary pain for eternal gain? What step will you take to trust His plan more deeply?

Two Types of Actions: Intrinsic and Consequential
Maturidi classifies actions into two types: those good/evil in themselves and those dependent on outcomes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of actions into those that are intrinsically good/evil and those that depend on outcomes, and why this leads to the necessity of prophetic guidance.

Core Idea
Actions are not all judged the same; some are good in themselves, others depend on outcomes.

Maturidi begins by laying out a foundational classification of actions that shapes the entire discussion of moral judgment in theology. He distinguishes between actions that are inherently good or evil and those whose moral value depends on their consequences and context. This passage introduces that crucial distinction and applies it to the example of sacrifices.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once without analyzing. Let the author's own classification come through.
Notice the two categories are introduced immediately.
2
Identify the two types
The passage explicitly states that things are of two types. Type 1: good/evil in themselves. Type 2: good/evil based on need and outcomes. Mark these in the text.
Type 1 includes all alternatives; type 2 is flexible.
3
Follow the argument from type 2 to necessity of a prophet
After stating type 2, Maturidi argues that one must refer to someone who knows praise and blame. Then he applies this to sacrifices, showing they cannot be intrinsically evil, leading to permissibility.
The commentary will explain the logical steps.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نقول وبالله التوفيق: إن الأشياء نوعان؛ أحدهما مما يَحْسُن لنفسه ويقبح ضده وكل خلافاته. والثاني ما يحسن الشيء وخلافاته على حسب الحاجة وقيام الدلالة من حمد العواقب وذمها. فلزم القول في هذا بمن يعرف أحوال الحمد والذم فيخرج الأمر عليه. على أنه لا بد لمن يكون يعتمد على عقله من الاختلاف المتناقض [الذي] ذلك سببه، أو يرجع إلى مخصوص من العقل، وفي ذلك القول بالرسول. ثم أمر الذبائح لا يحتمل أن يكون قبحها لنفسها لما يحل في موضع الانتقام، ويحسن في العقول إذا تفكر في ذلك دفع الأذى والمكروه، أو نفع العواقب، فبطل قبح ذلك لنفسه، فلزم جواز المحنة فيه بالترك والإذن، وفي ذلك إباحة.
And we say, and by God is success: indeed things are of two types: one of them is that which is good in itself and its opposite and all its contraries are evil. And the second is that the thing and its contraries are good according to need and the establishment of evidence from praise or blame of outcomes. So it is necessary to speak about this with one who knows the states of praise and blame, and the matter issues from him. However, one who relies on his reason inevitably faces contradictory difference, the cause of that, or he must return to something specific to reason, and that necessitates accepting the messenger. Then the matter of sacrifices: it is impossible that their evil be in themselves because [they occur] in a situation of vengeance, and it is considered good in intellects if one reflects on repelling harm and disliked things, or benefit of outcomes. So the belief that they are evil in themselves is invalidated, and thus the permissibility of testing in them by omission and permission is established, and that entails permissibility.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of actions into two categories. The author begins by saying that things (الأشياء) are of two types. First: what is good in itself and its opposite and all contrary alternatives are evil. These are intrinsically good or evil. Second: what is good or its contrary may be good depending on need and the evidence of praiseworthy or blameworthy outcomes. This includes actions like sacrifice that can vary. He then states that one must refer to someone who knows the states of praise and blame (i.e., a prophet) to determine the ruling. He explains that relying solely on reason leads to contradictory differences, and the only way out is to return to a specific rational principle or to accept the messenger. Then he applies this to sacrifices: they cannot be evil in themselves because they occur in a context of retaliation, yet reflection shows they repel harm and bring benefit. Therefore, the notion of intrinsic evil is nullified, and the matter becomes permissible (إباحة) based on divine permission or prohibition.

Key Terms
الأشياء
نوعان
يحسن لنفسه
حمد العواقب
الرسول
الذبائح
إباحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author denies intrinsic evil entirely.
Correction: Maturidi affirms intrinsic evil for the first category, while placing sacrifice in the second category, which depends on outcomes.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's classification, what is the determining factor for actions in the second category?
A) Their intrinsic nature
B) The need and evidence of praiseworthy or blameworthy outcomes
C) Rational deduction alone
D) The consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's classification distinguishes intrinsically moral actions from those whose morality depends on consequences, leading to the necessity of prophetic guidance for the latter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there actions in your life that you consider unquestionably right or wrong without ever stopping to ask whether God's revelation might say otherwise? What would it cost you to submit your judgment to divine command?
?
Maturidi argues that relying solely on reason leads to contradictory differences. When you face a moral dilemma, do you truly seek guidance from revelation, or do you trust your own reasoning and then look for scriptural support? Be honest.
?
The passage shows that even sacrifices—acts of worship—are not intrinsically evil or good but depend on God's permission. Is there any practice you have dismissed as wrong without considering that it might be permitted by God in certain contexts? What if your intuition is not the final judge?

Distinguishing Rational and Natural Prohibitions
Maturidi distinguishes rational and natural evil using habituation as evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between rational and natural prohibitions, and how natural aversions can be changed by habit.

Core Idea
Natural aversions are changeable; rational judgments are not.

This lesson examines Maturidi's argument that the prohibition of sacrifice is rooted in natural aversion, not rational judgment. He shows that while rational judgments are fixed, natural aversions can be overcome through habituation. This distinction helps clarify the role of reason and nature in Islamic ethics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between 'عقلي' and 'طبيعي'.
2
Identify the two types of evil
The passage distinguishes between evil that reason deems evil (fixed) and evil due to natural aversion (changeable). Find where each is discussed.
Maturidi uses 'القبيح من العقل' vs 'قبح لنفار الطبع'.
3
Understand the role of habituation
The key evidence is the example of butchers and those accustomed to fighting. Ask yourself: why does this show the prohibition is natural?
Habituation does not change rational judgments, only natural aversions.
4
Connect to the overall argument
Maturidi concludes that the prohibition of sacrifice is natural, not rational, and can change through habit. Relate this to the broader theme of divine commands altering natural dispositions.
This argument supports the idea that some prohibitions are based on human nature, not inherent rational evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن كل شيء حسنه العقل فهو لا يقبح بحال، وكذلك القبيح من العقل. وكل قبح لنفار الطبع [فإنما هو كذلك] بما يتوهم حلوله في جوهر المتوهم فينفر طبعه لألمه. ثم قد يجوز أن يذهب ذلك بالاعتياد نحو القصابين والذين اعتادوا القتال. فثبت أن النهي عنه طبيعي لا عقلي، فتغير ذلك من العادة. وذلك نحو جواهر من الحيوان طبعه التوحش، وعلى ذلك طبع الجميع عن الأحمال الثقيلة، ثم تصير بالرياضة وتعويد غيره كأنها على ذلك طبعت، فعلى ذلك أمر الحيوان.
And also, everything that reason deems good is never evil, and likewise the evil [deemed] by reason. And every evil due to natural aversion is only because of what is imagined to befall the substance of the imaginer, so his nature recoils due to its pain. Then it may be possible that this goes away by habituation, such as butchers and those accustomed to fighting. So it is established that the prohibition concerning it is natural, not rational, and that changes through habit. And that is like substances of animals whose nature is wildness, and similarly all are averse to heavy loads, then by exercise and accustoming to other things they become as if they were created that way. So upon that is the matter of animals.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between rational and natural prohibitions. The author begins by stating that whatever reason deems good is never evil, and whatever reason deems evil is always evil. But there is another kind of evil: one that arises from natural aversion (نفار الطبع). This happens when one imagines something befalling their own substance and feels pain. However, such natural aversion can be overcome by habituation, as seen with butchers and those accustomed to fighting. Therefore, the prohibition (i.e., against killing animals) is natural, not rational. This natural aversion can change through habit, just as wild animals can become tame through training, and all animals are initially averse to heavy loads but adapt through repeated practice. So the prohibition of sacrifice is not a matter of reason but of natural disposition, which can be altered by divine command or habit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reason plays no role in ethical judgment.
Correction: Maturidi does not reject reason's role; he distinguishes between rational judgments (absolute) and natural aversions (mutable). Reason still judges the aversion as evil due to harm.

Checkpoint

A) Butchers
B) Scholars
C) Prophets
D) Animals in the wild

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the prohibition of sacrifice is based on natural aversion, not rational judgment, and such aversions can be altered by habituation.

?
The passage says butchers become desensitized through repetition. Are there places in your daily life where you have become comfortable with something that initially disturbed you? What does that say about your moral compass?
?
If you were to test your own reactions—distinguishing what you consider rationally evil from what merely feels repulsive—what concrete step could you take today to ensure your reason, not just your nature, guides your choices?

Rational Judgment and Divine Permission in Slaughter
Maturidi argues that moral judgments are based on nature and habit, not reason alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the ugliness or goodness of actions is not intrinsic but depends on rational judgment and divine permission, and that natural aversion can be changed by habituation.

Core Idea
Moral value is assigned by reason and revelation, not fixed in actions.

This passage from Maturidi tackles a fundamental question: is an act like slaughter inherently good or evil? He argues that our sense of ugliness often stems from natural aversion rather than rational judgment, and that divine permission can override even strong instincts. As you read, notice how he distinguishes between rational and natural categories, and uses examples from daily life to ground his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage aloud
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for Maturidi's style. Don't worry about full understanding yet; just notice the key terms like 'حسن عقلي', 'قبح عقلي', 'نفار الطبع', and 'إذن'.
This passage is dense, so take it slowly.
2
Identify the main claims
The passage makes two key distinctions: between rational good/evil and natural aversion, and between intrinsic ugliness and permitted actions. Underline or note where Maturidi argues that slaughter is not intrinsically ugly.
3
Examine the habituation argument
Maturidi uses the examples of butchers and soldiers to show that natural aversion can be overcome by habit. Consider how this supports his claim that the prohibition of slaughter is natural, not rational.
Think about your own experiences with things that initially seemed repulsive but became normal.
4
Connect to divine permission
The passage concludes that since all living things die, no blame attaches when permission comes from the rightful owner (God). This is the key theological move: permissibility is grounded in divine authorization.
This links to the broader Islamic principle that actions are judged by divine command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرجع إلى مخصوص من العقل، وفي ذلك القول بالرسول. ثم أمر الذبائح لا يحتمل أن يكون قبحها لنفسها لما يحل في موضع الانتقام، ويحسن في العقول إذا تفكر في ذلك دفع الأذى والمكروه، أو نفع العواقب، فبطل قبح ذلك لنفسه، فلزم جواز المحنة فيه بالترك والإذن، وفي ذلك إباحة. وأيضا إن كل شيء حسنه العقل فهو لا يقبح بحال، وكذلك القبيح من العقل. وكل قبح لنفار الطبع فإنما هو كذلك بما يتوهم حلوله في جوهر المتوهم فينفر طبعه لألمه. ثم قد يجوز أن يذهب ذلك بالاعتياد نحو القصابين والذين اعتادوا القتال. فثبت أن النهي عنه طبيعي لا عقلي، فتغير ذلك من العادة. وذلك نحو جواهر من الحيوان طبعه التوحش، وعلى ذلك طبع الجميع عن الأحمال الثقيلة، ثم تصير بالرياضة وتعويد غيره كأنها على ذلك طبعت، فعلى ذلك أمر الحيوان وأيضا إن كل حي إذ هو يموت، ثم لم يلحق أحدا لائمة، فمثله إذا جاء الإذن ممن هو له.
It refers to something specific from reason, and in that is the statement by the Messenger. Then the matter of sacrifices: it is not possible that their ugliness is due to themselves, because what occurs in the place of retribution, and is considered good in minds when reflection occurs—warding off harm and dislike, or benefiting outcomes—so the ugliness of that due to itself is invalidated; therefore the permissibility of testing it by abandonment and permission is necessary, and in that is permission. And also, everything that reason considers good is not ugly in any case, and likewise what reason considers ugly. And every ugliness due to natural aversion is only so because of the imagined occurrence of it in the substance of the one imagining, so his nature recoils due to its pain. Then it may be that that goes away by habituation, like butchers and those accustomed to fighting. So it is established that the prohibition of it is natural, not rational, and that changes by habit. And that is like the substances of animals whose nature is wildness, and upon that all are naturally disposed against heavy burdens; then they become by training and accustoming to other things as if they were created upon that. So similarly is the matter of the animal. And also, every living thing when it dies, then no blame attaches to anyone; so likewise when permission comes from the one to whom it belongs.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the basis of moral judgments (ḥusn and qubḥ). Maturidi argues that judgments of good and evil are not intrinsic to actions but are determined by reason and revelation. He first notes that the matter of sacrifices cannot have intrinsic ugliness because what appears ugly in one context (retribution) can be considered good when it leads to avoiding harm or gaining benefit. Thus the ugliness is not inherent; it depends on divine permission. He then supports this by stating that whatever reason deems good is never ugly, and vice versa. However, ugliness due to natural aversion (nifār al-ṭabʿ) is not rational but instinctive, and such aversion can be overcome by habituation, like butchers or soldiers. Therefore, the prohibition of slaughter is natural, not rational, and can change through custom. He illustrates with animals: wild animals naturally dislike burdens but can be trained. Similarly, the act of slaughter is permissible by divine command, and since all living things die, no blame attaches when permission is given by the rightful owner (God). The point is that moral categories (good/evil) are not fixed in the objects themselves but are assigned by reason and revelation, and even natural repugnance can be overridden by divine permission.

Key Terms
حسن عقلي
قبح عقلي
نفار الطبع
اعتياد
الذبائح
الإذن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the permissibility of slaughter means it is morally neutral or that reason alone can judge it.
Correction: Maturidi grounds permissibility in divine permission and natural habit, not in intrinsic morality or reason alone.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the ugliness of something like slaughter not intrinsic to the act itself?
A) Because it is always considered good by reason.
B) Because it can be permissible when permitted by the rightful owner and can become habitual.
C) Because all actions are neither good nor evil.
D) Because the act itself is physically harmful.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that moral value is not intrinsic to actions but is determined by rational judgment and divine permission, and that natural aversion can be overcome by habituation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moral conviction you hold strongly—is it grounded in rational principle, or is it merely a natural aversion of your temperament? If God's command opposed that conviction, would you be willing to obey despite your natural recoil?
?
Have you ever judged an action as inherently wrong, only to later realize it was just a matter of habit or culture? How does Maturidi's argument challenge you to re-examine such judgments?
?
Consider something you find repulsive by nature (like consuming a certain food or performing a certain task). If divine permission came for it, would your natural aversion still prevent you? What would that say about the source of your moral compass?

The Rational Proof of Temporality and Its Application to Jesus
Maturidi argues that the proof of temporal origination of bodies is rational, not scriptural, and applies to Jesus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof of temporal origination must be based on reason rather than revelation, and how this argument undermines claims about Jesus's divinity.

Core Idea
The proof of temporality cannot depend on revelation without circularity — reason must be the foundation.

In this passage from Kitab al-Tawhid, Imam Maturidi presents a razor-sharp rational argument against those who would rely solely on scripture to prove the temporality of bodies. He shows that if temporality is only known through revelation, and revelation's truth is only known through temporality, the argument spins in circles. The only escape is to accept reason as the ultimate proof — and once reason is accepted, it applies equally to Jesus, refuting claims of his divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Try to grasp the overall flow of argument before diving into details.
Notice the compact style — every sentence carries weight.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage has two sections: first, the rational proof and its application to Jesus, and second, a rebuttal to an honorific objection about Jesus being called 'son'.
3
Trace the logical dilemma
Follow Maturidi's argument step by step: if the opponent says the proof is scriptural, then a circularity arises. Understand why this forces acceptance of reason.
Circularity: temporality known only by revelation, truth of revelation known only by temporality.
4
Examine the honor objection
Read the second part where Maturidi responds to the claim that calling Jesus 'son' is an honor. See how he flips it by pointing out that 'father' is greater honor and that 'son' might imply seniority.
This part refutes a common Christian proof for Jesus's exalted status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : ثم نتكلم في دليل حدث الأجسام، فإن صيّره العقل لزمه في عيسى ذلك . فإن قال السمع، قيل : ودليل صدق المسموع ما هو ؟ فإن قال : حدوث الأشياء، يصير حدوث الأشياء لا يُعلم إلا بالسمع، وصدقه لا يُعلم إلا بحدوث الأشياء، فانقطع سبيل معرفة فيها إلا أن يقر بالعقل، فيلزمه ذلك في المسيح.

ثم عارض من يقول : ليس من الإكرام أعظم من قوله : «يا بُنَيَّ». قيل: بلى، يا أبي أكبر في التعظيم و [لكن] لو قال: يوجب التقدم، أبطل اعتباره بالتعظيم، لأنه من ذلك.
Issue: Then we discuss the proof of the temporality of bodies. If reason makes it (temporal), then that follows for Jesus. If he says: (by) revelation, it is said: What is the proof of the truth of the revealed? If he says: The temporality of things, then temporality of things is not known except by revelation, and its truth is not known except by temporality of things — so the path of knowledge in it is cut off, unless he acknowledges reason, then that follows for Jesus.

Then one who says: There is no greater honor than His saying: 'O my son' is opposed. It is said: Yes, 'O my father' is greater in veneration, but if he said: it necessitates seniority, then his consideration by veneration is invalidated, because it is from that.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument for the temporal origination of bodies and applies it to Jesus. The author states that if the proof for temporality is rational, then Jesus must also be temporal (and therefore not divine). If the opponent says the proof is from revelation, then a dilemma arises: the truth of revelation depends on the temporality of things, but temporality is known only through revelation, leading to circularity. The only escape is to accept reason, which then applies to Jesus. The second part addresses an objection: some claim that Jesus being called 'son' (in the Gospel) is an honor. The author counters that calling someone 'father' is greater honor, and the term 'son' might imply seniority/priority, which would undermine the honor argument. Thus the arguments for Jesus's divinity are refuted.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of temporal origination can be purely scriptural without circularity.
Correction: If temporality is known only by revelation and revelation's truth only by temporality, the argument is circular. Reason must be the ultimate judge.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The proof of temporal origination must be based on reason, not revelation, to avoid circularity, and this rational proof applies to Jesus as well, refuting claims of his divinity.

The Proof of Contingency Applied to Christ
Maturidi presents a dilemma argument on Christ's contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the proof of contingency of bodies to argue that Christ is created, and why appealing to revelation leads to circular reasoning.

Core Idea
The contingency argument cannot be escaped: it either applies directly or exposes circularity.

Maturidi now applies the well-known proof of the contingency of bodies to the specific case of Christ. The Christian may try to avoid the conclusion by claiming the proof relies on revelation rather than reason. But Maturidi shows that this move leads to a vicious circle, cutting off knowledge entirely. The only consistent path is to accept the rational proof, which then requires acknowledging Christ as created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two conditional statements: 'if he makes it rational' and 'if he says it is from revelation'. This structure frames the dilemma.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
Trace what happens in each branch: the rational branch leads to the conclusion that Christ is contingent. The revealed branch leads to circularity between revelation and contingency.
The key terms are 'العقل' (reason) and 'السمع' (revelation).
3
Understand the circularity argument
Maturidi points out that if contingency is known only through revelation, and revelation's truth is known only through contingency, then neither can be established. This is a classic logical circle.
The phrase 'فانقطع سبيل المعرفة' (so the way of knowledge is cut off) is the core of the critique.
4
Apply the conclusion to Christ
In either branch, the proof ultimately applies to Christ as a body, so he must be contingent and thus created. Reflect on why this argument is effective against a Christian interlocutor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: ثم نتكلم في دليل حدث الأجسام، فإن صيّره العقل لزمه في عيسى ذلك. فإن قال السمع، قيل : ودليل صدق المسموع ما هو ؟ فإن قال : حدوث الأشياء، يصير حدوث الأشياء لا يُعلم إلا بالسمع، وصدقه لا يُعلم إلا بحدوث الأشياء، فانقطع سبيل المعرفة فيها إلا أن يقر بالعقل، فيلزمه ذلك في المسيح.
Issue: Then we speak about the proof of the contingency of bodies. If he makes it rational, then that applies to Christ. If he says it is from revelation, it is said: And what is the proof of the truth of what is heard? If he says: The contingency of things, then the contingency of things is not known except through revelation, and its truth is not known except through the contingency of things, so the way of knowledge is cut off in it unless he admits by reason, then that applies to Christ.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about the proof of the contingency of bodies (حدوث الأجسام) and its application to Christ. Maturidi examines two possibilities: if the Christian accepts that the proof is rational, then it applies to Christ as a body, so Christ must be created. If the Christian claims the proof is based on revelation (السمع), then a circularity arises: the truth of revelation is known by the contingency of bodies, but the contingency of bodies is known only through revelation. This circularity cuts off knowledge unless one admits the proof by reason. In either case, Christ is subject to the proof and thus created. The argument forces the Christian to either accept Christ's createdness or fall into circular reasoning.

Key Terms
دليل حدث الأجسام
السمع
العقل
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof of contingency only applies to inanimate objects, not to human bodies like Christ's.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly applies the proof to Christ as a body ('جسم'), showing that all bodies, including Christ's, are contingent and created.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue happens if the Christian claims the proof of contingency is based on revelation?
A) It leads to circular reasoning.
B) It proves revelation is false.
C) Christ is exempt from the proof.
D) Reason is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The proof of contingency cannot be avoided: either it applies directly via reason, or it leads to circularity, ultimately compelling the conclusion that Christ is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a religious belief simply because someone authoritative told you it was true, without checking its logical consistency? How does Maturidi’s exposure of circular reasoning in this passage challenge you to rely more on rational proofs in your own faith? Commit today to learning one rational proof for a core Islamic belief (like the existence of God) so that your faith is not built on blind acceptance alone.
?
When you encounter someone who holds a different belief (religious or secular), do you immediately dismiss them or engage rationally? Maturidi here does not simply reject the Christian view; he uses logic to show its internal problem. Are you willing to learn the arguments of those who disagree with you so that you can respond with reason rather than emotion? This week, choose one objection to Islam and find a rational response to it.
?
The passage shows that without reason, knowledge is 'cut off'. In your own life, are there areas where you avoid thinking deeply and rely on comfortable traditions? How would your relationship with Allah improve if you actively used your intellect to understand His signs? Take time tonight to reflect on one of Allah’s attributes and reason how it leads to tawhid.

The Distinction Between Rational Ugliness and Conventional Ugliness
Maturidi distinguishes ugliness by reason from ugliness by custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between ugliness by custom (which can change through habit) and ugliness by reason (which is absolute and unchanging).

Core Idea
Rational ugliness is absolute; conventional ugliness can become good by habit.

Maturidi presents a critical distinction for understanding divine actions: some things are considered ugly only by human custom, and these can become acceptable through habit. But other things are rationally ugly and never change. This passage sets the foundation for why God's actions cannot be judged by human moral conventions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let Maturidi's own phrasing introduce the distinction.
Notice the sharp contrast introduced by 'wa laysa dhalika ka...'.
2
Identify the two types of ugliness
The passage presents two types: ugliness by nature (tab') and ugliness by reason ('aql). Find how each is defined.
Ugliness by nature is described as changeable through habit.
3
Examine the example of slaughter and animal training
Maturidi uses slaughter and taming of wild animals as proof that natural ugliness can become good through habit and training.
Consider how this challenges the assumption that all ugliness is permanent.
4
Grasp the key point: rational ugliness is permanent
Understand that rational ugliness never becomes good; it only increases in repulsiveness upon reflection.
This is the foundation for Maturidi's argument about divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر فيه إلا فُحشا . وليس ذلك كالقبيح بالطبع، إن ذلك [قد] يصير حسنا بالاعتياد وطول الصحبة كالذبح وأنواع ذلك. وكذلك نجد جواهر الدواب والسباع والطيور مستوحشة عن الناس بالطباع نافرة عما يراد بها من أنواع المكاسب والأعمال؛ ثم تخرج عنها بالرياضة والتعليم حتى تألف بالذي كان تنفر عنه ، ويصير ذلك له كأنه الطباع المجبول. ولا يكون الذي قبح بالعقل بهذا الوصف أبدا، بل يزداد على طول النظر في شأنه.
Consideration of it is only foulness. And that is not like ugliness by nature, for that [sometimes] becomes good by habit and prolonged association, like slaughter and its varieties. Similarly, we find the essences of beasts, predators, and birds instinctively averse to humans, shunning what is intended from them of various gains and works; then they are brought out of that by training and education until they become accustomed to that which they used to shun, and that becomes for them as if it were innate nature. But that which is ugly by reason never has this description; rather, it increases with prolonged consideration of its condition.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage defines two types of ugliness: conventional (by custom) and rational. Maturidi explains that conventional ugliness, like slaughter, can become good through habit and prolonged association, just as animals can be tamed. However, what is inherently ugly by reason never changes; it only becomes more repulsive upon reflection. This distinction sets up the argument that God's actions cannot be judged by human conventions.

Key Terms

قبيح بالعقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all moral ugliness is relative and can change with custom.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that rational ugliness is absolute and unchanging, unlike conventional ugliness.

B) Rational ugliness can become good by habit.
C) Both are permanent and unchanging.
D) Neither is real; all ugliness is relative.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi distinguishes two kinds of ugliness: one that can change through habit and one that is permanent by reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Human Nature and the Training of Intellect
Maturidi explains that nature opposes intellect but can be trained.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that human nature, though initially opposed to intellect, can be disciplined to align with rational judgments, and that intellect is the ultimate proof.

Core Idea
Nature can be trained; intellect is the decisive proof.

Maturidi begins by describing the inherent conflict between human nature, which is inclined towards immediate pleasures and aversion to pain, and the intellect, which judges good and evil. He argues that while intellect's judgments are stable, nature's inclinations can be transformed through discipline. This passage uses vivid examples from animal training to illustrate how even deep-seated aversions can be overcome. Understanding this dynamic is crucial for grasping the role of intellect as the divine proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Maturidi sets up the contrast between nature and intellect.
The passage is incomplete but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on the terms طبيعة (nature), عقل (intellect), تحسين (deeming good), تقبيح (deeming evil), and رياضة (discipline). Understand their roles.
3
Analyze the argument
Maturidi says nature makes things seem good or bad based on pleasure/pain, while intellect judges based on unchanging criteria. Nature's judgments can change with training; intellect's cannot.
Note the stability of intellect vs. changeability of nature.
4
Consider the examples
Maturidi gives examples: birds and beasts can be trained to overcome their natural aversion; similarly, humans can overcome aversion to killing. Reflect on how this supports his point.
5
Understand the conclusion
The final statement: intellect is the proof (hujja), not nature. God holds people accountable based on intellect, not natural inclinations.
This is the key takeaway for Islamic theology.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصل ذلك أن الله تعالى خلق البشر على طبائع تميل إلى الملاذ الحاضرة، وتدعو صاحبها إليها، وتزيّنها في عينه، بما ركب فيه من الشهوات إلى ما إليه ميل طبعه، وهي تنفر عما فيه ألمه وتعب فيصير طبعه أحد أعداء عقله في التحسين والتقبيح. وإن كان ما حسنه العقل وقبحه ليس له زوال ولا تغير من حال إلى حال، وما حسنته الطبيعة وقبحته هو في حد الانقلاب والتغير عن حال إلى حال بالرياضة والقيام على ذلك بالكف عما ألفه، والصرف إلى ما ينفر عنه بحسن القيام عليه على ما يحتمل الطبع قبوله نحو المعروف من أمر الطيور والبهائم: إنها بطبعها تنفر عما أريد بها من أنواع منافع البشر، ثم بحسن قيام أهل البصر بذلك يصير مما طبع عليه بالميل إليه كالمستوحش ومما طبع على النفار عنه كالمطبوع عليه. وعلى ذلك أمر نفار الطبع عن القتل والذبح في البشر، ثم سهولة ذلك عليه. وما يدرك حسنه بالعقل أو قبحه، فلا يزال يزداد على ما فيه إدراكه ببديهة الأحوال. ولذلك جعل الله العقول حجة لا ميل الطباع؛ إذ أجرى قلمه على أهلها، وإن شاركوا في الطباع غيرهم ممن
And the origin of that is that God Almighty created humans with natures that incline towards immediate pleasures, and call their possessor to them, and beautify them in his eye, due to what He placed in him of desires towards that to which his nature inclines, and they recoil from what contains pain and fatigue, so his nature becomes one of the enemies of his intellect in deeming good and evil. And although what the intellect deems good and evil has no removal or change from state to state, what nature deems good and evil is in the realm of transformation and change from state to state through discipline and standing firm upon that by refraining from what he is accustomed to, and turning towards what he recoils from through good standing upon it, as much as the nature can accept, like the known matter of birds and beasts: they by nature recoil from what is intended of the types of human benefits, then through the good management of the people of insight they become, from what was naturally inclined to, like something familiar, and from what was naturally averse, like something natural. And similarly is the matter of the natural aversion to killing and slaughter in humans, then its ease upon him. And what is perceived as good or evil by the intellect only increases in its perception through the intuitive nature of states. For that reason, God made intellects a proof, not the inclination of natures; for He applied His pen upon their possessors, even if they share in nature with others who...
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage delves into the conflict between human nature (tab') and intellect ('aql). God created humans with natures inclined to immediate pleasures and averse to pain. This makes nature an enemy of intellect in the discernment of good and evil. However, while intellect's judgments are stable and unchanging, nature's judgments can be altered through discipline and training, just as wild animals can be tamed. Maturidi gives the example of birds and beasts that initially recoil from human benefits but can be trained to accept them. Similarly, humans can overcome their natural aversion to killing and slaughter. Ultimately, intellect is the decisive proof (hujja), not natural inclinations, because God has appointed intellect as the criterion. Those who possess intellect are under divine obligation, even if they share their nature with others.

عقل
حجة
تحسين
تقبيح
رياضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human nature is an unchangeable excuse for sin.
Correction: Maturidi argues that nature can be trained and intellect is the judge.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the relationship between nature and intellect?
A) Nature is always superior.
B) Intellect is the enemy of nature.
C) Nature can be trained to align with intellect.
D) They are independent.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that while human nature initially opposes intellect, it can be disciplined, and intellect remains the ultimate criterion for good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which desire or habit in your life today is contradicting what your intellect knows is right? What is one concrete action you will take this week to discipline that desire?
?
This lesson says nature can be trained like animals. Identify one area where you have used 'it's just my nature' as an excuse. How will you now train that nature?
?
If the intellect is the decisive proof, then your intellect is enough. What moral truth have you been avoiding that your intellect already knows? Will you commit to acting on it today?

Nature versus Reason in Moral Judgment
Maturidi explains the root conflict between nature and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the inherent conflict between human nature and reason in moral judgment, and why reason must be the ultimate arbiter.

Core Idea
Nature inclines to pleasure; reason reveals truth.

Maturidi begins by explaining that Allah created humans with natural inclinations toward immediate pleasures, which often oppose the judgments of reason. This conflict is the root of many confusions in moral and theological understanding. In this passage, we explore how nature can be trained, but reason remains the stable criterion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Pay attention to the contrast between 'tabʿ' (nature) and 'ʿaql' (reason).
2
Identify the contrast between nature and reason
Note how Maturidi characterizes the judgments of nature as changeable through discipline, while reason's judgments are stable and unchanging.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the examples: training animals, overcoming aversion to killing, and variations in speech. They illustrate how nature can be reshaped but reason remains constant.
Consider why Maturidi uses the example of birds and beasts.
4
Understand the conclusion
Recognize that reason is like direct visual knowledge, and when nature dominates, people confuse the clear with the ambiguous. The lesson ends with a prayer for protection.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصل ذلك أن الله تعالى خلق البشر على طبائع تميل إلى الملاذ الحاضرة، وتدعو صاحبها إليها، وتزيّنها في عينه، بما ركب فيه من الشهوات إلى ما إليه ميل طبعه، وهي تنفر عما فيه ألمه وتعبته فيصير طبعه أحد أعداء عقله في التحسين والتقبيح. وإن كان ما حسنه العقل وقبحه ليس له زوال ولا تغير من حال إلى حال وما حسنته الطبيعة وقبحته هو في حد الانقلاب والتغير عن حال إلى حال بالرياضة والقيام على ذلك بالكف عما ألفه، والصرف إلى ما ينفر عنه بحسن القيام عليه على ما يحتمل الطبع قبوله نحو المعروف من أمر الطيور والبهائم: إنها بطبعها تنفر عما أريد بها من أنواع منافع البشر، ثم بحسن قيام أهل البصر بذلك يصير مما طبع عليه بالميل إليه كالمستوحش ومما طبع على النفار عنه كالمطبوع عليه. وعلى ذلك أمر نفار الطبع عن القتل والذبح في البشر، ثم سهولة ذلك عليه. وما يدرك حسنه بالعقل أو قبحه، فلا يزال يزداد على ما فيه إدراكه ببديهة الأحوال. ولذلك جعل الله العقول حجة لا ميل الطباع؛ إذ أجرى قلمه على أهلها، وإن شاركوا في الطباع غيرهم ممن ليست لهم عقول سليمة، وألزم أهلها اتباع ما أراهم العقل حسنه وإن كان في الطبع النفارُ، واجتناب ما في العقل قبحُه وإن كان في طبيعة الجوهر قبوله، إذ العقل يُرى صاحبه على حقيقة ما عليه الشيء، والطبع - أعني طبع الجوهر ـ لا يوضح ذلك. إن طبع الجوهر لا يبصر به ولا يُمَثَّل غير الحاضر، والعقل يدرك به ما حضر وغاب، وبه يحضر على الطبع ما غاب حتى يصير له كالشاهد مما يكرهه ويتلذذ به، وعنده تسهل المحنة وتخف مُؤن الذي يكرهه الطبع. وعلى ذلك تقدير الكلام والعبارات: إنها وإن كانت تختلف في الحسن و القبح على الأسماع فإنها لا تغيّر الحقوق إذ هي تتغير. ويجوز أن تُؤدَّى عبارة واحدة بلسانين يكون أحدهما أحلى من الآخر. والحسن لنفسه أو الحق لا يختلف لاختلاف المعبرين. فلهذا لم يقدر حسن الأشياء بطبع الخلقة ولا بحسن العبارة، وإنما قدر بالعقل الذي لا يرى الحسن قبيحا. وهو الأصل الذي يلزم تسوية كل أمر من الأمور عليه. وذلك كعلم العيان الذي لا يحتمل التغير، ولا يناقضه جهل، فيكون هو أصلاً لكل خفي مستور، وكذلك أمر العقل وما أراه أصل لكل أمر مطبوع. ولما بينا من مخالفة الطبائع في التزيين العقول وفي التقبيح تعذر على كثير من الخلق إدراك ما أراهم العقل والطبع، فصار بذلك المحكم عندهم في صورة المتشابه، والمتشابه في صورة المحكم، وهكذا أريد دَرْك كل شيء بغير سبيله. فنسأل الله أن يعصمنا عن رؤية الباطل بصورة الحق والحق بصورة الباطل، فإنه قوى مدبر قدير.
And the root of that is that Allah the Exalted created humans upon natures inclining toward present pleasures, calling their possessor to them, and beautifying them in his eye, by what He instilled in him of desires toward that to which his nature inclines, and they flee from what has pain and fatigue, so his nature becomes one of the enemies of his reason in judging good and evil. And what reason deems good or evil has no passing away nor change from state to state, while what nature deems good or evil is in the realm of transformation and change from state to state through discipline and persistence in that by refraining from what it is accustomed to, and turning to what it flees from by good persistence in that according to what nature can accept, such as is known from the matter of birds and beasts: they naturally flee from the kinds of human benefits intended for them, then by good persistence of those with insight on that, it becomes like what is naturally inclined toward, as the wild becomes tame, and what is naturally averse becomes like the natural. And upon that is the matter of nature's aversion to killing and slaughtering in humans, then its ease upon them. And what is perceived as good or evil by reason only increases in its perception by the intuition of circumstances. Therefore Allah made intellects (al-ʿuqūl) the proof, not inclination of natures; for He ran His pen upon their possessors, even though they share in natures with others who do not have sound intellects, and He obligated their possessors to follow what reason shows them as good even if there is aversion in nature, and to avoid what is evil in reason even if there is acceptance in the nature of the substance, for reason shows its possessor the reality of what thing is, while nature – I mean the nature of the substance – does not clarify that. Indeed, the nature of the substance does not see with it, nor does it represent other than the present, while reason perceives with it what is present and absent, and by it the absent is made present to nature until it becomes like a witness to what it dislikes and delights in, and with it the trial becomes easy and the burden of what nature dislikes becomes light. And upon that is the estimation of speech and expressions: even if they differ in good and evil to the ears, they do not change the rights (al-ḥuqūq) because they change. And it is possible that one expression be conveyed in two tongues, one of which is sweeter than the other. And the good in itself or the truth does not differ due to the difference of those who express it. For this reason the goodness of things is not estimated by the nature of creation nor by the beauty of expression, but is estimated by reason which does not see good as evil. And it is the principle (al-aṣl) upon which every matter must be measured. And that is like the knowledge of ocular vision which does not admit change, and ignorance does not contradict it, so it becomes a foundation (aṣl) for every hidden and concealed thing. And likewise the matter of reason and what it shows is the foundation for every natural (maṭbūʿ) matter. And when we have explained the opposition of natures in beautifying and vilifying to the intellects, it became difficult for many of creation to perceive what reason and nature showed them, so by that the clear (muḥkam) became in their view in the form of the ambiguous (mutashābih), and the ambiguous in the form of the clear. And thus it was intended [by Satan?] that everything be perceived by other than its proper way. So we ask Allah to protect us from seeing falsehood in the form of truth and truth in the form of falsehood, for He is strong, disposer, powerful.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is an extended explanation of the root cause behind the confusion mentioned in the previous lesson. The author first states that Allah created humans with natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) that incline toward immediate pleasures, and these natures become enemies to reason (ʿaql) in moral judgment. He then distinguishes the judgments of reason, which are stable and unchanging, from the judgments of nature, which are mutable through discipline (riyāḍa). He illustrates with animals that can be trained to overcome natural aversion. In humans, even aversion to killing can become easy. Reason perceives both present and absent, while nature only perceives the immediate. Allah made intellects the proof (ḥujja), not natural inclination, and obligated people to follow reason even if nature recoils. Then the author turns to speech and expressions: they may differ in aesthetic quality but do not alter the actual rights (ḥuqūq). The good in itself is not affected by the speaker's eloquence. Therefore the standard (qadr) of good and evil is reason, not nature or eloquence. Reason is like direct visual knowledge (ʿilm al-ʿiyān) – unchanging and a foundation for hidden things. Because natures oppose reason in beautifying and vilifying, many people fail to perceive correctly, confusing the clear with the ambiguous. The author concludes with a prayer for protection from such inversion.

قبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nature (ṭabʿ) is a reliable guide to good and evil.
Correction: Maturidi insists that only reason provides stable moral judgment; nature is changeable and often opposes truth.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the judgments of nature (ṭabʿ) compared to reason (ʿaql)?
A) They are equally reliable
B) Nature's judgments are stable while reason's change
C) Nature's judgments are changeable through discipline, while reason's are stable
D) Reason is only for worldly matters, nature for religion

Key Takeaway
Reason, not nature, is the stable criterion for moral judgment, as it perceives reality beyond immediate inclinations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you knowingly followed a natural desire even though your reason told you it was harmful or sinful? What will it take for you to side with reason the next time that desire arises?
?
The author says that nature beautifies immediate pleasures and hides their true ugliness. What is one 'beautified' sin in your life that you have been excusing? Will you submit to reason today and see it as it really is, even if it means discomfort?
?
Consider a belief or habit you hold that feels 'natural' but might contradict reason. Are you willing to examine it honestly, knowing that your nature may be deceiving you? What step can you take this week to seek the truth of reason over the comfort of nature?

Intellect Overcomes Nature
Maturidi contrasts intellect and nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the intellect is superior to natural disposition because it perceives both present and absent, enabling sound moral judgment.

Core Idea
The intellect sees what nature cannot.

Maturidi begins by establishing that the intellect, not natural inclination, is the true guide for discerning good and evil. He contrasts the limitations of nature, which only perceives the present, with the intellect's ability to grasp the absent and the true reality of things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the contrast Maturidi draws between intellect and nature.
Pay attention to the words 'طبع' and 'عقل'.
2
Identify the key claims
Find where Maturidi states that the intellect shows true reality, while nature does not. Also note how the intellect brings the absent to the present.
3
Understand the implications
Reflect on why perceiving both present and absent makes the intellect a better guide for morality. How does this affect our understanding of right and wrong?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليست لهم عقول سليمة، وألزم أهلها اتباع ما أراهم العقل حسنه وإن كان في الطبع النفارُ، واجتناب ما في العقل قبحُه وإن كان في طبيعة الجوهر قبوله، إذ العقل يُرى صاحبه على حقيقة ما عليه الشيء، والطبع - أعني طبع الجوهر ـ لا يوضح ذلك. إن طبع الجوهر لا يبصر به ولا يُمَثَّل غير الحاضر ، والعقل يدرك به ما حضر وغاب، وبه يحضر على الطبع ما غاب حتى يصير له كالشاهد مما يكرهه ويتلذذ به، وعنده تسهل المحنة وتخف مُؤن الذي يكرهه الطبع. وعلى ذلك تقدير الكلام والعبارات: إنها وإن كانت تختلف في الحسن و القبح على الأسماع فإنها لا تغيّر الحقوق إذ هي تتغير. ويجوز أن تُؤدَّى عبارة واحدة بلسانين يكون أحدهما أحلى من الآخر. والحسن لنفسه أو الحق لا يختلف لاختلاف المعبرين. فلهذا لم يقدر حسن الأشياء بطبع الخلقة ولا بحسن العبارة، وإنما قدر بالعقل الذي لا يرى الحسن قبيحا. وهو الأصل الذي يلزم تسوية كل أمر من الأمور عليه. وذلك كعلم العيان الذي لا يحتمل التغير، ولا يناقضه جهل، فيكون هو أصلاً لكل خفي مستور، وكذلك أمر العقل وما أراه أصل لكل أمر مطبوع. ولما بينا من مخالفة الطبائع في التزيين العقول وفي التقبيح تعذر على كثير من الخلق إدراك ما أراهم العقل والطبع، فصار بذلك المحكم عندهم في صورة المتشابه، والمتشابه في صورة المحكم، وهكذا أريد دَرْك كل شيء بغير سبيله. فنسأل الله أن يعصمنا عن رؤية الباطل بصورة الحق والحق بصورة الباطل، فإنه قوى مدبر قدير.
They do not have sound intellects, and He obligated its people to follow what the intellect shows them as good even if it is repulsive in nature, and to avoid what in the intellect is its ugliness even if it is acceptable in the nature of the substance, for the intellect shows its possessor the true reality of the thing, and nature — I mean the nature of the substance — does not clarify that. Indeed the nature of the substance does not perceive nor represent anything other than the present, while the intellect perceives with it what is present and absent, and through it the absent is brought to nature until it becomes for it like a witness of what it dislikes and delights in, and with it hardship becomes easy and the burden of what nature dislikes becomes light. And upon that is the estimation of speech and expressions: even if they differ in goodness and badness upon the ears, they do not change rights since they are changing. And it is permissible that a single expression be conveyed in two tongues, one of which is sweeter than the other. And the good is for itself, or the truth does not differ due to the difference of those who express it. For this reason the goodness of things was not estimated by the nature of creation nor by the beauty of expression, but rather it was estimated by the intellect, which does not see good as ugly. And it is the principle upon which every matter must be measured. And that is like direct knowledge that does not admit change, and ignorance does not contradict it, so it is a principle for every hidden and concealed thing. And likewise is the matter of the intellect and what it shows is a principle for every natural matter. And since we have explained the opposition of natures in beautifying intellects and in making ugly, it became difficult for many creatures to perceive what the intellect and nature show them, so the clear became for them in the form of the ambiguous, and the ambiguous in the form of the clear, and thus everything was meant to be comprehended other than through its proper way. So we ask God to protect us from seeing falsehood in the form of truth and truth in the form of falsehood, for He is strong, disposing, powerful.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the primacy of the intellect over nature and language in determining good and evil. Maturidi begins by stating that those who deny this lack sound intellect. He then explains that the intellect reveals the true nature of things, while natural disposition does not. The intellect perceives both present and absent, whereas nature only perceives the present. Through the intellect, the absent is brought to nature, making it easier to endure hardships. He then discusses expressions: they vary but do not change the underlying rights. The true good is determined by the intellect, not by natural inclination or eloquent expression. He compares this to direct knowledge (ʿilm al-ʿiyān) which is unchanging. He concludes by noting that because nature often conflicts with the intellect, many people fail to see things correctly, and he prays for protection from confusion.

Key Terms
العقل
الطبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That natural instinct or feeling is a reliable guide to determine good and evil.
Correction: Maturidi shows that nature only perceives the present and cannot reveal the true reality of things, whereas the intellect perceives both present and absent, making it the correct criterion.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the intellect superior to nature in perceiving good and evil?
A) Because nature is always repulsive
B) Because the intellect perceives both present and absent
C) Because nature is more reliable
D) Because the intellect is innate

Key Takeaway
The intellect perceives both present and absent, making it superior to nature as a guide for moral judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent situation where your natural inclination (e.g., laziness, desire) pulled you away from what your intellect knew was right. How did you resolve that conflict? Are you in the habit of following your intellect or your nature?
?
The passage says that through the intellect, the absent is brought to nature until it becomes like a witness. When you contemplate the Day of Judgment or the consequences of your actions, does your intellect truly make those absent realities feel present to you, or do you live as if they are distant? What one thing can you do today to make the unseen more real to your heart?
?
Maturidi says that with intellect, 'hardship becomes easy and the burden of what nature dislikes becomes light.' Is there a duty in your life that your nature finds heavy but your intellect affirms as good? What is keeping you from embracing it with the vision of your intellect?

Intellect as the Unchanging Standard
Maturidi establishes intellect as the unchanging moral standard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the intellect serves as the unchanging standard for good and evil, independent of language and natural inclination, and is analogous to direct knowledge.

Core Idea
Goodness is measured by intellect, not by words or nature.

Having established the intellect's superiority, Maturidi now argues that it is the sole criterion for moral value. He explains that expressions and natural inclinations vary, but the intellect's judgment of good and evil remains constant, akin to direct knowledge (ʿilm al-ʿiyān).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting how Maturidi shifts from expressions to the intellect as the standard.
2
Identify the argument about language
Find where Maturidi states that expressions differ but do not change underlying rights. How does this support the intellect's role?
3
Understand the comparison to direct knowledge
Notice the analogy between the intellect and ʿilm al-ʿiyān. Why is this comparison important?
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why confusion arises when people follow nature instead of intellect, and how the prayer reinforces the lesson.

ليست لهم عقول سليمة، وألزم أهلها اتباع ما أراهم العقل حسنه وإن كان في الطبع النفارُ، واجتناب ما في العقل قبحُه وإن كان في طبيعة الجوهر قبوله، إذ العقل يُرى صاحبه على حقيقة ما عليه الشيء، والطبع - أعني طبع الجوهر ـ لا يوضح ذلك. إن طبع الجوهر لا يبصر به ولا يُمَثَّل غير الحاضر ، والعقل يدرك به ما حضر وغاب، وبه يحضر على الطبع ما غاب حتى يصير له كالشاهد مما يكرهه ويتلذذ به، وعنده تسهل المحنة وتخف مُؤن الذي يكرهه الطبع. وعلى ذلك تقدير الكلام والعبارات: إنها وإن كانت تختلف في الحسن و القبح على الأسماع فإنها لا تغيّر الحقوق إذ هي تتغير. ويجوز أن تُؤدَّى عبارة واحدة بلسانين يكون أحدهما أحلى من الآخر. والحسن لنفسه أو الحق لا يختلف لاختلاف المعبرين. فلهذا لم يقدر حسن الأشياء بطبع الخلقة ولا بحسن العبارة، وإنما قدر بالعقل الذي لا يرى الحسن قبيحا. وهو الأصل الذي يلزم تسوية كل أمر من الأمور عليه. وذلك كعلم العيان الذي لا يحتمل التغير، ولا يناقضه جهل، فيكون هو أصلاً لكل خفي مستور، وكذلك أمر العقل وما أراه أصل لكل أمر مطبوع. ولما بينا من مخالفة الطبائع في التزيين العقول وفي التقبيح تعذر على كثير من الخلق إدراك ما أراهم العقل والطبع، فصار بذلك المحكم عندهم في صورة المتشابه، والمتشابه في صورة المحكم، وهكذا أريد دَرْك كل شيء بغير سبيله. فنسأل الله أن يعصمنا عن رؤية الباطل بصورة الحق والحق بصورة الباطل، فإنه قوى مدبر قدير.
They do not have sound intellects, and He obligated its people to follow what the intellect shows them as good even if it is repulsive in nature, and to avoid what in the intellect is its ugliness even if it is acceptable in the nature of the substance, for the intellect shows its possessor the true reality of the thing, and nature — I mean the nature of the substance — does not clarify that. Indeed the nature of the substance does not perceive nor represent anything other than the present, while the intellect perceives with it what is present and absent, and through it the absent is brought to nature until it becomes for it like a witness of what it dislikes and delights in, and with it hardship becomes easy and the burden of what nature dislikes becomes light. And upon that is the estimation of speech and expressions: even if they differ in goodness and badness upon the ears, they do not change rights since they are changing. And it is permissible that a single expression be conveyed in two tongues, one of which is sweeter than the other. And the good is for itself, or the truth does not differ due to the difference of those who express it. For this reason the goodness of things was not estimated by the nature of creation nor by the beauty of expression, but rather it was estimated by the intellect, which does not see good as ugly. And it is the principle upon which every matter must be measured. And that is like direct knowledge that does not admit change, and ignorance does not contradict it, so it is a principle for every hidden and concealed thing. And likewise is the matter of the intellect and what it shows is a principle for every natural matter. And since we have explained the opposition of natures in beautifying intellects and in making ugly, it became difficult for many creatures to perceive what the intellect and nature show them, so the clear became for them in the form of the ambiguous, and the ambiguous in the form of the clear, and thus everything was meant to be comprehended other than through its proper way. So we ask God to protect us from seeing falsehood in the form of truth and truth in the form of falsehood, for He is strong, disposing, powerful.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the primacy of the intellect over nature and language in determining good and evil. Maturidi begins by stating that those who deny this lack sound intellect. He then explains that the intellect reveals the true nature of things, while natural disposition does not. The intellect perceives both present and absent, whereas nature only perceives the present. Through the intellect, the absent is brought to nature, making it easier to endure hardships. He then discusses expressions: they vary but do not change the underlying rights. The true good is determined by the intellect, not by natural inclination or eloquent expression. He compares this to direct knowledge (ʿilm al-ʿiyān) which is unchanging. He concludes by noting that because nature often conflicts with the intellect, many people fail to see things correctly, and he prays for protection from confusion.

Key Terms
العقل
الحسن والقبح
علم العيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the beauty of language or natural inclination determines what is good or evil.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that goodness is estimated by the intellect alone, not by nature or expression.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the analogy for the intellect as an unchanging standard?
A) The beauty of expression
B) Natural inclination
C) Direct knowledge (ʿilm al-ʿiyān)
D) The diversity of languages

Key Takeaway
The intellect, like direct knowledge, is the unchanging standard for good and evil, unaffected by variation in language or nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, when have you been swayed by eloquent speech or natural inclination rather than your own intellect to judge something as good? What can you do this week to train your intellect to be the decisive judge?
?
Maturidi says that natures often oppose the intellect, causing confusion. Reflect on a specific area where you have confused the clear with the ambiguous. What concrete step will you take to align your judgment with intellectual clarity?
?
If the intellect is the unchanging standard for moral truth, what does that demand from you when you face a situation where your desires or societal norms conflict with what your intellect tells you is right? How will you respond this week?
