The Distinction Between Power for Obedience and Disobedience
Maturidi presents the claim and definitions of distinct powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that the power for obedience is distinct from the power for disobedience, and how Maturidi defines these powers as divine success and protection versus abandonment.

Core Idea
The power for obedience is divine success; the power for disobedience is divine abandonment.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents the view of a group of scholars who hold that the power enabling obedience is fundamentally different from the power that would enable disobedience. They identify these powers with specific divine acts: tawfiq and 'isma for obedience, and khidhlan and tark for disobedience. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping how human action relates to divine will in Maturidi theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the statement that 'the power of obedience is other than the power of disobedience.'
2
Understand the definitions
The passage defines the power of obedience as tawfiq (divine success) and 'isma (protection), and the power of disobedience as khidhlan (abandonment) and tark (leaving). Reflect on what these terms mean.
3
Consider the significance
This distinction implies that God's assistance is specific to obedience, and His withholding of assistance is specific to disobedience. This does not negate human choice but highlights divine agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال جماعة منهم: قوة الطاعة هي غير قوة المعصية، منهم الحسين وغيره. وهم يذهبون إلى أن قوة الطاعة التوفيق والعصمة، وقوة المعصية الخذلان والترك على ما يُختار. ودليل ذلك وجود سؤال المعونة والعصمة على الإحاطة أن ليس معهما زيغ، والتوفيق على الإحاطة أن معه الإصابة. وكذلك القول الظاهر بـ«اللهم قوتي على طاعتك وأعني عليها»، وبالتعوذ من الخذلان والإزاغة. ثبت أن لو كان يكون بكل واحد منهما ما يكون بالآخر لم يكن الذي يُسأل بالسؤال أحق من الذي يتعوذ منه. ولو كان يكون بالعصمة زيغ لم يكن يطمئن القلب عند الوجود. فثبت أن قوة كل نوع من ذلك غير قوة النوع الآخر، وثبت بما يسأل العصمة والتوفيق كما يسأل المعونة والتقوية أنهما في الحقيقة واحد. وأيضا إنه لا أحد يطلق القول في الكافر أنه موفق للإيمان معصوم عن الكفر، ولا أحد يمتنع عنه في المؤمن؛ ثبت أن معنى ذلك المعونة على الإيمان، والآخر الخذلان. وأيضا إن القوة إذ هي وقتين لا يصلح بها الفعلان ولا سبيل إلى جمع الفعلين المتضادين في وقت واحد؛ ثبت أن ذلك قوة لأحدهما لا لهما، وأن الذي يكون لهما يبقى لاحتمالهما. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And a group among them said: The power of obedience is other than the power of disobedience, among them al-Husayn and others. And they hold that the power of obedience is divine success (tawfiq) and protection ('isma), and the power of disobedience is abandonment (khidhlan) and leaving (tark) according to what is chosen. And the proof of that is the existence of asking for assistance and protection comprehensively that there is no deviation with them, and success comprehensively that with it there is correctness. And likewise the apparent saying: 'O Allah, strengthen me for Your obedience and help me upon it,' and seeking refuge from abandonment and deviation. It is established that if what occurs by each one of them occurred by the other, then the one asked by request would not be more deserving than that from which refuge is sought. And if there were deviation with protection, the heart would not be at ease upon its existence. So it is established that the power of each type of that is other than the power of the other type, and it is established by the fact that one asks for protection and success just as one asks for assistance and strengthening that they are in reality one. And also, no one applies the saying to the disbeliever that he is successful for faith and protected from disbelief, and no one refrains from it regarding the believer; it is established that the meaning of that is assistance for faith, and the other is abandonment. And also, since power (al-quwwa) when it is for two times, both acts are not fit by it, and there is no way to combine two opposite acts in one time; it is established that it is power for one of them, not for both, and that which is for both would remain for their possibility. And there is no power except from Allah.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage presents the view of a group (including al-Husayn al-Basri) that the power enabling obedience is distinct from the power that would enable disobedience. They identify the power of obedience as divine success (tawfiq) and protection ('isma), and the power of disobedience as abandonment (khidhlan) and leaving (tark) by choice. The proof is drawn from common religious practices: believers ask Allah for assistance and protection, and seek refuge from abandonment and deviation. If one power were sufficient for both, then asking for one would be no different from seeking refuge from the other. Additionally, if protection could involve deviation, the heart would not be assured. Therefore, the two powers are distinct. Furthermore, no one attributes success and protection to a disbeliever, nor denies them to a believer, confirming they are specifically related to faith. Finally, since a single power in a given moment cannot produce two opposite acts, and two opposite acts cannot coexist, it is clear that any particular instance of power is for one act only, not both. The passage ends with the reminder that all power is from Allah.

Key Terms
التوفيق (al-tawfiq)
العصمة (al-'isma)
الخذلان (al-khidhlan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the power of obedience identified with?
A) Free will and effort
B) Tawfiq and 'isma
C) Khidhlan and tark
D) Natural ability

Key Takeaway
The power for obedience and the power for disobedience are distinct divine acts, not a single human capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obey Allah, do you stop to thank Him for granting you the very ability to obey? How would your worship change if you saw every act of obedience as a direct gift of tawfiq from Him?
?
When you sin, do you consider that Allah may have abandoned you to your own choice? What does that reveal about your need for His protection and your dependence on Him even to avoid sin?
?
If the power for obedience is completely distinct from the power for disobedience, then no sin can be committed using the power He gave for obedience. Does your repentance reflect this distinction—are you asking for a new power to obey rather than just forgiveness?

Proofs for the Distinction from Supplication and Logic
Maturidi provides three proofs for the distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proofs Maturidi provides from common religious practices and logical reasoning that the two powers are distinct.

Core Idea
We ask for success and seek refuge from abandonment, proving they are distinct.

Imam Maturidi now provides several proofs for the distinction between the power for obedience and the power for disobedience. He first appeals to the practice of supplication: believers ask Allah for assistance and protection, and seek refuge from abandonment and deviation. If one power could produce both, then asking for one would be equivalent to seeking refuge from the other. He also argues that no one attributes success and protection to a disbeliever, and that a single power cannot produce opposite acts simultaneously. These proofs ground the theological distinction in everyday worship and rational consistency.

Guided Path
1
Examine the supplication proof
Read the part about asking for 'assistance and protection' and seeking refuge from 'abandonment and deviation.' Reflect on why this implies distinct powers.
2
Consider the example of believer vs disbeliever
Notice that no one says a disbeliever is successful for faith. This shows that tawfiq and 'isma are specific to obedience.
3
Analyze the logical argument
The passage argues that a single power at a given time cannot produce two opposite acts. Therefore, any instance of power must be specific to one act.

Command and Prohibition Cannot Be Metaphorical at Action Time
Maturidi refutes the claim that command and prohibition are metaphorical at action time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that command and prohibition must be real at the time of action, and the absurd consequences of treating them as mere metaphor.

Core Idea
If command is only metaphorical at action time, obedience and disobedience lose all meaning.

Imam al-Maturidi here tackles a subtle but dangerous claim: that at the moment of acting, there is no real command or prohibition — only a metaphorical one. This might seem harmless, but Maturidi shows it destroys the very basis of religious accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once and then the translation to follow Maturidi's argument structure.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
The opponents say there is no real command at the time of action. Note how Maturidi critiques that.
3
Trace the absurd consequences
Follow Maturidi's logical chain: no real command → no obedience/disobedience → infinite regress → impossible burden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن من قولهم : أن ليس عليه وقت الفعل أمر ولا نهي إلا على المجاز كما يقوله المسلمون . وإنما معناه أنه مأمور به بمعنى أن كان من قبل ليفعل فيه . فإذا لم يكن هو مأمورا به ولا منهيا لم يكن بالفعل مؤتمرا ولا مرتكبًا النهي ،وقته وبه تجب العداوة والولاية، فصارا في الحقيقة لا لطاعة ولا لمعصية، أو لا أمر ولا نهى ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعلى ذلك قولهم: إنه مأمور بالفعل في الوقت الثاني، وفي الثاني مأمور به في الوقت الثالث، كذلك أبدا. فلا يفعل الذي أمر بكل وقت وليس بتارك للأمر، لما ليس بمأمور وقت الترك. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم الأصل الذي في العقل دركه أن كل مأمور بالفعل للغَدِ ليس بمأمور هو به للحال، فكذلك [ا] يجب في الوقت القريب؛ فيجب أن الذي أمر بالفعل للوقت الذي يتلوه ليس بمأمور به للحال في العقل . ثم ليس بمأمور به في الوقت الثاني عندهم، ولا منهى عن ضده، فيبطل حقيقة الأمر والنهي بما في العقل احتماله على قولهم، ويبطل قولهم بما في العقل دفعه . وهم مع ذلك لا يجعلون له قدرة في ذلك الفعل، فيكون تكليف ما لا يطاق على قولهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also, part of their statement: there is no command or prohibition upon him at the time of the action except metaphorically, as the Muslims say. Its meaning is that he is commanded in the sense that he was previously to act in it. So if he is neither commanded nor prohibited, then in the act he is not complying nor committing a prohibition, at his time and by it enmity and allegiance become obligatory, so they become in reality neither obedience nor disobedience, or no command and no prohibition, and there is no power except through Allah. And upon that is their statement: he is commanded to act in the second time, and in the second he is commanded in the third time, likewise forever. So he does not do what he was commanded at each time, and he is not abandoning the command, because he is not commanded at the time of abandonment. And there is no power except through Allah. Then the principle that the intellect grasps is that everyone commanded to do something tomorrow is not commanded to do it now, so likewise [the same] must apply in the near time; so it is necessary that the one commanded to do something for the next time is not commanded to do it now in the intellect. Then he is not commanded at the second time according to them, nor prohibited from its opposite, so the reality of command and prohibition is nullified by what the intellect can admit from their statement, and their statement is nullified by what the intellect rejects. And despite that, they do not assign him power in that act, so it becomes imposition of what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq) according to their statement. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of those who claim that at the time of action, there is no actual command or prohibition except metaphorically. Maturidi argues that if this were true, then the commanded person would not be acting in obedience or disobedience at the moment of action, which would nullify the very meaning of command and prohibition. He then shows the absurd consequence: if command is always for a future time, then at each present moment there is no command, so the person is never actually commanded or prohibited from the opposite, thus destroying the reality of divine command. Furthermore, since they deny power at the moment of action, this results in imposing what cannot be borne, which is irrational.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
مجاز
طاعة
معصية
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Command can be postponed indefinitely so that at each moment there is no actual command.
Correction: Maturidi shows this leads to no real obedience or disobedience and imposes an impossible burden.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what follows from the claim that command and prohibition are only metaphorical at the time of action?
A) The reality of command and prohibition is strengthened.
B) Obedience and disobedience become meaningless.
C) The intellect cannot grasp any principle.
D) Power becomes irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that real command and prohibition at the time of action are necessary for the meaningfulness of obedience, disobedience, and moral accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you delay a command of Allah, thinking 'I will do it later', have you effectively treated that command as metaphorical for now? What does this lesson reveal about the reality of that command in this very moment? Will you now act immediately on something you have been postponing?
?
Consider a sin you are currently committing or tempted by. If you tell yourself 'I will repent later,' are you denying the reality of the prohibition right now? How does Maturidi's argument strip away that excuse? What is one step you will take today to treat Allah's prohibition as literal and binding at this moment?
?
If you believe that your actions are entirely created by Allah, you might think 'I have no power now.' But Maturidi warns that denying power at action time leads to impossible burden (taklif ma la yutaq). Do you truly accept responsibility for your choices right now, or do you hide behind divine decree? Identify one area where you must reclaim accountability and act.

The Two Methods of Analogy from the Witnessed to the Unseen
Maturidi refutes two methods of analogy from witnessed to unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of analogical reasoning that transfers human traits to God without considering the real meaning.

Core Idea
Analogy from the witnessed to the unseen must be based on meaning, not superficial resemblance.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi addresses a common error in reasoning about God: assuming that what is observed in humans applies directly to the divine. He presents two possible methods of analogy and shows that both are flawed—one leads to absurd consequences, the other is overturned by the immediate intuition of reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that Maturidi presents a dilemma with two options.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two methods
Find the two methods: (1) transferring every observed human trait as wisdom or foolishness to the unseen, (2) looking at the actual meaning that makes a thing wise or foolish.
These are in the first sentence after 'أحد أمرين'.
3
Examine the first method's consequences
Maturidi shows that the first method leads to absurdities: creating useless things, creating from nothing, punishing without reprieve, and permitting lying.
These are listed as 'في خلق ما لا ينتفع به...'.
4
Understand the refutation of the second method
The second method is invalidated by 'بديهة العقل' (immediate intuition of reason), which distinguishes between what is permissible and impermissible.
Maturidi does not elaborate here; he simply states it is overturned.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this argument protects theology from false analogies. The opponent is left with no valid method to transfer human traits to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا بخروجه [عن] ذلك وتعنّته فيهما ادعى. على أنه لا يخلو من أحد أمرين : إما أن يجعل كل شيء يعرفه في الشاهد من البشر حكمة أو سفها يقول به في الغائب، أو ينظر إلى المعنى الذي له صار كذلك في التحقيق فيقول به في الغائب. فإن قال بالأول لزمه ذلك في خلق ما لا ينتفع به، وفي خلق الشيء من لا شيء، وفي التعذيب من غير دفع، ثم يقال نفسه في إجازته قوله بالكذب . فمهما أجاب من شيء فذلك لازم له فيما قال. وإن نظر إلى المعنى أبطل قوله ذا ببديهة العقل. وذا يجوز له، وذا لا يجوز.
And we have shown by his departure from that and his stubbornness in both what he claimed. He is not free from one of two matters: either he makes everything he knows in the witnessed from humans as wisdom or foolishness, and says it about the unseen, or he looks at the meaning by which it became so in reality and says it about the unseen. If he says by the first, he is obliged to apply it to the creation of what is not beneficial, and to the creation of a thing from nothing, and to punishment without repelling, then he says himself regarding his permission of the statement of lying. Whatever he answers from anything, that is necessary for him in what he said. And if he looks at the meaning, then his statement is invalidated by the intuition of reason. This is permissible for him, and that is not permissible.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage refutes an opponent's approach to reasoning from the witnessed world to the unseen (God). Maturidi explains that the opponent cannot avoid one of two methods. First: He may simply transfer all observed human traits (wisdom or foolishness) to God. This leads to absurd consequences: that God creates useless things, creates from nothing (which is not observed), punishes without removing the punishment, and even permits lying. Any answer the opponent gives to these consequences will be self-defeating. Second: He may consider the actual meaning that makes something wise or foolish. But if he does that, his claim is overturned by 'بديهة العقل' (the immediate intuition of reason), which distinguishes between permissible and impermissible. The passage thus forces the opponent into a dilemma: either absurdity or self-contradiction.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
بديهة العقل
حكمة
سفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'بديهة العقل' (intuition of reason) is itself a source of knowledge for making analogies.
Correction: In this passage, 'بديهة العقل' is used to invalidate a false analogy, not to affirm a positive one. It distinguishes between permissible and impermissible without providing new content.

Checkpoint
What are the two possible methods of analogy from witnessed to unseen that Maturidi describes?
A) Analogy by essence and analogy by accident
B) Analogy by superficial resemblance and analogy by real meaning
C) Analogy by causality and analogy by necessity
D) Analogy from the firmaments and analogy from the earth

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that superficial analogy from humans to God is invalid; proper reasoning requires considering real meanings, but even that is checked by reason's intuition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to understand God's actions, do you ever catch yourself attributing human motives to Him? How does Maturidi's warning about the first method expose this error in your own heart?
?
How often do you rely on your own reasoning about God without checking it against the immediate intuition of reason that distinguishes what is permissible for Him? Reflect on a specific instance where you may have overstepped.
?
This passage shows that both methods of analogy fail. What does that mean for your personal efforts to know God? Are you willing to submit to the limits of reason as set by 'بديهة العقل'?

Refutation of Dualism and Intuitive Knowledge
Maturidi refutes dualism and the false claim of intuitive knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila claim of inherent evil by showing that attributing absolute aspects to God leads to dualism and that their reliance on rational intuition is unfounded.

Core Idea
To claim inherent evil in actions is to fall into dualism and false intuition.

Maturidi opens his refutation by exposing a dangerous logical consequence: if you insist that an action is inherently evil regardless of context, you end up attributing that absolute aspect to God, which leads to a dualistic worldview akin to Zoroastrianism. He then challenges the Mu'tazila's appeal to 'intuitive reason' as merely a disguised analogy. This passage sets the stage for a deeper critique of moral objectivism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dualism accusation
Read the first two sentences where Maturidi says that referring one aspect to God and realizing the other aspect in the world necessitates dualism. Think about what 'the two aspects' refer to—presumably good and evil as absolute principles.
This is a strong theological charge: the Mu'tazila are indirectly aligning with ancient dualists.
2
Understand the objection about useless creation
Maturidi mentions an objection: God creating something useless. Notice his response: this does not prove the impossibility of imposing obligations on the disabled (zamin). Why? Because the creation of useless things is not the same as imposing impossible duties.
The term 'zamin' refers to a person with a disability, likely mental disability, who is not obligated.
3
Analyze the critique of intuitive reason
Maturidi declares that the one claiming knowledge by 'intuition of the intellect' (badihah al-'aql) is lying. He argues that such a claim is merely an analogy (qiyas) based on what the opponent already agrees with. Why is this a problem?
Intuitive knowledge should be self-evident and universal, not dependent on agreement.
4
Reflect on the self-defeating nature of the Mu'tazila proof
The passage says that the Mu'tazila's objection itself establishes a truth that is false in the witnessed world. What does this mean? Possibly that their claim of inherent evil contradicts observable reality where context matters.
Maturidi is turning their own reasoning against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم رجعوا إلى إحالة أحد الوجهين عن الله. فمن حقق الوجه الآخر في الموجود في العالم فقد أوجب ما قالت الثنوية. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم دفع ما عورض بخلق شيء لا ينتفع به بما لم يدل ذلك على تكليف الزمن. فهذا يبين أن الذي قال ببديهة العقل كذب، وأنه إنما ادعى القياس على ما وافقه خصمه عليه لا غير. ثم حصل بما عارض به خصمه مما ادعى ببديهة العقل على ما يثبت شيئا صدقا مما هو كذب في الشاهد. ثم أجاب بما يوجد احتراز النفع بالفعل بأنه لا يحمد عليه. ثبت أن ما قبح منه لم يقبح لعينه. وكذلك يجد خصمه مما يقدر عليه ولا يجوز التكليف به، ثبت أن ذلك لم يقبح لنفسه، نحو الفواحش والكفر. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وذا أولى، لما يجئ مثل هذا من الصغار ولا يلحقه وصف فحش ولا كفر، ولا يجئ منهم فعل غير نافع لنفسه يوصف بالحكمة والذي قال من احتراز النفع فهو من ذلك الوجه حكمة، ولكن من وجه الضرر.
Then they reverted to referring one of the two aspects to God. Whoever realizes the other aspect in what exists in the world has necessitated what the dualists said. And there is no power except through God. Then he refuted what was objected to regarding creating something that yields no benefit by [saying] that this does not indicate [the impossibility] of [the] imposition upon the disabled [zamin]. This shows that the one who claimed [it is known] by the intuition of the intellect is lying, and that he only claimed analogy on what his opponent agreed with him upon, nothing else. Then he obtained, by what he objected to his opponent—who claimed [it is] by the intuition of the intellect—something that he establishes as true, while it is false in the witnessed [world]. Then he answered by [saying] that what is found of avoiding benefit by an action is not praised for it. It is established that what is deemed ugly from him is not ugly in itself. Likewise, his opponent finds what he is capable of and whose imposition is not permissible; it is established that that is not ugly in itself, such as obscenities and disbelief. And there is no power except through God. And this is more appropriate, because such [actions] come from children and no description of obscenity or disbelief attaches to them, and no action that is not beneficial to oneself comes from them [that] is described as wisdom, and the one who said [that] avoiding benefit is, from that aspect, wisdom, but from the aspect of harm.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that certain actions are inherently evil based on rational intuition. Maturidi begins by stating that if one attributes one aspect to God and realizes the other aspect in the world, one falls into dualism. Then he addresses the objection that God creating something useless is problematic. He argues that the Mu'tazila's claim of intuitive knowledge is false, because they only rely on analogies that their opponents agree upon. He then shows that what they claim as true by intuition is actually false in the witnessed world. He further argues that avoiding benefit in an action is not praiseworthy, so ugliness is not inherent. Similarly, actions like obscenities and disbelief, which the opponent might attribute to God, are not inherently ugly because similar actions from children or others are not considered ugly. Thus, the notion of inherent evil is refuted.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
تكليف الزمن
إحالة
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila claim of inherent evil is based on self-evident rational intuition.
Correction: Maturidi argues that it is not intuition but analogy based on prior agreement, and that it leads to dualism.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does claiming inherent evil in actions ultimately lead to?
A) Relativism
B) Dualism
C) Atheism
D) Anthropomorphism

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that their claim of inherent evil leads to dualism and is based on false intuition, not genuine reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever claimed something is inherently wrong without examining whether that judgment is based on divine command or your own assumption? How does Maturidi's refutation challenge that tendency in you? Take a moment to repent if you have made such claims.
?
Do you appeal to 'common sense' or 'intuition' in matters of faith? Maturidi says that is often just an analogy your opponent agrees with—can you think of an example in your own reasoning where you might have done that? What will you change?
?
The Mu'tazila fall into dualism by attributing evil to the world rather than to God's decree. In your heart, do you sometimes resent God for evil in the world? How does this passage correct that? Commit now to trust Allah's wisdom even when you don't understand.

Actions Are Not Inherently Ugly: The Child Example
Maturidi argues that ugliness is not intrinsic, using the child example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that ugliness (qubh) is not an intrinsic property of actions, using the example of children committing obscenities and disbelief.

Core Idea
If children can do an act without being called ugly, the act is not inherently evil.

Maturidi continues his refutation by dissecting the Mu'tazila argument that avoiding benefit in an action is praiseworthy. He shows that their own reasoning backfires, because when children perform the same actions they deem ugly, no one describes them as obscene or disbelieving. This proves that ugliness is relative to the doer and context, not intrinsic. The passage ties together the themes of benefit, harm, and moral attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument about benefit
Maturidi says that what is found of avoiding benefit by an action is not praised for it. Understand: if someone avoids benefit, that in itself is not praiseworthy. So the Mu'tazila cannot use 'avoiding benefit' as a criterion for ugliness.
This counters the idea that an action is evil because it involves harm or loss of benefit.
2
Extract the key conclusion
The text says: 'It is established that what is deemed ugly from him is not ugly in itself.' This is the central thesis. Circle this sentence.
The pronoun 'him' likely refers to God, but the principle applies universally.
3
Examine the child example
Maturidi notes that children can do obscene acts or acts of disbelief (like cursing or pretending to worship idols) but are not described as obscene or disbelieving. Why? Because they lack legal responsibility and intention.
This shows that the same act can be ugly for one person but not for another, depending on context.
4
Reflect on the conclusion about wisdom
The passage ends by noting that an action that avoids benefit can be wisdom from that aspect, but harm from another. This reinforces that moral judgments are multifaceted.
Maturidi is not denying that some actions are harmful, but denying that harm alone makes them inherently evil.

Power Only for Actual Action and the Pharaoh Objection
Maturidi argues that power is only for actual action and answers the Pharaoh objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that power (qudra) is only for the actual action, and how he answers the objection that if Pharaoh had power to believe, it would nullify God's knowledge.

Core Idea
Power is only for what actually happens, not for impossible possibilities.

Maturidi begins by establishing a key principle of Kalām: rational judgment dictates that power (qudra) is only for the act that actually occurs. He then raises a powerful objection concerning Pharaoh: if Pharaoh had the ability to believe, wouldn't that contradict God's foreknowledge that he would not believe? The response is subtle and hinges on what the power is for. This passage clarifies that power is for the opposite action, not for changing what God knows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument unfold.
Notice the initial premise and the self-objection structure.
2
Identify the initial premise
The passage begins by stating that power cannot be for something impossible; it must be only for the actual act. This is a rational necessity.
The phrase 'إحالة القول بالتقدم' means the claim of priority is impossible.
3
Understand the self-objection about Pharaoh
Maturidi raises an objection: if Pharaoh had power to believe, that would imply he could nullify God's knowledge. This applies to anyone God knows will not believe.
This is a strong objection that must be answered.
4
Grasp the answer
Maturidi answers that the objection is not necessary because power is for something other than the specific belief that is known not to occur. In Pharaoh's case, power is for disbelief, not for the impossible belief.
The answer preserves both divine foreknowledge and human capacity.
5
Reflect on the concluding challenge
Maturidi turns the argument back: if this reasoning were binding for us, it would also bind the opponent regarding the command (al-amr).
This completes the dialectical exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معطيّا القوة لشيء يستحيل كونه ، وفي ذلك فساد كون القوة للفعل. فألزم ما أوجبه العقل أنها لا تكون إلا للفعل إحالـة القول بالتقدم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم عارض نفسه بأمر فرعون إنه لو كان يقدر على الإيمان لكان يقدر على إبطال علم الله، وهذا في فرعون وكل من في علم الله أنه لا يؤمن . فأجاب بأن ذا لا يجب، لأن القدرة على غير الإيمان الذي هو المعلوم أنه لا يكون؛ ولو لزمنا ذلك في القوة ليلزمكم في الأمر.
Giving power to something whose existence is impossible entails the corruption of the concept of power being for the act. So reason necessitated that power is only for the act, making impossible the statement of priority. And there is no power except through God. Then he objected to himself regarding the matter of Pharaoh: if he had power to believe, he would have power to nullify God's knowledge — this applies to Pharaoh and everyone whom God knows will not believe. He answered that this is not necessary, because power is for something other than the belief which is known not to occur; and if this implication were necessary for us regarding power, it would be necessary for you regarding the command.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a kalām argument. It first establishes a premise: power (qudra) is only for an actual action, not for something impossible. The phrase 'إحالة القول بالتقدم' means that the claim of priority (that power could be for an impossible thing) is refuted. Then the author presents a self-objection concerning Pharaoh: if Pharaoh had the power to believe, that would imply he could nullify God's foreknowledge — which is absurd. The answer is that power is for something else, namely the opposite action (disbelief), not for the specific belief that God knows will not happen. The argument concludes that if this reasoning were binding for us (the Maturidis), it would also bind the opponent regarding the command (al-amr).

Key Terms
قوة
إيمان
علم الله
إبطال
فرعون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a person has power to believe, then he could change God's knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that power is for the opposite action, not for changing what God knows. Pharaoh's power is for disbelief, not belief.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does Pharaoh's hypothetical ability to believe not nullify God's knowledge?
A) Because God's knowledge is not about possibilities.
B) Because power is for something other than the belief that is known not to occur.
C) Because Pharaoh is an exception.
D) Because power is only for impossible things.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument shows that power is only for what actually occurs, and that divine foreknowledge does not constrain human capability because power is for the opposite of what is foreknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Pharaoh could never believe because God knew he would not. Is there any belief or virtue that you have convinced yourself you will never attain because of your past? How does Maturidi's argument challenge that assumption? What step can you take today to act toward that virtue, knowing that your power is for the actual act you choose now?
?
Maturidi says power is only for what actually happens. Consider a sin you habitually commit. Do you tell yourself 'I can't stop'? According to this passage, your power is exactly for what you do. What does that mean for your claim of helplessness? How can you realign your will to use your power for obedience instead?
?
If you knew that God foreknows your failure to practice a certain good deed, would that make you give up trying? Maturidi's answer to the Pharaoh objection applies to you too. Reflect on one area where you have surrendered to your own low expectations. What would it look like to treat your power as real and your choice as decisive, even in the face of your own past failures?

Proof from Necessary Reason
Maturidi argues from common experience to establish that a divine will controls all actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the discrepancy between human intention and actual outcome proves that a divine will governs all actions.

Core Idea
Your actions betray a will greater than your own.

Maturidi begins with a simple observation from everyday life: we often intend one thing but another occurs. This common experience, he argues, points to an external will that determines outcomes. Let's examine how this rational proof unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage slowly. Notice how Maturidi moves from common knowledge to logical conclusion.
Focus on the word 'ضرورة العقل' (necessary reason).
2
Understand the human experience
Everyone knows that actions often turn out contrary to intention. Reflect on examples from your own life.
3
Apply the logical inference
If my will is not sufficient to produce the outcome, another will must be involved. That is God's will.
The phrase 'ثبت أن لغيرهم إرادة' is the key inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العبرة بما توجبه ضرورة العقل توجب ذلك، إذ يعلم كل أحد أن فعله يخرج على غير الذي يريده من الحسن والقبح، واللذة والألم والمحبة والسخط. ثبت أن لغيرهم في خروج فعلهم على ما خرج إرادة، على تلك الإرادة يخرج. والله الموفق. وأيضاً إن إحداث شيء في سلطان آخر وفي مملكته من حيث لا يشاء ولا يريده آية الضعف والقهر، ومن ذلك وصفه محال أن يكون ربّا وإلها؛ لذلك لزم وصف الله بذلك. والله الموفق.
Then the lesson from what necessary reason necessitates: it necessitates that, for everyone knows that his action comes out other than what he wants of good and bad, pleasure and pain, love and anger. It is established that for other than them in the coming out of their action as it came out there is a will, upon that will it comes out. And Allah is the granter of success. And also, initiating something in another's dominion and in his kingdom from where he does not will and does not desire it is a sign of weakness and coercion, and from that describing Him is impossible that He be a lord and a god; therefore it is necessary to describe God with that. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage contains two rational proofs. First, Maturidi invokes necessary reason (darurat al-‘aql): everyone knows that their own actions often turn out differently from what they intend—they intend good but evil occurs, they intend pleasure but pain occurs, etc. This shows that there is another will (that of God) that determines the outcome. So human actions are dependent on God's will. Second, Maturidi argues from sovereignty: if someone creates something in another's dominion without the owner's permission, that is a sign of weakness and subjection. It is impossible to describe God with such attributes because He is the Lord and God; therefore He must be described as having absolute will over all things. Thus, it is necessary that God's will is effective in all events.

Key Terms
ضرورة العقل (darurat al-'aql)
الإرادة (al-irada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human actions are independent and self-determined.
Correction: Maturidi shows that since actions often contradict our own will, they must be subject to a higher will.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say everyone knows about their own actions?
A) They always happen as intended
B) They often happen other than what they want
C) They are completely independent of God
D) They are always good

Key Takeaway
The gap between human intention and outcome reveals that God's will directs all events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent disappointment—a result that was not what you wanted. In that moment, did you feel anger or acceptance? This lesson says your action 'comes out other than what you want' because another will is at work. Will you now thank that will for what it brought, even if you don't understand it?
?
You often plan your day, assuming you control the outcomes. But this passage says necessary reason shows your actions 'come out other than what you want' in terms of good and bad, pleasure and pain. If you truly believed that, would you be as anxious about tomorrow? Who are you trusting—your will or His?
?
This lesson strips away the illusion that you are the sole author of your life's results. When you read the words 'ثبت أن لغيرهم... إرادة' (it is established that for other than them there is a will), does that feel like a loss or a liberation? Will you live today as if that will is guiding every moment—even the painful ones?

Proof from Sovereignty
Maturidi argues from the concept of sovereignty that God must have absolute will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's sovereignty necessitates His absolute will, as creating anything in another's domain without permission is a sign of weakness.

Core Idea
God's dominion proves His will is absolute.

Maturidi offers a second rational proof based on the concept of sovereignty. If someone were to create something in another's kingdom without their will, it would indicate weakness in the creator. Since God is the sovereign Lord, such weakness cannot apply to Him. Let's explore this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Notice how Maturidi uses a hypothetical scenario to draw a necessary conclusion about God.
2
Understand the scenario
Imagine someone acting in another's dominion without permission. That indicates weakness and coercion. Reflect on why.
3
Apply to God
Since God is the ultimate sovereign, He cannot be described with weakness. Therefore, His will rules over all things.
The phrase 'لزم وصف الله بذلك' means it is necessary to ascribe to God what He deserves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will does not extend to all events, some occur independently.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if any event occurred without God's will, it would imply a deficiency in His sovereignty.

Checkpoint

A) Creating something in one's own kingdom
B) Initiating something in another's dominion without their will
C) Having no power
D) Being unable to create

Key Takeaway
God's sovereignty implies that nothing can happen in His creation without His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you believe God is sovereign over all, but are there areas in your life—your career, your relationships, your habits—where you act as if your will is independent of His? What would it look like to genuinely acknowledge His absolute will in that area today?
?
When something 'goes wrong' in your life, do you ever feel that God has lost control, or that events happened against His will? How does Maturidi’s argument challenge that feeling, and what would change if you truly believed His will covers every event?
?
Consider a moment when you sinned. Did you think that act was outside God's will? If the proof shows that nothing occurs without His will, then even sin occurs by His permission. How does that affect your understanding of your own responsibility and your need for His mercy?

First Proof: The Necessity of Reason
Maturidi argues from necessity of reason that God's will is effective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof from the necessity of reason that establishes that a will other than one's own determines the outcome of human actions.

Core Idea
Our actions often diverge from our will, proving another will is at work.

Maturidi begins with a basic fact of human experience: we frequently fail to achieve what we want in terms of good and evil, pleasure and pain. This observable discrepancy points to the existence of another will that overrides our own—the will of God. In this passage, he presents this argument concisely and invites us to reflect on its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the key observation Maturidi makes about human actions.
The passage uses the term 'ضرورة العقل' (necessity of reason).
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the argument: (1) everyone knows that their actions often come out contrary to their desire regarding good/bad, pleasure/pain, love/anger; (2) this shows there is a will other than one's own that determines the actual outcome; (3) that will belongs to God.
Notice that Maturidi does not prove God's existence here but assumes it as given.
3
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider that this proof does not deny human free will; it only shows the ultimate outcome is determined by God's will. Human will is real but limited in effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العبرة بما توجبه ضرورة العقل توجب ذلك، إذ يعلم كل أحد أن فعله يخرج على غير الذي يريده من الحسن والقبح، واللذة والألم والمحبة والسخط. ثبت أن لغيرهم في خروج فعلهم على ما خرج إرادة، على تلك الإرادة يخرج. والله الموق.وأيضاً إن إحداث شيء في سلطان آخر وفي مملكته من حيث لا يشاء ولا يريده آية الضعف والقهر، ومن ذلك وصفه محال أن يكون رباً وإلهاً؛ لذلك لزم وصف الله بذلك. والله الموفق.
Then the lesson is from what the necessity of reason necessitates, for every person knows that his action emerges in a way other than what he wants in terms of good and evil, pleasure and pain, love and anger. It is established that for other than them in the emergence of their actions in the way they emerge there is a will, and according to that will it emerges. And God is the Granter of success. And also, to originate something in another's authority and in his kingdom without his consent and will is a sign of weakness and subjugation, and from that, describing Him as such is impossible for Him to be a Lord and God; therefore describing God with that is necessary. And God is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct proofs. The first proof begins by appealing to the necessity of reason (ḍarūrat al-ʿaql), which every person experiences: one's own actions frequently deviate from one's own desires regarding good/bad, pleasure/pain, love/anger. This shows that there is another will (irāda) besides one's own that determines the outcomes. That will is God's. The second proof argues that if someone were to initiate an action in another person's domain without the latter's permission, that would indicate weakness or coercion. Since God is perfect and almighty, He cannot be described as such; therefore God must be the one whose will is effective in all things, including the actions of others.

Key Terms

إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies human free will entirely.
Correction: The argument only shows that the ultimate outcome is determined by God's will; human will is still real but its effects are subject to God's overriding will.

Checkpoint
What does the first proof in this passage establish based on the necessity of reason?
A) That human actions are entirely beyond God's control.
B) That there is a will other than one's own that determines the actual outcome of actions.
C) That human beings have no will at all.
D) That moral values are subjective.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of reason shows that a will beyond our own directs the final outcome of all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific moment this week when your actions did not lead to what you wanted. Did you pause to consider that another Will was at work? How can you train yourself to see God's hand in those disappointments? This week, when a plan fails, verbally acknowledge, 'This is from God.'
?
The passage mentions love and anger as examples of feelings that don't match outcomes. Recall a time you felt intense anger or love but the situation turned out differently. Did that experience shake your sense of control? Will you let it remind you that only God's will prevails? Commit to saying 'Alhamdulillah' when results differ from your feelings.
?
If God's will determines every outcome, what does that mean for your anxiety about the future? Are you still holding onto the illusion that you control your life? What would it look like for you to truly surrender one area of your life to God's will today? Name that area and a concrete action to trust Him.

Second Proof: The Argument from Dominion
Maturidi argues from dominion that God's will is effective in all realms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof from dominion and authority, which argues that God's will must be effective because initiating an action in another's domain without permission is a sign of weakness, incompatible with divinity.

Core Idea
Acting in another's domain without consent indicates weakness—impossible for God.

Maturidi's second argument shifts from personal experience to a logical principle about authority and dominion. He posits that if someone acted in another person's territory without their consent, that would show weakness or coercion. Since God is perfect and sovereign, He cannot be described by such weakness; therefore His will must always be effective, even in the actions of others. This passage presents that reasoning succinctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the hypothetical scenario Maturidi presents.
The key term is 'سلطان' (authority/dominion).
2
Understand the logical flow
The argument: (1) producing an effect in another's realm without their will indicates weakness or subjugation; (2) such a description is impossible for God because it contradicts His lordship; (3) therefore, God must be described as having effective will in all domains.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if God's will were ineffective, He would be weak, which is false.
3
Connect to the broader theology
Reflect on how this proof supports the doctrine that everything that happens occurs by God's will, without negating human responsibility.
Maturidi’s emphasis is on God's authority, not on determinism versus free will.

Source Text

The second proof argues that if someone were to initiate an action in another person's domain without the latter's permission, that would indicate weakness or coercion. Since God is perfect and almighty, He cannot be described as such; therefore God must be the one whose will is effective in all things, including the actions of others.

Misconception: This proof implies that human actions are forced by God and humans have no role.
Correction: The proof focuses on God's authority and perfection, not on the mechanics of human agency. It does not negate that humans have will; it asserts that nothing escapes God's will.

B) Because ineffective will would be a sign of weakness, incompatible with being Lord and God.

D) Because the Qur'an states it explicitly.

Key Takeaway
God's will must be effective in all events because any limitation would contradict His perfection and sovereignty.

God's Will is Not Wishful Thinking: Choice, Compulsion, and Divine Will
Maturidi distinguishes God's will from human tamanni and argues that God's will cannot be ineffective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's will cannot be ineffective or reduced to human wishful thinking, and how Maturidi distinguishes between divine will and human tamanni.

Core Idea
God's will determines all actions; otherwise it would be mere wishing.

Maturidi sharpens the distinction between God's will and human wishful thinking. He argues that if God's will were ineffective — if things could occur against His will — then His will would resemble human tamanni, which is a weak, fruitless wishing. But God's will is necessarily effective and all-encompassing. This passage establishes the logical necessity that God wills every existing thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp its flow. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi uses a conditional argument.
There are three key concepts: ikhtiyār, iḍṭirār, and tamannī.
2
Identify the condition for will
Find the sentence that states: 'the condition for one whose action is by choice is will.' Understand that will is necessary for voluntary action.
Contrast with compelled action which lacks will.
3
Apply the condition to God
Maturidi applies this condition to God: if God were not willing for the servant's action, then God would be compelled in relation to that action. But that is impossible.
This shows God's will must be involved in all actions.
4
Understand the rejection of ineffective will
The passage concludes that if something existed that God did not will, then God's will would be like wishful thinking (tamannī). Since that's impossible, God wills all things.
This refutes the view that God's will can be ineffective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن شرط كلَّ من فعله اختيار هو الإرادة، وكلُّ من فعله الاضطرار فإنه غير مريد لذلك. فلو كان الله لفعل العبد غير مريد ليكون على ما كان يكون مضطرا؛ ولذلك لا يجوز أن يكون لأحد في فعل غيره إرادة، لما لا يحتمل خروجه على ما يريد، وسموا ذلك تمنيا؛ فعلى ذلك لو توهم كون شيء لم يرده الله كانت إرادته تخرج مخرج التمني.
And also, the condition for everyone whose action is by choice is will, and everyone whose action is by compulsion is not willing that. So if God were, regarding the action of the servant, not willing, then He would be, according to how things are, compelled. And therefore it is not permissible for anyone to have will in the action of another, because it cannot be that its outcome is according to his will, and they call that wishful thinking (tamannī). According to that, if one supposed the existence of something that God did not will, then His will would exit as wishful thinking.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between will (irāda) and choice (ikhtiyār) versus compulsion (iḍṭirār). The condition for an act of choice is that the agent has will. If an act is compelled, the agent does not will it. Applying this to God: if God's will were not involved in the servant's action, then God would be compelled in relation to that action, which is impossible. Moreover, it is impossible for anyone to have a truly effective will in another's action if the outcome is not guaranteed by that will; such a will is merely wishful thinking (tamannī). So if God's will did not determine the occurrence of things, His will would be reduced to futile wishing, which is impossible.

Key Terms
الاختيار
الإرادة
الاضطرار
التمني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will is like human wishful thinking and can sometimes be ineffective.
Correction: The passage argues that God's will is always effective; if it were otherwise, His will would be imperfect wishful thinking, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the condition for an action to be considered one of choice?
A) It must be compelled.
B) It must be based on will.
C) It must be based on wishful thinking.
D) It must be without any will.

Key Takeaway
God's will is necessarily effective; any supposed reality beyond God's will reduces His will to wishful thinking.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which area of your life do you live as if your actions are outside of God's will? Examine your fears and plans—where have you assumed God's will is merely a wish? What will you change today to align your will with His?
?
The passage says God's will cannot be wishful thinking. Does your dua reflect certainty that God's will is effective, or do you sometimes pray as if you're just hoping without conviction? How will you transform your supplication to be built on this truth?
?
When you sin, do you think you have escaped God's will? This lesson says no. How does that truth humble you and drive you to repentance rather than despair? What specific step will you take this week to acknowledge His sovereignty over your failures?

The Impossibility of Willing the Impossible
Maturidi uses the example of asking guidance for Iblis to show we cannot will the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Imām Māturīdī, it is impossible to genuinely will something that one knows will never occur, and how this principle applies to both humans and Allāh.

Core Idea
You cannot sincerely will what you know will not happen—this is true for you and for Allāh.

This passage from Imām Māturīdī’s Kitāb al-Tawḥīd tackles a subtle point about the relationship between knowledge and will. He argues that if you know with certainty that something will not occur, it is impossible (muḥāl) for you to genuinely will it. He then extends this principle from human beings to Allāh, showing that Allāh’s will is perfectly aligned with His knowledge. The example of asking guidance for Iblīs illustrates the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it, then read the literal translation. Note the flow of the argument.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the hypothetical scenario
The passage begins with a hypothetical: if a prophet's prophethood were confirmed by a human statement, willing that statement would be a sin. This is a setup to highlight that God's knowledge prevents willing the impossible.
This is not the main point; it's an analogy.
3
Understand the principle about the servant
The passage then says that a servant cannot sincerely ask God to guide someone (like Iblīs) if he knows that person will not be guided. Because his knowledge makes it impossible for him to genuinely will that guidance.
This is the key principle: knowledge of impossibility precludes sincere willing.
4
Apply the principle to God
The passage concludes that if it is impossible for us to will what we know will not happen, then it is even more impossible for God, because His knowledge is perfect. So God's will only concerns what He knows will occur.
This refines our understanding of God's will (irādah) in relation to His knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنه لو جعل لنا أن نبوة نبي بقول بشر، يكون ذلك معصية لنا أن نريده، من حيث يكون آية، وإن لم يكن له أن يعصى. فمثله إذ علمنا الله أنه أخبر عن ذلك وأنه علم أن لا يكون، كان له أن لا يريد في الحكمة. على أنه لا يختلف في أن ليس للعبد أن يسأل الله هداية من يعلم أنه لا يهتدى نحو إبليس، لم يكن له أن يقول: اللهم اهده، لما يعلم أنه لا يكون، ثم محال أن يكون علينا إرادة ذلك؛ وإذ لم يكن علينا إرادة ما نعلم أنه لا يكون لم يجز أن يقال ذلك على الله؛ إذ كونها جائزا علينا إنما يكون إذا جهلنا بحاله. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, if it were made for us that the prophethood of a prophet by the statement of a human, that would be a sin for us to will it, because it would be a sign, and he cannot disobey. Similarly, when God taught us that He informed about that and that He knew it will not be, He has the right not to will in wisdom. Indeed, it is not disputed that the servant may not ask God for guidance for one whom he knows will not be guided, like Iblis; he may not say: O God, guide him, because he knows it will not be. Then it is impossible for us to will that; and since it is not for us to will what we know will not be, it is not permissible to say that about God; because its being permissible for us is only when we are ignorant of his state. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage presents a sophisticated argument about the relationship between knowledge and will. First, it sets up a hypothetical: if a prophet's prophethood were confirmed by a mere human statement (without miracle), then willing that statement would be a sin because it would be a sign. But that is not the case. The key point: God has informed us that He knows certain things will not happen (e.g., Iblis's guidance). Since God knows it will not happen, it is not in wisdom to will it. The example of asking guidance for Iblis illustrates that we cannot sincerely ask God to guide someone we know will not be guided. If we know it will not happen, willing it is impossible (muḥāl). Since this impossibility applies to us when we know, it applies even more to God, who has perfect knowledge. Therefore God's will is perfectly aligned with what He knows will occur, and He does not will things contrary to His knowledge.

Key Terms
الهداية
العلم
المستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about forbidding us from asking for the impossible (like Iblis's guidance).
Correction: No, the argument uses the fact that we cannot sincerely will what we know will not happen to prove that God, who has perfect knowledge, cannot will the impossible. It is not a prohibition on asking; it is a logical point about the link between knowledge and will.

Checkpoint
Why, according to this passage, cannot a servant sincerely ask God to guide Iblis?
A) Because Iblis is a jinn, not a human.
B) Because the servant knows that Iblis will not be guided.
C) Because asking for guidance is always sinful.
D) Because God does not hear such prayers.

Key Takeaway
God's will is perfectly aligned with His knowledge; He never wills what He knows will not occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever asked Allah for something while in your heart you knew it would not happen? What does that reveal about the sincerity of your will? Will you commit today to only asking Allah for things you truly believe He can and will do, trusting His wisdom?
?
This lesson proves that we cannot sincerely will what we know will not be. Is there a dua you make habitually that you have stopped believing will be answered? How can you bring yourself to either renew your hope or stop asking in a way that disrespects both your own knowledge and Allah’s knowledge?
?
If you know that Allah knows everything, why do you sometimes ask Him for things that contradict His decree? How does this lesson call you to be more honest in your supplication, aligning your request with your belief in His perfect knowledge?
